TORRES'
UNKNOWN
DIS-
COVERIES
If any man shall marvell that, in the discoverynges of
the World, there be little extent of Englishmen’s
travayles, the which have been made for the most part to
places first discovered by others : hee is to understand,
that when those discoveries come to more perfection, and
become more profitable to the adventurers, will then be
more fit for the saide Englishman who will come along
and occupy the same for the honour of his counthrey.”
John Rasteli,.
THE true story of early Australasian Mari-
time Discovery is yet untold. What we
know of it consists of shreds of narrative—
rescued from oblivion and secrecy—con-
fusedly interwoven by modern historians, and
further distorted by clumsy conjecture: so that
he who would fain unravel the mystery which
enshrouds early Australian discovery, and track
the courses of the bold mariners whose ships first
ploughed the waters around this continent, meets
with many difficulties.
The recovery of ancient MSS. and charts, how-
ever, is gradually throwing light on the subject.
Now and again these documents turn up, and we
are enabled to realise more fully the extent of the
work achieved, and give honour to whom honour is
due by fixing the claim of priority of discovery.
Thus, to-day, Torres’ name comes to the front
owing to the recovery of some old Spanish charts.*
With the help of these charts and other docu-
mente hitherto ignoredf I shall endeavour to lift a
portion of the veil which hangs over the most
skilfully conducted and pluckiest navigation ever
undertaken by Spanish navigators in the unknown
waters of the South Pacific Ocean. I refer to
Torres’ voyage along the southern shores of New
Guinea, the full narrative of which, if extant, has
not yet been found.
The events that led up to the cruise in question
were as follows:—The Spaniards, for many years,
had been endeavouring to reach the east coast of
Australia from South America. In pursuance of
this scheme of discovery, an expedition was sent
out in the year 1605. It was composed of three
ships, and an experienced navigator named De
Quiros —who had sailed on a similar voyage ten
years before—was appointed commander. De
Quiros’ lieutenant was one Luis Yaes de Torres,
who is the man we are concerned with in this
gtper. Among the other officers we must note
on Diego de Prado, because he was the carto-
grapher of the expedition and the author of the
recovered charts already alluded to.
On the Ist of May, 1606, the little fleet sighted
the New Hebrides, and De Quiros thought that
he had accomplished his end and discovered the
“ Great South Land.” He was not allowed time,
however, to verify his conjectures, for a mutiny
broke out on board his ship, and he was taken
back to America. Torres, now in command of the
other two ships, ascertained that De Quiros’ Aus-
tralian continent was but an island. Then, having
consulted with his officers, he decided to carry
out the sailing instructions' which, it appeared,
De Quiros had sadly neglected.
In doing so Torres came remarkably close to
Australia, reaching 21° S. latitude in longitudes
between New Caledonia and the coast of Queens-
land. He met with no signs of land in that
direction and steered N.W. to S. latitude,
where, he says: “We fell in with the beginning
of New Guinea.”
* The charts in question were pillaged from the
Spanish archives during the wars of Napoleon I, and
taken to Paris. There, buried away and uncatalogued,
the were .found some years ago by a friend of mine, who
caused them to be returned to their original owners and
acquainted me with their existence, thus enabling me to
get copies of them shortly after their appearance in 1883
in Zarazoga’s work on De Quiros’ voyage, The Sydney
F.P. Library now possesses a copy of Zarazoga’s work.
f Principally Pierre Mortier’s Atlas, published at
Amsterdam in 1700.
We come now to Torres’ unknown discoveries as
revealed by the old Spanish charts. There lies at
the eastern extremity of New. Guinea a group of
beautiful islands supposed to have been first
sighted in the year 1873 by the leader of an
English expedition, bent on discovery. Captain
John Moresby, of H.M.S. Basilisk, the leader in
question, in the account of his discoveries in New
Guinea, published in 1876, says: “I trust that
the work done by H.M.S. Basilisk, in waters
hitherto untracted, on shores hitherto v/ntrodden,
and amongst races hitherto unknown by Europea/ns,
will be held to call for some account.”
Now, a glance at maps Nos. 1 and 2 will show
how Moresby’s work had been forestalled by
Torres. In those maps I have given the outlines
of portions of Moresby’s surveys (map No. 2), to-
gether with the corresponding features of the
first of the three long-lost Spanish charts which
belong to our subject (see map No. 1). Those
features and place-names will reveal some of the
most important of Torres’ discoveries at the south-
east end of New Guinea, where the Spanish
navigator made his first stay in order to refresh
the crews of his two little ships.
From a legend on the chart we learn that during
five days and nights the Spaniards stood in sight
of those tantalising, verdant shores, unable to
effect a landing, threading their way through
perilous reefs and over dangerous shoals. Then,
at last, they rounded, no doubt, the oape which
Torres called Gabo de tres Hermanns, or Cape of
the Three Sisters, passed the next point marked
A on the map, and came to anchor in a little bay
which was called Puerto de San Francisco. It is
situated near the south-east entrance to Rocky
Pass, between Basilisk and Hay ter Islands, and
formed, in all probability, during their sojourn in
these parts, the centre of their various excursions
to the islands and bays around. Its name gives
us the date, 14th J uly (1606), of Torres’ landing,
for it was customary in those days to name dis-
coveries after the saints of the calendar; but the
feast of St. Bonaventure occurs also on July 14,
so that name was likewise made use of, and given
to the whole territory discovered.
Contrary to Torres’, Moresby’s approach, in the
year 1873, was from the N.E., where the mainland
of New Guinea was supposed to extend beyond
Hayter, Basilisk, and Moresby Islands. The
English captain had already cut off Moresby
Island, left his good ship Basilisk at anchor in the
strait thus discovered (Fortescue Strait), and—
the numerous reefs rendering further navigation
impossible for his ship—taken to his boats, the
galley and cutter (“Discoveries in New Guinea,”
Moresby, page 197 et sequitur). They rounded
the northern shores of what Moresby thought
might prove to be the beginning of New Guinea,
when suddenly a bay seemed to open towards the
South. Moresby entered it, and, by the merest
chance, hit upon the identical narrow passage
which Torres, 267 years previously, had discovered
from the south side and named Bocca de la Battala,
Mouth of the Battle, —having, no doubt, had an
encounter there with the natives. Moresby called
that mouth Rocky Pass, and grew enthusiastic
at the discovery, and at having “separated
another island from New Guinea.” He was
anxious to find if Rocky Pass would afford a
passage for the ship, and spent the remainder of
the day in examining it; but a rocky ledge,
which ran aoross, barred it to the ships, and made
it dangerous even for boats at the strength of the
tide.
Moresby’s experiences help to show the diffi-
culties that the Spaniards had to deal with, and
also that Torres must have been compelled to
leave his two ships at anchor somewhere to the
south of the Baya de San Millan —St. Francisco
Bay, for instance—and use the only rowing-boat
he had for his excursions. In this he explored the
bay formed by the horse-shoe shape of Basilisk
Island, named it the Baya de San Millan (modern
Jenkins Bay), and penetrated to the largest bay
of all the lands he had discovered in this region—
that is, Milne Bay. He says: “We went a long
way out from Gabo Fresco (modern Challis Head
of Moresby’s chart) which is as far as we could go
towards the east in a boat.”
Other nautical remarks which I translate from
the old Spanish text of the chart are: “Towards
the east (N.E.) we did not see the end of the land,
but we could judge from the various small islands
that the channels were wide. We did not notice
any channels towards the west, only land and con-
tinuous lofty ridges, Tierra alta y gerrada (The
Mount Owen Stanley Range). We steered in that
direction, but had to give up further progress
after a while owing to the inadequacy of our
boat.” These and other notes on the Spanish
chart correspond exactly with Moresby’s descrip-
tion of Milne Bay ; and the dimensions given to
that bay by De Prado, the cartographer (40 leagues
in circumference), may be considered as a very
correct estimate.
On the 18th of July Torres and his party, having
concluded their running survey of Basilisk Island,
landed and took possession in the name of the
King of Spain, naming, as I have said, the whole
territory the Tierra\ de San Buenaventura.
I mentioned in the beginning of this paper,
“ other documents hitherto ignored.” It is now
opportune for me to make use of some of those
documents. One of them, a much-distorted copy
of a general map, made by Torres’ cartographer,
is invaluable at this juncture. A careful examina-
tion of its features shows that Torres’ Tierra de San
Buenaventura (Basilisk Island) is one of several
islands off the south-eastern extremity of New
Guinea; and, by coupling this fact with what
Torres says of his inability to navigate the bay
(Milne Bay of Moresby’s chart) and proceed east
of Cabo Fresco (Moresby’s Challis Head), al-
though he noticed wide channels in that
direction, we may infer that the reefs and
coral patches (not contrary winds as generally
believed) compelled him to seek the south-west
passage to Manila. This becomes still more
evident when we consider that Moresby also was
unable to take his ship through to the northern
shores.
From these regions Torres Sailed to the Great
Bay of St. Lawrence (Orangerie Bay), discovered
August 10th, 1606. Here another extensive survey
was made and a township laid out. Then
the littlfc squadron went right up into the
Gulf of Papua and down again to 11° S. latitude.
Not, therefore, through Torres Strait, so called, did
Torres pass, but through Captain Cook’s Endea-
vour Strait. Sailing along the islands to the
north of Australia, between Cape York and
Prince of Wales Island, Torres regained the
shores of New Guinea, and put in at the Bay of
St. Peter of Arlanza (modern Triton Bay). There
he took possession on the 18th of October, 1606,
and, after a lengthy stay, sailed away to the
Philippine Islands.
Included in the title-piece of this papw will be
noticed a general outline of Torres’ run along the
southern shores of New Guinea. His three prin-
cipal ports of call are marked by Maltese crosses;
a continuous line shows the track he followed,
whereas a dotted line indicates what is erron-
eously believed to be the course he followed. In
passing through Endeavour Strait it will be
noticed that he came much closer to Australia
than is generally believed.
GEORGE COLLINGRIDGE.
We have n’t heard much of Frank Myers lately,
in the local literary way; but in his heyday the
man had vim. Possibly no other ever silenced old
Jack Robertson. Myers pinned Jack once to the
wall of the Athenaeum club, when that also was
in its heyday, and treated him to a scathing de-
nunciation of the Free Selection - Act—a real
Demosthenian invective which described the
greed, the roguery, the perjury the Act had be-
gotten, and wound up with a terrific) picture of the
permanent degradation of society due to “You,
the source and origin of evil; you, the archi-
tect of the edifice of chicanery; you, the
Father of Free Selection—the Father of Woe
Everlasting.” The rhetoric swept old Jack off
his mental legs; he listened, speechless, to the
end; and when he was let go shuffled to the
bar before he loosed the single comment he was
ever known to make. “Brandy !by God ! ”
On another occasion Myers gave Dailey occasion
for one of his happiest speeches. The scene was
again the Athenaeum club; the time, when the
club gave a dinner to Archibald Forbes. Myers
was more than eloquent; he was violent.
The Athenaeum, he said, was the only club in
Sydney worth the name. The others—he shud-
dered while denouncing them. He referred to
the rumours of high play at a certain club—and
Jack Robertson, the president of the Reform
Club, which was the one meant, sat opposite,
glowering at him. The situation was strained;
and everybody felt uncomfortable, expecting old
Jack to reply and spoil the evening’s harmony—
he was obviously waiting his chance. But Dailey
interposed before Jack could rise—Dailey the re-
sourceful and alert. The table breathed: surely he
was going to smooth things over. Then there was
one ghastly moment. ‘ ‘ Everything, ” commenced
Dailey, “ everything that has been said is true,
and I grieve to say it. But Mr. Myers has not
told one-half—no, not one-tithe of the truth.”
Then Dailey out-Heroded Herod. He continued
Mgers’ attack to the pitch of ludicrous exagge-
ration. He warned Forbes that if, relying on his
Jouth and innocence, he went to the Reform Club,
e would be swept into the awful whirlpool of green
cloth. Nothing could save him, once caught in
the vortex. He might appeal to his virtue—
in vain ! He might rely on his experience— a
reed, a broken reed ! His good angel would fly
shrieking; his bad angel would preen triumphant
wings. “Round and round you will go, Mr.
Forbes, down and down and down, until the
great war-correspondent is lost! lost! LOST!”
And laughter and the waiters saved the situation.

SONG.
Seek not the tree of silkiest bark
And balmiest bud,
To carve her name while yet ’tis dark
Upon the wood :
The world is full of noble tasks
And wreaths hard won :
Each work demands strong hearts, strong hands,
Till day is done.
Sing not that violet-veined skin,
That cheek’s pale roses,
The lily of that form within
Her soul reposes !
Forth to the fight, true man 1 true knight I
The clash of arms
Shall more prevail than whisper’d tale
To win her charms.
The warrior for the True, the Right,
Fights in Love’s name ;
The love that lures thee from that fight
Lures thee to shame :
That love which lifts the heart, yet leaves
The spirit free,—
That love, or none, is fit for one
Man-shaped like thee.
J*
Lond. Times proprietary’s sale of Literature to
The Academy may be taken as a proof that in
England a first-rate literary weekly cannot be
made to pay. It cannot subsist on circulation;
it must cater to the publishers for advertisements;
whence arises a dilemma unpleasant for either the
editor’s self-respect or the proprietor’s purse.
The Athencev/m is a conservative exception of long
standing, at whose thunder the other old fogeys
still tremble, though during the last few years
lightning has been rarer. H. D. Traill’s idea of
Literature was an Athencev/m slightly more up-to-
date ; and since his death the paper has been at-
tempting the interesting compromise between
dignified tradition and the New Journalism.
Apparently it lost old readers without gaining new
ones; and the transfer to The Academy proprietary
ends the agony. The Academy has been the
brightest _of the Lond. literary weeklies since
Lewis Hind took charge five years ago; and the
new purchase should give scope for still better
things.
“A.J.G.”: I once had simple faith in Mary
Spencer Warren, the lady whose forte it is to
write of kings and queens and such in the English
magazines. (I had not met those kings and
queens. ) But M.S.W. has recently written of our
late visitor, the Cipher o’ York, in Pearson’s ; and
my faith in her has vanished. Says she (italics
mine): —
... All who have come in contact with the Duke of
Cornwall and York will have realised at once that he is
a 'most thorough Englishman in his disposition and ways,
and an Englishman of the best type, too. Frank, generous,
vigorous, methodical, fond of a good joke, and thoroughly
kind-hearted. He is a man who exerts a powerful in-
fluence over those who are in any way associated with
him ; and in his seafaring days especially he helped many
a weak-minded one by his honest fearlessness and rigid
uprightness of character. . . . [He] has won for himself
quite as much popularity amongst the British people as
ever he did amongst the officers and men of the naval
service. The more he is seen in their midst the more he
is liked. When he spealcs it does one good to hear his
clear and vigorous enunciation, and to note the manly,
earnest way in which he gives expression to his thoughts
—utterances which bespeak a cultivated mind and a wide
knowledge of affairs of the world. . . .
You see, I have both heard and seen the Duke!
Fancy talking in that way about the enervated
and characterless little being whom we saw
brushed, dressed, and carted here, there, and
everywhere like a showman’s dummy!
Reported from London that both Henry Lawson
and Louise Mack are making preparations for
return to Australia. The fact is, as Patchett
Martin affirmed and Ambrose Pratt re-affirms, •
the Australian literary man or woman can very
well, nowadays make sis London profit without
leaving Australia. London does not particularly
want Australian work, unless of the adventurous
Becke class, which is readable whatever its field.
The fidelity to Australian life and character, for
example, which is Lawson’s peculiar merit, gives
him a foreign and uninviting air in England. The
English don’t comprehend such work unless it is
universalised, so to speak—made plain to limited
understandings by a levelling of local features and
an increase of general dramatic effect. And, as
Pratt found out,. for the inexperienced author to
deal in person with the average London publisher
is merely to increase sis risk of being robbed. The
literary agent—and there are possibly half-a-dozen
good agents in London—is better able to find a
market and better able to get the highest market
price.

A Letter from Archdeacon Lingard.
Christchurch, N.Z.,
February 25th, ’Ol.
Dear Sirs, —
I have much pleasure in testifying to the value
of Warner’s Safe Cure.
Nearly four years ago, after having tried several
remedies for Diabetes without success, my medical
man consented to my trying Warner’s Safe Cure.
At that time there was no prospect of my living
above a few months. At first I took six table-
spoonfuls a day. I began to improve in health
immediately, and was soon able to undertake my
regular duties. During the last four years I have
never been without the Safe Cure in the house, at
times reducing the doses to one per diem, and occa-
sionally going without altogether for a month or so.
At the present time, though not altogether
cured (at my age I can hardly expect it), I am able
to undertake the arduous duties of a large parish
single-handed, and without much fatigue.
I have no hesitation in saying that my present
fairly good state of health is due to Warner’s Safe
Cure, and I shall ever feel grateful that it came my
way.
Yours, etc.,
EDW. ATH. LINGrAR,D,
Archdeacon of Akaroa.
Messrs. H. H. Warner & Co., Limited.

Indoors or Out?
Who lives longest, those who work indoors or
those exposed to the weather ? Who is healthiest,
the indoor or the outdoor man T the bootmaker or
the postman, the compositor or the ’bus-driver.
’Tis hard to say, more especially as_we have it on
the authority of an eminent physician that even
horses sometimes suffer from indigestion. Pro-
bably the trade which partakes partly of both is
healthiest for the worker. Certainly indigestion
is not specially the property of workers in large
cities, for here is Mr. Thomas Deere, who writes
from Ferry Lane, Newtown, Maryborough, Queens-
land, under date March 13,1901, saying : “ About
16 years ago I was working on the roads and
was badly attaoked by indigestion. Wind col-
lected round my heart, sometimes at night, un-
til I thought that it would cease to beat, and I
used to jump out of bed thinking my time had
come. After suffering like this for a number of
years I happened to read a pamphlet which
induced me to try what Mother Seigel’s Syrup
would do for me. You will not be surprised
that I was soon restored to good health. But
I was very much surprised indeed myself, for
before using that medicine two doctors in Roek-
hamption had certified that I was suffering from
disease of the heart. Since my cure I have been
in excellent health, except occasionally when
some food may disagree with me, in which case
a dose of Seigel’s Syrup never fails to set me
right again. I have lived in Maryborough for 10
years and have recommended Mother Seigel s
Syrup to hundreds of people, explaining to them
my own case.”
Mr. Deere’s case is in no respect uncommon.
Many men who were long ago told they had but
a short time to live are happily still with us. A
fentleman is now residing at St. Albans, _ in
Ingland, aged 69, not very robust, but m fairly
good health for a man at his period of life,
who in May, 1855, went to a physician of the
highest rank in London, explained his ailments,
paid a fee of five guineas, and in return was
advised to arrange his affairs, and it was unlikely
that he would live until the end of the year !
Verily, the Irishman who said that no man had a
right to consider himself dead until he had been
buried a month, was an accurate observer.
The St. Albans gentleman in 1855, living within
20 miles of the greatest city in the world, paid
five guineas for a prophecy which for absurdity
has rarely been surpassed by a gypsy fortune-
teller. Forty-five years later, at the other side
of the world, in a place almost terra incognita
in 1855, Mr. Richard Deere is cured of his malady
for a few shillings. It is a case of other times
other manners, later age more knowledge.
Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
One Austral Night.
The sunflowers facing to the West,
Brave pages of the court, have bowed
Their King, in all his splendor drest,
Through the gold arras of the cloud;
Then lovingly, more lowly yet,
Make deep obeisance to the arc
Where the dear Queen of Night has set
Her jewelled hands across the dark.
A South wind gathers from the range
The blended scent of herb and pine
To lay upon her lips, and change
Her dewy breath to bridal wine.
A thousand crickets shake to song
The feathery oak-boughs in the grove;
To Love, those thrilling notes belong,
And you and I belong to Love !
The bull-frogs in the moonlight call,
The curlews in the night confide,
And, hand in hand, we know them all,
As lovesick lovers undenied !
Low down the plain the team-bells chime,
A lone note here—a jingle there !
Ah, Love ! My Love ! How sweet the time !
Oh, Love ! for love the place how fair!
Your eyes make challenge unto mine,
And bold I snatch the gauntlet up;
The merry moonbeams mix the wine,
The laughing South wind lifts the cup!
The world is all too far to blame
White Honour bartered for Despair,
And if the Shadows hide for shame,
The sunflowers know the price is fair !
The soft snow lies upon the wold,
The moaning fir-boughs sway and drip,
The moon whose hand we loved to hold
Turns down a careless finger-tip.
Remember ! —Love beyond the Sea !
There is a night that none can take —
Ah ! keep that one witch-hour for me
And memory—for memory’s sake !
U.K.
WILL OGILVIE.
Song.
He wanders all his shore down,
The reedy River slow;
And we have laid the oar down
To sing that she may know:
“ There’s Summer still to hold the hill
Before the blossoms go 1
“ 0 daybright of the Day !
’Tis only Heart can tell with art
What Tongue finds hard to say.
And we are here and waiting
For twilight time to die;
And we are here and hating
The rays of sunset sky
That play upon the water gray
That swings your garden by.
“ Ah come, whate’er befalling ;
The Summer’s joyance pledge l
Ah, trace the old steps falling
To reedy river-edge !
’Tis Night and I are calling,
Nor take for answer, No :
We shall not be denied with you,
For we have come to bide with you,
To dream the summer-tide with you
Before the blossoms go ! ”

MERIDEN.
To My Country.
A wind-blown, shimmering, shifting, awful waste,
Fringed by a broken edge of green and grey—
A ghastly field for devilish winds at play ;
A painful tale of desperate men that faced
The loathly hell, of hard-won pathways traced
In dull white bones, of a race whose long decay
Gives warning to the pallid crowd to-day
To seek a land by greener beauty graced :
Australia ! thou whose dust, made flesh, has given
Power to my soul to warn thy people now,
To me these horrors are a thing of naught.
O, nurse of serfdom, how shalt thou be shriven
Of threadbare knees and dust-enshrouded brow,
Mother of slaves who dare not speak their
thought ?
N.S.W.
B.
The Old Gods.
O, ye gods, if you could tell us
What ye are—if banned or blessed—
Ye that reigned of old in Hellas 1
Ye that ruled the radiant West!
Old-bom gods ! The Past still flashes
In the eyes of Greece and Rome,
Ye are not mere dust and ashes
Umed for all the years to come.
Ye that ruled in heavenly places,
And the faith of mortals won !
Gods created by old races
Perished from beneath the sun,
Bom of faith and with it blended
Ye shall still the world inspire;
Till the last breath has ascended
From the latest altar fire.
All the hopes and invocations
Breathed by lips of heroes dead,
All the genius of the nations
Who the march of Freedom led—
Though your temples broke and fell as
Dusty fanes of little worth—
These will keep ye, gods of Hellas !
Still alive upon the earth.
N.S.W.
R. CRAWFORD.

DRAIN-FAG is one of the penalties of civilisation. Excessive brain labor exhausts the
nervous system more speedily than physical duties. One affects the muscular system
only, demanding' its strength. The other saps and drains the sources and springs of that’
nervous energy which is
the foundation of the
faculty of creative
thought, and mentally ex-
hausts the worker. Men
who earn their living: by
the pen suffer from brain-
fag ; thousands die yearly
through _ mental decline.
With them, the pen is not
mightier than the sword.
To any of the class of
thinkers who concentrate
and focus their thoughts
laboriously upon one
especial study, they should
read the following testi-
mony from a professional
musician, lately starring
with one of Mr. Percy
Dix’s theatrical companies
in New Zealand. He went
through a long, trying engagement successfully.
He attributes his success to the restorative
methods of the Specialists of the Institute. Here
is his testimony :
Empire Theatre, Castlereagh-street, Sydney,
August 12th, 19C1.
The Specialists,
Electro-Medical and Surgical Institute, Sydney.
Gentlemen, —I have much pleasure in testifying
to the marvellous benefits' I have obtained from the
Electro-Medical treatment I have received from your
Institute.
In my profession, as pianist, in theatrical work, I
find, after a lengthy engagement such as I have had
in New Zealand, that the system is thereby greatly'
impaired, and for this purpose I had consulted your
staff of Specialists.
I had been treated by some of the best medical
practitioners here before visiting you, but from their
treatment I found no benefit whatever. It was
utterly useless.
I write this purely unsolicited, and just to say that
anyone suffering from prostration, general debility,
brain-fag through excessive labor, and complete
decline of the nervous system, could not do better
than pay an immediate visit to the Specialists of
your remarkable Institute.
I remain yours sincerely,
(Signed) F. H. BROWN.

Another Bad Li
CALLED
BACK TO DUTY.
The Casa of Mr. T. GLEESON.
(For the “ Suburban Times.’*)
The destiny of great issues at stake de-
pends greatly upon the ability with which
they are handled. All audiences contain a
percentage of listeners who require plain
language to produce a comprehensive ap-
preciation of the matters under debate,
their perception not being sufficiently keen
to understand the purport of a brilliant
array of high-class words and phrases.
Learned' men sometimes experience diffi-
culty in finding words to express uncom-
mon impressions, and Mr. Thomas Gleeson,
of No. 45 Turner-Btreet, Redfern, acknow-
ledged himself similarly situated when re-
cently seen by a reporter.
“ Whenever I talk about it I can hardly
find words strong enough to express my
Surprise and gratitude,” said Mr. Gleeson.
“ I was very ill for fully six months.”
“ What was your sickness like ? ”
'* T suffered from a severe attack of indi-
goobiou, which nearly took my life. The
many complaints which arose from my dis-
ordered system made life not worth living.
To begin with I had a feeling of distention
or weight in my bowels, which were very
inactive, although I d.d everything that I
thought likely to give relief, without
success. Much annoyance was caused
through wind in the stomach, which made
a rumbling sound, and caused no end of
pain. I felt restless, and discontented with
everything. Things that used to interest
and entertain me I now looked at with dis-
like. Every little trifle that did not appear
quite favourable to me I magnified into a
great trouble, making myself feel constantly
miserable. I could not help worrying my-
self with an unnecessary watchfulness for
some evil I expected to happen, whilst at
other times I was afflicted with a great
drowsy weariness, which made me unable
to move. I also had wandering pains in
various parts of the body, whilst other pains
remained lixed for months.”
“ Where were they most severe?”
'* The agony I felt through a sharp,
pricking pain between the shoulders was
quite enough in itself; but I also had a heavy,
stifling sensation in the chest. Wearisome
aches in the head never left me, and my
limbs were as tired as if I had beon walking
without a rest for a week. Impure wind,
causing a nauseous taste in my mouth, arose
from the stomach, and my tongue was
covered with a thick coating. I changed to
a deadly colour, my lips being almost white,
and dark lines appeared under my eyes. The
act of stooping caused an unnatural rush of
blood to my head, and the giddiness which I
felt on straightening myself up nearly made
me reel to the ground. I was obliged to
hold on to something to save myself from
falling. A profuse perspiration broke out
all over me whenever I got a start, which it
took little to produce, and my heart beat
rapidly with nervous excitement when my
state of lethargy was suddenly disturbed.
It was a case of farewell to my appetite
from the start of my sickness. I had not the
least inclination to eat anything, and became
so weak that I was compelled to leave my
work. I stuck to it as long as possible, but
when I found that every ounce of flesh had
left my worn-out frame I thought my case
was too serious to continue without laying
up.”
“ Could you sleep fairly well ?”
“My mind was too troubled and irritable
to allow me any sleep, and when I got up in
the mornings I was like a dead man. I had
lost my strength and vigor, and always felt
languid and fatigued. I was so uneasy in
mind and body that I could not rest any-
where long. A sudden feeling of sleepiness
used to come on during the day, and then I
laid down, only to get up in a couple of
minutes and wander about like one demented.
Everything was a trouble to me, and my
painful afflictions became more numerous
and violent as time went on. I despaired of
a recovery, and gave way to despondency.
“ I suppose you were taking medicine 1 ”
“ Yes. I tried an endless variety of
medicines and pills, but not one of them did
me any good. The only effect they had
upon me was to Inake me sick and increase
my misery. It was this bitter experience
which drove me to desperation. My sickneßS
seemed unconquerable.”
“ How were your sufferings overcome ? ”
“ By using Clements Tonic regularly till
it cleared the disease from my system. A
member of my family had reaped inestimable
benefilsfrom Clements Tonic before I was
taken ill, and yet it did not occur to mo till
now that it was the only medicine I could
expect to cure me. I made a start with
Clements Tonic, and it picked me up won-
derfully, reaching my weak parts at once.
The knowledge that I was.rauuhy sinking
from exhaustion through"excessive pAftfAnd
loss of appetite was dispelled by a few
doses of Clements Tonic, which made me eat
without feeling any bad after-effects. Con-
stipation troubled me no more, and my head-
aches were driven away. My eyesight,
which had been very poor during my long
.sickness, was soon restored, and the melan-
choly languor gave way to renewed vivacity.
I never slept better in all my life than I did
after a dose or two of Clements Tonic, and
my limbs no longer ached with weariness in
the mornings. Once more I grew contented
and happy when I discovered that Clements
Tonic was working wonders with my debili-
tated constitution, and I was glad to bo able
to go to work with a will. Every day im-
proved my general appearance, as my colour
came back and my emaciated frame got
stouter and more robust. The unhealthy
sensitiveness of my nerves was banished by
the stimulating and restorative elfects of
Clements Tonic, which I continued to take
until it accomplished a thorough defeat of
my disease, not a single ailment of which
has troubled me since. 5
“ Are you satisfied the cure is permanent?”
“Perfectly. Nearly three years have
elapsed since my health was restored by
Clements Tonic, and I have felt grand all the
time. It is for that reason I desire to pub-
licly express my gratitude, which you are
at liberty to do in any way.you think best.”

SATURDAY, MARCH 15, 1902.
The Federal Loan.
The announcement that the Federal Govern-
ment proposes to float a loan is not a matter
of surprise. Its loans will probably become,
in course of time, as regular as those of the
State Governments, and where there used to
be six great borrowers in the Commonwealth
there will now be seven—until the Northern
Territory is organised as a separate item of
the Union, and then there will be eight.
The proposed Federal loan is almost wholly
for unproductive works. A part of it is for
fortifications and defence works, which will
probably be obsolete in a few years through
the constant changes in the art of slaying
our fellow-creature. A good deal more
is for guns which will certainly be
obsolete in a few years, or else worn
out. The rest is for various purposes—
replacing with borrowed money worn-out or
obsolescent telegraphic apparatus, which was
originally purchased with borrowed money,
and the like. Treasurer Turner, who is a
desperately cautious man, as Australian
Treasurers go, though in England he would
be regarded as a public financier of a most
reckless order, proposes to take strong
measures—as strong measures are under-
stood in Australia—to prevent the Federal
debt degenerating into a perpetual*burden.
Each loan is to have a sinking fund of 1 per
cent, attached to it, which sinking fund
ought to be sufficient, at compound interest,
to extinguish the liability in 47 years—
though the guns and other trash bought
with the loan money will mostly last
about half that time. Of course, when
the sinking-fund has grown to a large
enough figure, some impecunious Treas-
urer will, persuade Parliament to author-
ise its seizure. If this can’t be done
directly it will be done indirectly, as is the
case in Maoriland, where, for every £lOO,OOO
that is paid into the sinking fund to comply
with the law, £lOO,OOO is borrowed and put
into the revenue. Or it will happen as in
N.S. W., where £150,000 is paid off the old
deficit every year, and about an equal sum
added to the new deficit. There are various
sinking funds in the Commonwealth already,
and the best of them only amounts to a
pitiable joke.
* * • *
Assuming that the Federal loans average
£500,000 a year (they will probably amount
to much more than that, but whatever they
average the moral lesson is the same), it is
worth while considering how even Treasurer
Turner s careful policy will pan out.
Assuming that no Treasurer lays a sacrilegi-
ous hand on the sinking funds, which is
almost beyond belief, the first loan will be
wholly fpaid ofi at the end of 47 years,
so that date may be taken as a con-
venient stopping place at which to review
the situation. If the Federal Government
has great good fortune it may float its 3 per
cent, loans at a small discount—it may per-
haps raise £500,000 by giving its lOU for
£525,000. It isn’t likely to get much better
terms than these as things go nowadays, and
it may get worse. There will probably also
be about 10s. per cent, for expenses and cost
of management—that is to say, if the loans
are all floated locally—which may, or may
not, be covered by the margin suggested
above. If the Federal Legislature ever
begins to borrow large sums, and goes to the
English market for them, the expenses will
be much heavier, but The Bulletin here
gives it the benefit of the doubt. Then, at
the end of 47 years, when, barring accidents,
the first loan will be repaid, the account will
stand something like this :
After 47 Years Borrowing : Average Loans £500,000 a
Year at 3 per cent.
Paid in Interest (about) £16,920,000
„ Sinking Funds (about) 5,640,000
~ Discount and Expenses (about) .. 1,175,000
Debt still owing over and above Sinking
Funds (about) 11,750,000
£35,485,000
* * * *
And against this, if the Federal Govern-
ment borrowed nothing at all, and instead
raises £500,000 a year for its public works
policy by taxation, the exact amount the
people of the Commonwealth would have to
pay in the above period of 47 years would be
£23,500,000. The Commonwealth would
save, roughly speaking, £12,000,000 in 47
years, or an average of £250,000 a year, by
not borrowing. The borrowing policy costs,
on a very low average, £750,000 a year ; the
non-borrowing or self-reliant policy, £500,000
a year. Even if no allowance is made for the
debt that will exist at the end of the 47 vears
and for the burden of about £920,000 in in-
terest and sinking fund (3 per cent, interest
and 1 per cent, sinking fund on a gross debt
of about £23,000,000) which will remain to be
met every year thereafter, for as many years
or centuries as this rate of borrowing is
maintained—even if this huge load is not
reckoned, the actual outgoings of the first 47
years of the Federal borrowing policy will be
as heavy as if the whole trouble had been
met out of taxation. And The Bulletin
asks once more : What use or joy or
profit is there in the whole miserable busi-
ness ? There will be no more satisfaction,
no more public works, no direct or indirect
profit whatever.
* * • «
What the politicians of this country re-
gard as the advantage of the great loan
idea is this, and this only : If the
Federal Government borrows £500,000 a
year for public works instead of raising
£500,000 a year by taxation, it will make
the burden lighter for the first 20 or 25
years. After that it will be much heavier,
and will remain so for ever. It is a poor,
wretched device, worthy of a crowd of
cowardly and unfeeling politicians, backed
by a cowardly, or unfeeling, or unthinking
community, to leave the sons and daughters
that come after us to shoulder the burden.
The consistent policy of the present genera-
tion of Australians is to sell the public
lands, and exhaust the gold deposits, and
cut down the forests, and spend the pro-
ceeds of all these ; and to leave to its chil-
dren an exhausted land and the bill for all
the roads, the worn-out public buildings, the
unprofitable railways, and for many other
things even less tangible. In many cases,
however, the old Australian who did his
share of these things lived long enough to
experience, in his old age, some of the deso-
lation that his own policy had brought about,
and no one deserved it more.
* * * f
There is no reason, save cowardice and
heartlessness and incompetence, why the
Federal Parliament should resort to a loan
policy. The customary excuse that it is
hampered and restricted by the Braddon
clause of the Federal Constitution, under
which three-fourths of the customs and
excise revenue must, for a certain number of
years, be returned to the State Treasuries,
has often been made, and by persons in high
political station, but, whoever makes it, it is
none the less a lie. If there were no Braddon
clause, the Federal Government could cer-
tainly use more of the tariff revenue for its
own purposes, but, if it applied the tariff
revenue to public works purposes, the
State would have to levy more direct
taxation to fill the gap and meet the interest
on their liabilities. The Federal Govern-
ment could levy that direct taxation just
as well itself, and thereby get the money for
its public works, and the community would
pay no more. The only difference is that
while there is a Braddon clause the Federal
Government, if it wants more revenue than
its share of the customs and excise affords it,
must take the responsibility of raising that
revenue itself. Supposing there were no
Braddon clause it could put the responsi-
bility on to the State Governments, which
would be an easy and, at the same time, a
cowardly and shufflesome thing to do. And
yet it is questionable if the Federal Govern-
ment would have even the courage to do
that easy shuffle. For this year, at all
events, the Braddon clause isn’t in the
way. The £500,000 or so which is wanted
this year could be taken out of revenue, and
yet the Federal Government would be spend-
ing a smaller proportion of the tariff revenue
than the Tasmanian ex-Premier’s so-called
Blot permits. If the Federal Government
hasn’t the courage to use the powers it
already possesses, then there is very little to
be gained by giving it larger powers.
* * • •
Australia is full of gassy politicians who
profess to have a patriotic and far-sighted
eye to the future of the Commonwealth and
the Empire, and to be willing to make
sacrifices for the sake of that future. They
eventuate in speeches at agricultural shows,
where the bloated cow and the dropsical
pumpkin are on view, and they don’t even
pay their own expenses in getting there, or
for their own victuals when they are there.
They transpire at banquets when the con-
tingent— whose pay is added to the floating
debt—is going away ; or at the opening of
the hopelessly unpayable railroad, the bill
for which is left for posterity to
liquidate. But among the whole gang
there appears to be not one of any
prominence who cares a straw for the
country’s future. There hardly appears to
be one who cares even for the future of his
own children to an extent which will lead
him to propose carrying his own fair share
of the national burdens and trying to make
Australia a country which will be as good for
them to live in as it was for himself. The
beginning and middle and end of Australian
statesmanship is to pass the load on to the
infant. Possibly the infant will decline to
pay, but that doesn’t make the present gang
of Bartons, Kingstons, Reids, Turners,
Philps, Peacocks, Jenkinses, Forrests,
Sees and all their kind any the less a dis-
grace to their country, and to their alleged
intelligence and their bogus patriotism.
Australia has already had one warning. It
is the only civilised country on earth which
is dotted over with notoriously insolvent
banks that are living on the forbearance of
their creditors, and it smells badly enough
already in the world's atmosphere without
worse things happening to it. A while
later, if the present order of statesman-
ship continues, the Australian bubble will
burst with a bad perfume, and Australia will
wake up to a knowledge that the habit of
living on loans is only an unhealthy and
deceptive kind of disease ; and in the waking
process the banks which went in 1893 will go
all over again ; and the ones which didn’t go
then will also go ; and even the great A.M.P.
Society will totter on its basis amid the
slump in the value of its mortgages, and the
old age pensions will stop, and O’Sullivan,
if he is still about, will apply vainly at the
Labor Bureau for a job of sand-shifting, and
will be hooted by the other sand shifters there
assembled.
Under New Conditions.
Ever since the days when Cromwell’s Navigation
Laws and Blake’s naval victories shattered the
Butch maritime supremacy, with a few short
interruptions, Britain’s control of sea-power has
made her practically the world’s arbiter. Which
ever scale she threw her weight into quickly made
the other kick the beam, for she could strike
where she chose, where the enemy was weakest,
and with a comparatively small force keep a whole
nation underarms. To-day, even after 50 years
of Ereetrade and unrestricted competition have
sapped her energy and killed off the sturdy agri-
cultural population which, on land and sea,
stamped flat everything that stood up against it,
it is an open question if a combination of the
next two greatest naval powers could smash
the heirs of the old sea-kings. But the conditions
are changing. The decisive factor of the 20th
century promises to be Control of Railroad power.
* * * *
The world resounds with the clang of railroad
construction. The Russians are just completing
their Trans-Siberian railway— two ribbons of steel
4525 miles long, across which the Czar can shake
his rifle in the face of all Asia. Rhodes’ vaster
scheme of the Cairo to Cape line, 1100 miles longer
than even the gigantic Siberian road, is nearly
half completed. The Cairo-Khartoum section of
1341 miles is now working, and in the south the
Cape-Buluwayo section has traversed 1373 miles,
and the intermediate gap of 2950 miles presents
less engineering difficulties than the two com-
pleted sections. A branch of what will eventually
be the great trunk line has been constructed from
Mombassa, on the east coast of Africa, to Kampala
in Uganda—the first cross-bar of the gridiron on
which Darkest Africa is to be cooked for the
benefit of civilisation. In Asia, the Germans
have in hand a line from Scutari and Smyrna which,
when completed, will run down the valley of
Mesopotamia, past the ruins of Babylon, have
a refreshment-room at Bagdad, the city of the
Caliphs, and end somewhere on the Persian Gulf.
» * * *
But vaster projects are looming ahead. The
tobacco-stained hand of the all-fired Yankee is
reaching out over South America for what he calls
the “Pan-American Railroad”—a vast concern
which is to be the father of all railroads, and
which, when completed from New York to Buenos
Ayres, will be 10,220 miles long. The trial sur-
veys have already been made at the expense of the
United States out of that tremendous annual sur-
plus, and the report is favorable. Although the
engineering difficulties are enormous, they are not
as great as those which have already been over-
come on the Mexican plateau. At present
unbroken lines run from New York to Oajaca, in
Mexico, 3277 miles, and only 461 miles additional
remain between the terminus and the Mexican
southern border. In Central America there are
210 miles of lines which can be utilised, and 820
miles, to be built, will carry the line over the
Isthmus of Panama into the United States of
Colombia. Colombia being innocent of railways,
1354 miles will require to be built to Ecuador,
whence 658 more will bring it to Peru. The land
of the Incas has 151 miles of line which can he
utilised, but 1633 miles must be built. Then the
railway builders will strike 587 miles across Bolivia,
of which only 195 miles are already built. From
the Bolivian border 125 additional miles will con-
nect the great road with the Argentine north-west
terminus at Jujuy, whence lines already run to
Buenos Ayres on the Atlantio side.
* * * *
When this line is completed the United States
will have a practical monopoly of the back-door
trade of seven-eighths of South America. Owing
to the difficulties of transport the practically in-
exhaustible supplies of raw material of every sort
in the valleys of the Orinoco, the Amazon and
the Paraguay, have never been put on the world’s
markets. If the U.S. can build its record rail-
way it will flood these regions with its manu-
factures, and take from them in return cheap raw
tnaterial. A great Yankee mining population
will probably flow in, and what with one influence
and another, the South Americas will be merely a
United States plantation. In the end the gentle-
man with the twang and the star-spangled banner
of Liberty and Lucre will probably own South
America—if that wonderful railway is built.
* * * *
A few weeks ago it was cabled that American
capitalists were making heavy purchases of
Canadian Pacific stock in order to get control of
the Canadian railways. Now comes further news
that the Russians are surveying a route from the
Siberian railway to the Straits of Behring, and it
is seriously proposed to join the Asiatic and
American railway systems by either a viaduct
oyer, or a tunnel under the Behring Strait. The
distance is 48 miles, but right in the middle is an
island some 12 miles across. Recent soundings
have proved that nowhere is the strait more than
40 feet deep, with a hard, firm, level rock bottom.
Being so shallow there are no icebergs of any size,
and engineers declare that a viaduct from both
sides, meeting on the island in the centre, is a com-
paratively easy matter. If this is accomplished
the Argentine will be able to take his first-class
railway ticket at Buenos Ayres for Paris or Cape-
town, and get there without stepping on the deck
of a steamer, for the scheme includes the tunnel-
ling of the Straits of Gibraltar so as to connect
Europe with a line which a British, French and
American syndicate is proposing to build
across northern Africa to connect with
the Cape to Cairo railway. On this line
refreshments will doubtless be procurable at
Timbuctoo. When these lines are completed the
control of sea-power will slump considerably in
value. Of course, the time must come when the
British possessions in Asia and Africa will be
assailable from Europe by land, and the control
of the sea will no longer be a guarantee of their
safety. The expansion of railways made that
inevitable. But the time appeared an indefinite
distance away. The Yankee, in his haste and his
billions of dollars, reckons that it will come early
this century.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Invariable Story.
The Newcastle (N.S.W.) Herald with an air of
pained surprise (March 1):
The first official inspection of portions of the city by
members of the Borough Council has resulted in the dis-
closure of a shocking state of affairs. Practically in the
heart of the city there exists a set of buildings so inexpres-
sibly foul and insanitary that the visitors there and then
came to the conclusion that their immediate demolition
was necessary. The account given elsewhere by our
special reporter is sufficient to show that the Borough
Council is one of the first offenders against the sanitary
laws which it is its duty to enforce. The indictment
which the municipality has prepared against itself is
heightened by the fact that reports on the insanitary con-
dition of certain of its revenue-producing properties have
been brought before it, and that it has taken no action in
the matter. The civic inspection has not yet concluded,
and it is to be feared that before it is at an end, further
horrifying disclosures of a similar kind may be made, ft
is earnestly to be hoped that the council will put an end
once and for ever to this shameful evil.
Whenever a plague scare, or any other disease
scare arises, and people go out to inspect things
in an Australian city, there is ever the same
invariable story of filth. And there is always a
fair chance that the Borough Council is found
to be well up in the running for first prize
for shocking disregard of the sanitary laws,
to enforce which should be its main object
in life. The Borough Council whose property
isn’t dirty is generally the hard-up one, which has
no property. From the study of a multitude of
facts, The Bulletin is forced to the conclusion
that the alderman, as a class, is not a wildly clean
individual, and that if he has a serious compe-
titor in dirtiness it is the Government depart-
mental officer, as evidenced by the state of
Sydney General Post Office and the complaints
about the neglected condition of the resumed area
of Sydney. By way of initiating a reform this
paper proposes that each State should exchange
its State Governor, who is really superfluous now,
for a scientific Sanitary Commissioner at the same
salary, with extensive powers to keep local bodies
up to the mark in sanitary matters The Sanitary
Commissioner could open the State Parliament,
assent to Acts of Parliament, and summon R. D.
Meagher to form a Ministry in his spare time,
so these gubernatorial duties need not be ne-
glected. And if the maintenance of the British
connection is essential he could be appointed by
the authorities in London—so that the silken
bonds might be disinfected as well as strengthened.
Wanted: A Department of Sweeps.
A Tasmanian toiler writes his opinions on a
matter of Commonwealth concern :—-
The chief objection to “ Tattehsall’s ” establishment,
apart from the “moral” one urged by the parsons, is,
first, that it is a gambling machine, and therefore, econo-
mically considered, a mere waste. The latter doesn’t
matter much—one method more or less of wasting sub-
stance can hardly affect a society wherein five-sixths of
the population’s working time is already wasted in com-
petition. But a private monopoly of any sort is always a
menace to the community, and the smaller the com-
munity the greater the menace. Already “ Tatter,sail ”
practically bosses the island State ; his “ pull” in politics
is acknowledged on all hands, and his “ pull ” over
the newspapers is undisputed. Distribution of favors
in the shape of donations to charities, fancy billets
to “well-connected” young Tassies, complimentary
tickets to pressmen, politicians, and other persons of
influence in the island, naturally increases the number of'
“barrackers” for the institution. Also, Adams has
recently (with a possible eye to the Tassies’ traditional
worship of the landholder) become a large owner of
Hobart property. It is not too much to say that if
“ Tattkrsall” were allowed undisputed possession of his
monopoly for twenty years longer the descendants of
George Adams would practically own the whole Flyspeck.
As a protected industry “ Tattersall’s ” takes first place


in the Commonwealth. Competitors are absolutely
barred. But, unlike other protected industries, it cannot
possibly either teach the young a useful trade, develop
the natural resources of fee country, reduce imports,
increase exports, or help in paying off the country s
liabilities. It cannot enrich the community in any
way, but must impoverish it, to at least the extent of
the cost of feeding George Adams and his assistants.
Regarded as an amusement “ Tattersall’s” gambling
school is possibly as legitimate as a theatre or a circus, or
any other place of worship, and, as such, has an equal
right to conduct business without hindrance. But there
is no more justification for giving it a monopoly of the
gambling business than there would be for granting any
one parson the monopoly of the soul-saving business and
threatening to gaol any other person who dared to wave
a collection-plate in public or in private. The average
citizen does not object to consultations anymore than he
objects to tote betting, but he has a decided objection to
being compelled to always lose his money in the same
tote-shop; he wants to have the opportunity of
going broke in any way he chooses, and argues
that no Government ever had the remotest right
to deprive him of whatever variety there may be
in the roads to ruin. Of course, the argument isn t
particularly sound from a sociological standpoint, but
that doesn’t matter ; it is just about how the average
man, ?who isn’t a student of sociology, feels about it,
and, in the main, the average citizen is right enough.
The whole duty of the State is to see that the gambling is
done decently and in order, and that reasonable facilities
are provided to prevent overcrowding on the road to
ruin. The best way to do it is to place the whole “ con-
sultation” business directly under the control of the
State. Absolute freetrade in gambling is, like absolute
freetrade in anything else, obviously undesirable, if not
more so. If there has to be a monopoly in the business
at all it should be owned and controlled by the Common-
wealth. If Australia has to pay for running its “ two-
up” school it may just as well pay itself as “ Tatter-
SALL.” It isn’t good enough for a community to permit
any individual to coin money out of either its vices or its
virtues. “Tattersall” may build pubs, out of his pro-
fits, but is not likely to build hospitals or establish old-age
pensions. If the Commonwealth had the profits it could
do both.
Another Phase of Cohentingenting.
A last week’s cablegram :
Mr. Brodrick (Secretary for War) hopes that colonial
statesmen will be willing to undertake responsibilities,
rendering all the Imperial forces available for a war
involving the interests of the whole Empire.
From a strictly British point of view the Secretary
of War is, of course, quite right. From an Aus-
tralian point of view there are doubts and ques-
tions and side issues. Who is to decide what kind
of war “involves the interests of the Empire?”
If the British authorities make the war, or adopt
the policy which leads up to the war, and simply
report to “the colonies” that it is a war
“involving the interests of the Empire,” and
“the colonies” have to take their word for
it and supply all their available troops, and
Britain gets the conquered territory, theu
“the colonies” might as well give up their
self-governing rights bodily. At present
things have not quite reached this stage.
Certainly Australian politicians had no share in
bringing about the present South African war.
They did not cheat the Dutch colonists out of the
compensation voted by Parliament when slavery
was abolished, which compensation was paid in
all other parts of the Empire. They did not steal
Natal from the Boers in time of peace, or take the
Diamond Fields from them in time of peace, or
arm the Zulus against them, or offer a reward for
the capture of the President who objected to the
Natal steal, or declare their country annexed in
the ’4o’s, or cheat the Transvaalers out of their
promised right to a local Parliament when their
country was really annexed in the ’7o’s, or pro-
hibit them having a seaport, so that their foreign
commerce must go mostly through British
territory and be taxed en route, or make
the demand that the government of the Transvaal
should be practically handed over to the foreign
adventurers at Johannesburg in the ’9o’s. All this
long accumulation of insults, injuries, and hatreds
was built up by British statesmen, and when
war came of it Au tralia went into that purely
local war as one “involving the interests of the
Empire.” But Australia, at all events, went into
it voluntarily. Secretary for War Brodrick
now proposes that some kind of permanent
arrangement shall be made by which Australia
will always go into such wars, voluntarily or not.
Its forces are just to be “available,” whereby it
will get down to the level of the Hindu, who also
has no voice in m king or unmaking the Empire’s
wars, but whose forces are also available. And
here the rock on which the ill-jointed British
Empire must needs ultimately split, if the Empire
doesn’t reorganise, comes fairly in sight. Unless
a workable scheme of Imperial Federation is
devised “ the colonies ” can’t have any voice in
making the Empire’s wars, despite the fact that
they are the biggest part of the Empire in size,
and will yet be the biggest in white popula-
tion also. A workable scheme of Imperial r edera-
tion can’t be devised without abolishing the
hereditary House of Lords —to which Britain
clings with profound reverence; without drawing
a definite color line against the huge black
majority which can’t be allowed to boss the white
minority, yet which must boss the white minority
if representation in a really Imperial Parliament
goes by population; and without facing the fact
that the Imperial capital must needs shift some
day when the centre of population shifts -to
Australia or Canada or some other place, and the
local affairs of the dependency of England be at-
tended to by a Governor sent over from Ottawa or
Bombala. And all these are great difficulties—
the nigger majority probably the greatest of the
lot. So Britain doesn’t see its way to give the
colonies’’any control of the empire’s policy, yet
it wants them to undertake that they will tight
for the empire’s policy every time. The empire
has a war on about once a year ; it had fully 50
in Victoria’s reign alone—and that is calculated
to make “the colonies” tired. _ One war, let alone
fifty, has almost made them tired. Then, if an
agreement is arrived at, as suggested, by which
“the colonies” must supply forces whenever
called upon, the politician of the day will gam no
credit, no glory, no title, no bang of drums and
crash of trumpets by supplying these forces, and
that will make him tired. He will just-be doing
a dreary, stodgy, dusty, everyday duty. Probably
he won’t even get the order for so much human
meat; if there is a permanent arrangement, then
the local commander will simply receive instruc-
tions to put the meat on board, as per usual, and
send it along in the ordinary way. With the
contingent business always going on, and the
limelight always turned off, and the bill always
coming in, and all the glory of “ standing by the
Empire ” reduced to the level of the weekly
washing-day, things will be sadly different. And
when things grow too different -then the
differentness of things leads to differences.

Melbourne’s “ Tip.”
Melbourne continues to prepare a, royal re-
ception for the bubonic rat. Its City Council
last year started with a deficit of £20,000.
With much reluctance it raised the very moderate
city rate of Is. in the pound up to Is. 4d. This
enabled it to reduce the deficit to £10,487. But
now with plague knocking at the door it reverts
to the old rate of Is. and trusts to “economies” to
come out square. The said economies mainly
depend on letting filth accumulate. In every
ward of the city the maintenance men have been
reduced from twelve to three, the street watermen
from seven to three —30 men being suddenly
sacked in one act —while 42 of the 47 hands at the
quarries have been given the order of the bag.
When the plague threatened to visit Melbourne a
couple of years ago the Council bought a garbage-
destructor—only a small affair, but it, at least,
destroyed the worst of the abominations. But
the destructor cost a few pounds to work, so
it was decided that the destructor fires must
go out and the rubbish again go to. the tip.
This tip is several acres in extent, and is a fetid
mass of corruption—of ancient bananas, out-of-
date fish, and diseased rats. A better breeding
ground for disease germs couldn’t be imagined.
But with the fear of Plague before it Melbourne
Council decided to continue the tip and not set the
destructor going again. The fear of a higher city
rate (which would affect the rich) was greater than
the dread of disease (which would mostly affect
the poor). One or two councillors urged the
re-starting of the destructor, but they were
silenced by such as Cr. Strong and Alderman
Pigdon (suggestive name). Also Mayor Sam
Gillott bluntly told the Council that “unless
they were prepared to strike a higher rate than Is.
in |the £ the present system of garbage-disposal
would have to be adhered to,” and that settled it!
It always does settle it: the aversion of any
Australian public body to paying its way honestly
positively amounts to a disease.
♦
The Hare-Somebody Voting Muddle.
That huge and cumbrous foolishness, the Federal
Electoral Bill, with its HARE-Somebody system of
voting, its contingent vote, its first, second, third,
fourth, and 127th preferences of candidates, its
quota, its “Droop quota,” its arrangement for the
transfer of parcels and sub-parcels of votes, its ten
or more countings of the vote, its provision for the
adequate or inadequate or intoxicated representa-
tion of minorities, its obscurity, frills, and general
exasperation, appears to be practically bogged in
the Commonwealth Parliament. And it certainly
deserves to be bogged. The worst misery about
the scheme, and it is full of miseries, is its utter
futility. Tasmania has a law of this kind, and it
tried the same on the federated dog at the elec-
tion of Senators. The result was worth noting.
The vote had to be counted 14 times before the
contract was finished, and far into the night
candle-grease was dropping on the heads of weary
returning officers, as they pursued the elusive
quota to its lair. There were 113 parcels of con-
tingent votes transferred. Defeated candidates
“cut out” at different stages, as if they were
wool-sheds, and there were “surpluses”—as if
the Treasurer had at last got the accounts to
square. And in the end the whole 15 candidates
stood in exactly the same order as if the first vote
only had been counted, and there had been no
contingent vote whatsoever; and the same six
were elected as would have been elected
if the first vote only had been counted;
and all the rest was wasted time and ink.
The same result crops up with unvarying
regularity at Tasmania’s State elections. The
ordinary mode of election often leads to the return
of a member who represents nothinglike a majority
of the votes polled, and the only advantage gained
by the HARE-Somebody system is that, after
counting the votes 14 times, the public is left in
the same hole as at first. Tasmania, when it had
gone through all its trouble, had only the same old
Dobson to show for it that it would have had in
any case. If the old way of voting failed to
adequately represent the minority or the majority,
the HARE-Somebody method failed in exactly the
same way and the same place, and stubbed its toe
on the same root, and it was all vanity and com-
plication of spirit.

The Failure to Reform James.
The necessity for prison reform obtrudes itself
from the story of James Cathcart, an “old
offender,” aged 32, whowas lately sentenced to three
months’ imprisonment by the Ballarat East (Vic.)
magistrates. Cathcart got into trouble this time
for the indiscretion of bashing his niece’s coun-
tenance when she protested against his brutal ill-
usage of her aunt. It was his 19th conviction.
One of his previous offences was the manslaughter
of a Chow, for which he received six years hard
labor. And Cathcart is now an able-bodied
blackguard of 32 ! The Law has been treating
him for many years for his criminality, and will
no doubt resume the treatment soon after he
regains his liberty to get drunk. The gaol is the
place where Cathoart goes into physical and
moral training for further deeds of violence.
His country is not proud of Cathcart.
Nevertheless the Government takes him in hand at
every possible opportunity, and makes him fit and
eager for the fray. The “ reforming ” influence of
gaol is a confessed failure in James Cathcart’s
case, yet every time he is presented at Court
for another sentence the police quote a long list of
failures to reform him as a reason why the next
period of failure should be as long as the Bench
can make it. And the dull, lumbering Law, whilst
acting on this suggestion, cheerfully goes on to
admit that it is bad in principle. Perhaps the
very next case to be tried is a First Offender
whose offence doesn’t come under the heading of
“serious.” Then the evil tendencies of the gaol
where Cathcart is being reformed are advanced
as reasons why the novice prisoner should not be
sent there. The habitual offender is inferentially
quoted as proof that imprisonment creates crime.
And the Law admits the awful failure of its prison
system by releasing the F.O. on his own recog-
nizances or some similar futile substance.

Political Profligates.
The combined agitation of the Freetrade and
Protectionist daily newspapers in Australia has
resulted in the Federal Parliament removing the
small duty of 10 per cent, on printing paper.
Both classes of papers showed a political profli-
gacy, naked and unashamed, during the agitation.
The Freetrade newspapers have been crying out
for a tariff which would raise revenue by 10 or 15
per cent, ad valorem duties and avoid heavy im-
posts on the food and clothing of the poor. The
paper duty was a light one, and promised a good
revenue, drawn from a class of the community
exceptionally well able to pay; and so it
fulfilled all the requirements of the Freetrade
papers’ demand; but, by open and secret
effort, they united to down it, because it
touched the pockets of their proprietaries. The
Protectionist daily papers joined in the agitation
against the duty from equally sordid motives.
Printing paper is now made almost wholly from
pine wood, of which there is no great supply in
Australia, therefore it is claimed that paper can’t
be made here. If it can’t, then the promised 10
per cent, duty would not be a Protective duty,
and the Protectionist papers have a right to object
to it being regarded as such. But paper can be
made from many substances, and there is no
evidence whatever that because the Yankee makes
it from pine the Australian can’t make it from
other materials. And the Protectionist organs
have no right to object to a revenue duty on their
paper, seeing that revenue is required. At all
events, the rich Freetrade newspaper proprietors
denounced the duty on their own material on the
ground that it was a Protectionist duty, and the
rich Protectionist ones because it wasn’t, so one
party must be a sordid Ananias ; and the solid
resolve of both these rich parties to run no possible
risk of contributing to the revenue was a splendid
instance of how generous foes can sink their
differences at a crisis and unite to pass the taxa-
tion on to somebody else. Both parties denounced
the proposed duty as a tax on knowledge—-but the
public is weary of the thin pretence that these
rich newspaper owners carry on business, not for
the sake of dividends, but from a high sense of
their duty to educate the community. The public
sees clearly that these papers pursue the one
object of profit with undeviating and unflinching
purpose : that for the sake of profit they will
smother any truth and father almost any lie ; and
that a slight regard for public interest
is only shown when the public penny makes
resolute demand therefor. In the one matter
of public finance, the people in Sydney have
seen, within the past few years, one daily journal
defending Reid’s rotten financial system day after
day, month after month, with an absolutely blind
disregard for facts ; then suddenly discovering the
necessity for telling the truth in a day, and turn-
ing from that brief spell of candor to just as shame-
lessly buttress up the Lyne system ; and finally
telling the truth about that only when it was
too late to effect any good. The people of the
same city, or of any other of the Australian capi-
tals, can pick from the columns of that, or any
other paper, dozens of other instances where, on
the testimony of their own columns, the newspaper
proprietaries can be convicted of the most abso-
lute and shameless falsehood. Those sturdy
beggars, the paper proprietors, extort concessions
from politicians, not in recognition of their ser-
vices as public educators, but of their power as
party organs. It is a Tammany system by which
the politician placates the paper out of the pockets
of the taxpayer ; and it will never be done away
with until—but that opens up altogether too large
a question.
The Ethics of Breach of Promise.
The jury in the Cousins-Carr (Sydney) breach-
of-promise case did not act logically in awarding
the plaintiff a beggarly £l5O as damages. He
stated in evidence that he had lost much more
than that in solid cash in the pursuit of the fickle
heiress who jilted him for Mr. Carr. If the jury-
men believed him, they should have awarded full
compensation for his cash losses, his vain expendi-
ture of time, etc. If they didn’t, they had no
right to award him anything. It is the cash loss,
and not the sentimental loss, which has to be
taken into account in such cases. The sentimen-
tal loss might be taken into account also if it
could be translated into anything that the jury
has control over, but a broken heart and a disillu-
sioned mind, and the breakage of a high ideal,
and the falling of a goddess from her pedestal,
can’t be assessed in cash. Nor can the jury award
plaintiff a new ideal, or a poem, or a dream, or a
sunset, or a new goddess on a pedestal, or
anything supposed to be good for soul-break-
age. That item has to stand, but material
loss is a thing within a jury’s reach. And though
much absurdity is talked nowadays about the
impropriety of breach-of-promise actions, the
law should take as full cognisance of such a breach
of contract as of any other involving a loss to
either of the parties. If a man engages himself
to marry a woman, and, on the understanding of
that engagement, induces her to abandon, for,
perhaps, a lengthy term, the ghief business of her
life—securing a suitable husband, and subse-
quently breaks his engagement, he inflicts just
as real a cash wrong as if he had engaged her as
governess and not paid her any wages. For a
term of perhaps two years, possibly the best years
of her life, he has kept the woman from the quest
of a husband and thus materially injured her
matrimonial prospects. It is right, therefore,
that he should pay compensation. Similarly, if
a woman whose great beauty or whose for-
tune makes her a very desirable wife, engages
herself to a man and then jilts him, she inflicts a
cash wrong, since, during the period of engage-
ment, he might have been hunting another beauty
or another fortune. Matrimony is not so much a
matter of sentiment as a matter of business—
especially on the woman’s side. Why, then,
should there be a chorus of ridicule, and a disin-
clination on the part of Judge and jury to be
serious over the matter, when a breach of marriage
contract comes into court ? A bill-of-lading case
is not treated in that way. And the two really
stand on the same Commercial footing.

The Jingo Lie.
George Reid, in the course of his flatulent oration
at the S’Africa Day’s eating and drinking at
Clontarf (Sydney), managed to make quite a sur-
prising number of mis-statements, even for him,
about matters of fact. Here is one :
Never had there been known such a thing as a country
protecting the women and children of the men who were
fighting her own soldiers, a country feeding the very men
who had fought her.
There have been dozens of such instances. They
happen in every war. Take the case of the Ger-
mans in France in 1870-71. The Germans didn’t
even ask for “unconditional surrender.” Sedan,
Metz, and Paris were all surrendered on terms of
capitulation, and as soon as the terms were agreed
to the Germans began feeding the very men who
had fought them. The French and British did
the same with their respective prisoners in the
time of Napoleon I. The British, French,
Italians and Russians did it in the Crimea. The
Russians did it in Turkey ; the Boers did it with
their British prisoners at the beginning of the
present campaign, and do it still when they cap-
ture any prisoners. In 1870-71 the Germans pro-
tected the women and children of the French
soldiers just as they did all the other inhabitants.
Their houses were not burned after Ketciiener’s
fashion. They weren’t hustled away to disease-
sodden concentration camps. They weren’t
robbed of their possessions, and they were allowed
to follow their usual pursuits. The agents of
British, French, Belgian and other charitable
societies were allowed to go about freely distribut-
ing relief instead of being excluded ct la
Ketchener. Foreigners were permitted to travel
in the country wherever they pleased provided they
didn’t get right in the road of the armies—not shut
out on the Ketchener principle. George Reid’s
statement that no nation was ever before known to
protect the women and children of the enemy’s
country is about as silly a remark as any
old man of limited experience ever uttered.
Compared to all recent wars fought in
Europe, Ketchener’s mode of campaign-
ing has been grossly barbarous, and the
more barbarous it grows the louder is the cry of
admiration raised by peaceful, globular, bookless
old gentlemen whose titles haven’t arrived yet.
There will never be in Australia any honest ex-
pression of opinion on matters as between Britain
and other countries until titles are abolished, or
until the other countries also give titles to our
corrupt politicians and corrupt newspaper-bosses.
When there is an equal bribe on each side, or no
bribe on either side, then the square and honest
truth may be told.

N.S.W. Public Service Board is still wrestling
with the 900 odd appeals from its last regrading
of the Service, and the peculiar form of wrestle
which it generally adopts is causing more dissatis-
faction in the ranks. For instance : Some half-
dozen officers in the Red Tape Department
appealed in August. Last week, when they
had almost forgotten the circumstances, they
received notices that the Board would hear
them in defence. The time of service of
the notices was 2.10 p.m., and the time appointed
for hearing their cases was 2.30 that afternoon.
By 2.50 it was all over. Another case. An officer
in another branch of the Dept, also appealed some
eight months ago. Last January his health gave
way and he went away for three months’ sick-
leave. One day this week he returned to Sydney
en route to Tasmania, where he proposed to finish
his holiday, The Board somehow heard of his
presence and served notice on him that his appeal
was coming on that same afternoon. His papers
were all locked up, facts and figures forgotten,
and besides he was too ill to argue with any
Board. But down he had to go. So when it is
remembered that the Board has asked for £l5OO
to satisfy the claims of 900 appellants, or about
30s. apiece, it is no wonder that the bulk of the
disaffected regard the appeal system as a farce.
