Sydney Telegraph item:
The House of Representatives to-day rejected the
Government’s 10 per cent, duty on printing paper. This
impost was placed on the tariff in very peculiar circum-
stances. It was the result of some bluff on the part of
Mr. Reid. The leader of the Opposition, at the beginning
of the Parliament, twitted Treasurer Turner that, as head
of the Victorian Government, he had never dared to place
a duty on printing paper, and that it was through fear
of his principal journalistic supporter in Melbourne. The
sally had so much effect on the House and was so popular
that it was repeated on platforms outside.
And Mr. Reid himself not only dared not put a
duty on printing paper for fear of his principal
journalistic supporter in Sydney, but he also
dared not refuse to carry that supporter’s
wares free through the post and to supply
it with free special express trains daily to
carry its goods all over the country at the tax-
payer’s expense ! And how howling mad the
Telegraph is at the Federal Government, which
refuses to thus pauperise it any longer, and pro-
poses to let the bracing air of Freetrade blow on
the Freetrade organ !
-4
Speaking at Clontarf, a few days ago, G. H.
Reid got off the old wheeze about the white flag
and “ Boer treachery ” after this fashion :
Why, when the white flag was used as a cowardly
shelter for his attack, even then mercy was shown.
Hasn’t that nonsense been exploded often
enough ? The white flag simply means, “ Want
to communicate.” The British drill-book says :
When it is necessary to communicate with the enemy
under a flag of truce, a smart officer . . . accompanied
by a trumpeter . . . should ride towards the enemy’s
position with flag flying and trumpet sounding. . . They
should not retire until satisfied, after being repeatedly
fired at, that they have been seen by the enemy, and that
they will not be received.
Nobody in his senses believes in a flag of truce.
As often as not it is sent with some frivolous
excuse of a message, in the hope that the “ smart
officer” will be able to pick up some piece of
information about the other side. In war, the
whole aim of each side is to fool the other. For
that purpose the flag of truce is as good a means
as any other to penetrate inside your enemys’
outposts, so that you can get a chance, if he is
careless enough to allow you, to see what he is
doing, and guess, if you are sharp enough, what
he means to do. But the main point is that the
British drill-book alludes to firing on the white
flag as quite an ordinary incident of warfare—as
something that is done as a regular thing. The
alleged sacredness of the white flag only exists
in the imaginations of the ignorant. At Nichol-
son’s Nek, according to the reports published
with pride in every Australian paper, the British
kept on firing for nearly half-an-nour after they
had hoisted the white flag. And the Boers
showed them mercy. The incident wasn’t
described as a sinful treachery, but as an evidence
of the no-surrender character of the British
soldier.
Bananas.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
In Cairns, N.Q., at Chinese New Year, the white laborers
were out of work as only half a truck of bananas came
forward for shipment. —Daily Paper.
’Arf a truck of bananas !
Isn’t it heathen luck !
’Arf a truck of bananas
—Only a 'arf a truck.
And the white man sits with a gloomy brow
And sucks at his old black pipe.
And waits till it pleases the cheerful Chow
To cease from making his heathen row
And gather “ Banana ripe ! ”
’Arf a truck of bananas —
They have no kids to feed !
’Arf a truck of bananas,
Little of clothes they need ;
For they live as cheap as a poor-house rat
Which a white man cannot stand.
’Arf a truck —and be glad of that, —
For the white man starves while the Chow grows
fat
In the white man’s native land !
’Arf a truck of bananas,
They have no wives and bairns;
’Arf a truck of bananas,
They are the rulers of Cairns.
For the fittest lives—as the schoolboy knows,
In Nature’s cruel test,
The horse must starve where the rabbit goes,
And the sheep must perish to feed the crows,
And the fittest is not the best.
’Arf a truck of bananas,
’Ark to the bloomin’ gong!
’Arf a truck of bananas,
Look at ’em march along
With a brass concern like a dragon’s snout
That causes them great delight,
And the crackers bang and the Chinkies shout—
But the day will come when we’ll march ’em out
And see that the land is White !
ROBERT CARR.

“ Ben Hur,” at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, still
views the procession from his ancestral flat-
roofed palace and drops loose bricks on the
Procurator, and then goes to the galleys
and rows “ stroke ” for the slave club
against the pirate combine. Judah’s dress and
that of his fellow slaves as they feather their own
oars, is said to be the real historical undress of
men who filled the galley benches in those bad,
exciting old days. It consists principally of
leather straps supporting the muscles most under
strain. The work was fearfully hard, and there
appears to have been no pay-day at all. No post-
office orders for two talents and upwards ever
reached the wife of the Roman galley slave. But,
as he rarely survived the work three years, she
might look forward to widowhood and a subsequent
second marriage. That little summer tent on the
roof of Ben Hur’s palace is pretty. In it, no doubt,
the mother of Hur warmed the afternoon goat’s
milk on her days at home for visitors named Ruth,
Rebecca, Tamar and the like. But in it now
Miss Lane, as the mother of Hur, nuzzles her
son’s head against her knee, and her daughter
Tirzah’s head against her breast. Rose Musgrove
no longer plays Hur’s beloved sister Tirzah. She
has gone with the second “San Toy” Co., and
Flurrie Gleeson takes her Jewish place. The
Hurs kept a huge staff of servants. When the
cook-ladies, nurses, handmaids and tirewomen
run up to the roof in terror of the Roman soldiers
one can form an idea of Mrs. Hur’s responsibilities
—and comforts.

In the Revels of Daphne scene a masque
of Eros is a good “ turn.” A maid and a lad clad
in lambskins are in love, but the maid is coy, or
doesn’t know her own mind, or hasn’t asked
mamma, or something, and holds hack. Anon
comes Eros in the person of a baby girl dressed in
pink satin wings. Eros fixes matters with his
bow and arrow and the lamb-skinned maid grows
quite fond and demonstrative. The three horses
don’t love the white camel any more than at first,
and the camel’s scorn of the chorus is undisguised.
It doesn’t seem to like anything but chewing its
cud and groaning. The betting scene and the
chariot race remain two of the finest effects.
*# * *
“ Aladdin,” at Sydney Royal, is to be con-
gratulated on getting away from the usual ugly
Chinese costumes, at least so far as the women
are concerned, and robing its little Cheekees and
Washee-Washees quite irrespective of nationality.
They are dressed as the inhabitants of No Place in
Particular, which is a bright green country and
situated next to Things in General. In No Place
in Particular they only give the clothes a dry
wash after all. The management keeps its little
Chinkee ladies pretty well to dark hair, though
black locks—on the stage—are apt to strike a
harsh, coarse note amidst the harmonies of pinks
and pale yellows. The contrast in opera land is
always a little too violent. The Jap ladies,
however, rush in with locks shading into their
costumes. There is a gradual dawn of beauty :
First a few damsels, pale-garmented and pale-
haired, arrive like a small Japanese day-break;
then a few more dance in with a deeper tint of
gold on their floating draperies and gleaming
tresses, till at last there comes a rush of
Elizabethan locks and garments to match. These
ladies, who are all one color, suggest a useful idea.
If the same idea was adopted off the stage then it
would only be necessary for a lady to lay the end of
her tail of hair on the sales-eounter with a request
for clothes to match.
j** * *
“ Hearts are Trumps ” continues its glit-
tering career at Sydney Lyceum, and as a com-
bination of fairly good drama and an excellent
show of millinery commands the respect of male
and female onlookers alike. There is very little
love in the production with the sentimental title,
and the young cleric, whom the troublesome good
girl is understood to marry, is a shadowy person
who becomes useful only in the last scene when
several of the characters fall off precipices. Sydney
by this time has got over the shock of seeing its
Baker as a grumpy, grey-haired personage, whose
brother, by the way, is understood to be a convict
because he thoughtlessly went to Australia.
There was a time when it was dangerous to one’s
reputation to be known as having been in Aus-
tralia, but that has long since passed, and doesn’t
fit in with frocks of 1902. The refreshing circum-
stance about “Hearts are Trumps ” is that, though
various reputations are lost, they are not mislaid
by means of forged letters or dropped marriage
certificates, but by new, up-to-date methods, such
as Biographs and Art Academy Pictures. There
is something in being up-to-date, even in one’s
method of getting lost.
** * *
“ The World’s Entertainers ” lay down
their arms before a sea of troubles and leave
Sydney Palace this Saturday. They departed
from Sydney some months ago after having
revolutionised variety business in that city, amid
enthusiastic cheering, but came back to a decidedly
cool welcome. This was partly due to their
serious mistake in trying to make up the major
part of the programme of stale items; partly to
plague fears; partly to Bunth, Rudd and
Co. (far the best of the new turns) fading
out of the programme because of Bunth’s
illness, and the impossibility of doing much
with Rudd without Bunth; and the rest of
the trouble arose from other misfortunes. The
co., which to new audiences is as fine a
variety combination as could be imagined,
goes on tour, and an entirely fresh one
will resume at the Palace after the interval of a
week. During its last week the World’s Enter-
tainers are strengthened by the Casino trio, excep-
tionally artistic musicians with a clever vein of
comedy. Perhaps if they and others had arrived a
little earlier the result might have been different,
but then the world is full of things that might have
been different. And, when we come to think of
it, nothing ever is different.
** * *
The Lenton Trio of agile “ hat-jugglers ”
appeared for the first time at Sydney Tivoli on
Saturday. Miss Lenton, besides catching and
wearing several hats at once, sings coon songs as
they are not often sung. She does it in such a
way as to suggest that the writer of the coon song
needn’t be hanged after all, or, if he is hanged,
that he might at least be allowed spiritual conso-
lation first; and the two male Lentons do some
clever athletic business as well as the hats-
trick. Altogether, they are a rather agreeable
trio to meet.
The foregoing foolishness is one way of repeating
the statement that “ Sweet Nell,” at Melb. Prin-
cess’s, and “ The Runaway Girl,” at Her
Majesty’s, continue to be accepted as “ bits of all
right ” in their respective spheres of rightness.
The two shows have nothing in common beyond
certain names on the programmes. For instance,
Harcourt Beatty is His Majesty Charles 11, King
of England, at th-3 'Princess’s, whereas Fred,
England and May Beatty are wandering minstrels
at Her Majesty’s. Also, Howard Vernon is Sir
Somebody on one bill, and Miss Sadler is Lady
Vernon on the other. This will help to explain
the mental confusion of the rhymester who has
partaken freely of both entertainments during the
past week.
By the way, the nights of “Sweet Nell”
are numbered, owing to arrangements over which
that spiritualised orange-girl had no control. On
March 29 the estimable farewell tourist, Robert
Brough, will return to the Princess with Mrs.
Brough and the co., and will devote five weeks in
all to “ The Amazons” and other matters, includ-
ing a first Melbourne production of Pinero’s
“ Iris.” Therefore “ Nell ” seems certain to have
an exceptionally lovely time between now and
Easter.
** # *
The Bij jou is also hooked for. a new com-
pany on the 29th, when Geach and Willoughby
intend to put “The Wrong Mr. Wright” in
possession of the floor. Meanwhile the World’s
Entertainers have been recruited by the miniature
Dartos and infantile Floradorians, late of Sydney.
Salerno, the dainty juggler, is astounding as usual,
and the tenor, Neal M‘Cay, supplies a want, but
the Warsaw Bros, add nothing to the gaiety of
nations.
** * *
At Melb. Royal, a revival of Widow
O’Brien, which amounts to much the same thing
as the recent revival of Johanna Murphy, will
provide an outlet for John F. Sheridan’s she-male
humor until Friday of this week. On Saturday
(15th), Anderson’s dramatic co.—the co. wherein
Miss Duggan is the suffering heroine, and Bert
Bailey heads the comic relief detachment —falls
due at the Royal. Melbourne has not experienced
any Andersonian thrillers for a year or two. Some
that he brings along this time are new in the
melodramatic sense of the word, and the lurid
novelty billed for the 15th is “The Night Birds
of London.”
** * *
Ten energetic girls who play net ball, and
are becoming expert by degrees, were introduced
at the Opera House last Saturday. Otherwise,
the Rickards programme remains as before, or
nearly so. Derenda and Breen, the club jugglers,
are generally considered to deserve everything the
advt. says about them, and the popular Irish senti-
ment re the Callahan and Mack is endorsed by all
other sections of the local White Australia. More-
over, the talent of these two unorthodox artists
is so well-balanced that nobody troubles to wonder
which of them is Mack.
** * *
“The Two Worlds,” recently produced at
Adelaide Royal with much preliminary booming,
whs a wonderful spectacle. Heaven was freely-
shown in the opening scenes, a kind of electric-
lighted Catherine-wheel at the back presumably re-
presenting the Almighty, who had a big speaking
part, given by a voice “off.” There were 34
scenes, some of them lasting barely a minute (the
author, l 1 ather Duff, is evidently quite ignorant
of stagecraft). The audience stood things fairly
well until, a series of back-cloths developing, it
suddenly realised that it was assisting at the
creation of the world! The G-reat Deep, full
of “living souls” (as they were oddly called)
was a great joy, being apparently imitated
from one of E. T. Rees’s “prehistoric peeps,”
with an immense dragon in the middle distance,
and an irresistible schnapper—mouth open and an
astonished expression—gazing straight at the
audience in the foreground. With the Garden of
Eden came something like a dramatic interest,
but also obvious chances for the gallery boys, who
begged Eve to “ beware of the codlin moth.”
After that things became quite mixed. Peter and
John were suddenly found talking to the Virgin
Mary on the banks of the Jordan. There was a
“ Temptation scene ” (eight fully-clad girls danc-
ing in a wood with nobody else present), and the
final scene was “Sunset on the Coral Islands ” ! !
The dialogue was partly Milton, partly Genesis,
with slabs of Duff, as when Satan refused to
tempt Adam in person, because “ he would waste
time in asking thousands of questions.” The play
ran just two nights ; but someone with a sense of
humour, a large blue pencil, and £lO,OOO to mount
and dress it adequately, might make something
of it.
MARSH, N.S.W. Aboriginal Cricketer
and Athlete.
Photo, by Kerry & Co., Sydney
Marsh, for long more or less prominent through
cinder-track feats and bowling, has, this season, been
forced to the forefront by sole selector M. A. Noble’s
persistent ignoring of his proven wicket-bagging
capabilities, and also Maclaren’s “kick” against
his inclusion in the Bathurst team to play against the
Englishmen, as well as Bathurst’s weak-kneed backdown.
Marsh is a full-blooded aboriginal and first saw light at
Ohatswood Island (not Grafton, as generally stated),
Clarence River, 28 years ago. A neatly-built, nattily-
attired, well-behaved and presentable chap in every way
he looks all ascribed to him but his origin. Noble insisted,
when he did speak (and then not publicly), that Marsh
wasn’t favored because the quality of his bowling, though
disastrous in club cricket, fell much below inter-State
and international necessities ; besides he could not be
relied upon at a pinch, which would—more likely than
not—find him all astray. This was only Noble’s alleged
opinion; there was nothing whatever to'suppdrt it, and
everything to prove the opposite. Maclaren averred fear
for the limbs of his men on the eve. of the fourth test, and
dubbed Marsh a “ shyer.” Coming from a man who
stuck to Britisher Mold, after he had been named by the
Marylebone Club committee and no-balled by Phillips,
and in face of the fact that no umpire had all through
now-closing season questioned Marsh’s deliveries once,
this is distinctly good. Noble had no qualms about pick-
ing M‘Beth—Marsh’s fellow club bowler—and ignoring
the black, though it was patent to the veriest chump that
Marsh had been much more successful against batsmen
of the first class.
Last Monday’s cablegram :
The Japanese Government, in making announcements
in the Diet, expressed the belief that the steps taken for
the removal of the discrimination of Japanese in the alien
legislation by Australia would succeed.
As nothing is being done here, the inference is
that the “steps which are being taken” to force
Australia to admit Japanese are being taken in
England, and that these steps consist of judicious
breathing into the ear of Salisbury and Chamber-
lain. And the Jap Government reckons that suc-
cess is assured. AsSalisbury, Chamberlain and co.,
despite their oily palaver about “consulting the
wishes of the colonies,” are a most high-handed
gang in their dealings with British subjects, while
pitiably servile to the Jap, and as they never con-
sult “ the colonies ” even about things that most
concern them, the Jap statement is less im-
probable than it looks. This country’s bursts of
Jingo fervor have only served to convince Cham-
berlain that it is a place that can be trodden on
with impunity.
Sing hey ! for Sweet Nell of Old Drury—
The dear little Runaway Girl
Who laughs at the impotent fury
Of Jeffries and Rochester’s earl.
She scoots from a Corsican convent,
Despite Lady Castlemaine’s snarls,
Whilst Flipper and Alice
Play “ picks ” in the palace,
She mashes King Kenningham Charles—
She calls him her sire,
And sets him afire—
For Nell is the flame of King Charles.
But Jeffries pretends to discover
That Coodle is writing an ode
Concerning Sweet Nell and a lover,
Who lives in the Blackfriars Road;
So Howard Olivia Vernon *
(Oft named in historical books)
Cries “Odds fish ! and Dammy !
Gee-up, Brother Tammy,
And follow the Lauri from Cook’s :
The hurdles are low,
So over you go,
And follow the Lauri from Cook’s ! ”
Then Winifred Grey and Palotta
In company visit the King :
They tell him the Judge is a plotter, '
And Nell a quite innocent thing.
His Majesty pardons young Fairfax,
The victim of Jeff’s wicked scheme,
And brings down the curtain
By gaily assertin’
That Nelly May Beatty’s “ a dream.”
He rightly declares,
As she goes down the stairs,
That Nell is a vanishing dream.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 11, 1902.
My Dear Arini, —
A vegetarian cafe, with mostly American
vegetables in the window, has commenced busi-
ness in Sydney. It is not by any means the first
of its kind. Eight or nine or ten years ago a
vegetarian restaurant didn’t flourish in George-
street. A few thin, burdened-looking people
dined there daily. It turned into an ordinary
restaurant after a while, and then it lifted the
spectre—as a foreign gentleman, who had forgotten
the idiom about giving up the ghost, once said to
me.
“ Love redeems the world.” I once saw that in
print somewhere, and it recurred to me (Eros
knows why, perhaps ; I don’t) when I read that
the plaintiff in the Cousins-M‘Nevin breach-of-
promise case had presented one of the Elsies of
his acquaintance with an infant pig named
“ Minnie.” Miss M'Nevin’s pet name was Minnie.
Mr. Cousins loved Miss M‘Nevin. He said so
himself. So did Mr. Reid, his counsel. Yet that
piglet trifle went against him heavily with the
jury. If the gift had been a canary bird named
Minnie, poor Richard would probably have got
another £2O added on to the paltry £l5O damages.
The principals in this breach-of-promise case came
from Molong, and were almost unknown in Syd-
ney until they gave it a good laugh on a day last
June. On that day Miss M‘Nevin was to
marry Mr. Cousins, and a number of guests,
including Mon.signor O’Brien and Dr. O’Haran,
and other members of the R.C. hierarchy,
awaited the pair in the church. But the
Voice was not Breathing just then over Molong.
The bride had met another lover, a commercial
traveller named Carr. Him she married a month
later. The wedding took place from Pfahlert’s
Hotel, Sydney ) and the hall urchin in buttons
bore the bride’s train, and principals and guests
made a much-stared-at theatre-party the same
night. Successful wooer is a fresh-faced youth,
said to be even younger than the 27-year-old one
the bride jilted.
Miss MNevin’s mother evidently believed in
keeping her one child “down,” for Minnie,
in earlier days, does not appear to have
been able to meet a man save by stealth.
Judging by the readiness with which she fell
in love and kept both admirers on, she
must have been of an affectionate nature. As
her estate is worth £50,000 there was no lack of
reciprocity. Over one thing Mrs. Carr may
rejoice : Her real age did not come out in court.
One of the counsel said it was 38, the opposition
hazarded it at 50. Love-letters of the Molong
couple were few in number and uninteresting in
substance, and not so ridiculous as the letters of
most engaged couples seem to a third person.
One of the angriest of the breach outsiders must
be the postmistress who was greatly “ shook ” on
the wrong Mr. Right who got left. ’ She probably
didn’t care a penny stamp for him—he no doubt
spoke truly when he said he wrote that in jest.
But juries and loungers are so humorless. When
the Yorks were here, a plain kharkee ranker, who
helped to line the streets during some rainy cere-
mony, wrote to a chum at Gumbough, or some
such place, that the Duchess had taken a great
fancy to him, and had held her umbrella over him
during the pouring rain. Did the Gumboughers
say, “Very feeble joke!” and go on with their
timber-getting and milking? No ! They knocked
off work to talk about the boldness of a recently-
married royal personage thus publicly mashing a
free Australian warrior.
Miss M‘Nevin’s counsel offered to settle the
case with a solatium of £5OO and costs, but
Richard simply scoffed at the offer and fought it
out, only to fare worse.
From Maoriland, a wire from George Smith,
Wilson Barrett’s manager, announces his marriage
to Miss Gertrude Boswell, the plump “boy”
Stephanus in the “ Sign of the Cross.” Miss
Boswell has been with the Barrett co. since she
was a child, and is getting a bit too grown-up-
looking for the role of tortured Christian youth,
which she plays so well. George Smith is a Mel-
bourne man, son of late Washington Smith who
was one of the business managers of Lyster, the
impresario.
Poor, pretty little Ada Willoughby Was so ill
last week that only pluck and quinine got her
through her nightly singing, washing, dancing,
and love-making. A funny little Skye doggie
accompanies her to the theatre every night, sleeps
in her dressing-room, but wakes up every time his
missus comes in to make one of her many changes
of dress. But when her last frock—a pale green
satin with chrysanthemums—is laid ready by the
dresser the Skye sniffs it, shakes himself, and does
not nap again, for he knows the missus and he
have stopped mumming for the night.
Widow Twankey’s Chinese laundry robe in
“ Aladdin ” is well worth study under the opera
glass. It has a raised border of black velvet cats
—large ones, shedding tears. Each cat’s tail is
elaborately curled, and the heads drop pathetically
as the big tear-drops fall. No rats in the laundry
—hence those tears.
“ What to wear at the Coronation.” “ Corona-
tion Coiffures,” “ Coronation Calves” (for spindle-
shanked noblemen and footmen), these are the
matters that now occupy fashionable London to
the exclusion of the war, which it treats as a bore-
some back number. A Coronet has, it seems, a
band of silk across the cavity which the head is
supposed to fill. You will carry it slung on your
wrist until it is time for you to put it on in the
Abbey after the Queen puts on her lid. Many
venerable papers are showing pictures of Corona-
tion Coiffures for Duchesses, Marchionesses and
other grades of peeresses. The faces below these
coiffures are as beautiful as Helen of Troy and as
young as twenty years. No account seems to be
taken of the many peeresses with unfurred skulls
and doddery with age.

Vanning Tables. —The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the Wesley-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Collins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 Hunter-street, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*

* « * *
There has arrived in Victoria the full text of
the decision of Judge Riner, of the Circuit Court of
Colorado, in the suit in which the proprietor of the
Wilfley concentrator sought to restrain the Denver
Engineering Co. from manufacturing and selling the
Cammet concentrating table, on the ground that to do
so would constitute an infringement of the patent rights
held by him. The Judge in finding for the applicant,
declined to recognise the Wilfley table as a mere improve-
ment on existing concentrating tables, holding that the
patentee had, by the use of riffles in the way they were
employed on the table, solved a problem in ore concentra-
tion that had never before been answered. IICalso drew
attention to the fact that the inventor had foreseen that
other ingenious minds might achieve good results by
making various changes in the construction of riffles, anil
therefore he had included in his specification the words
“ I do no not limit the invention to any special construc-
tion of riffle.” The Judge in finding for the complainant,
gave costs against the Denver Engineering Works Co.*

The U.S. War Department lately published a
report of much importance to Australian coal-getters and
shippers—on the coal measures of the Philippines, pre-
pared by the officer in charge of the mining bureau at
Manila. The Cebu and other measures give “ a coal suit-
able for many commercial purposes,” free-burning and of
good steaming value; arid the officer finds “no reason
why it should not compete all through the Orient with
the Japanese and Australian coals so largely used there.”

The Wild Cat Column.
Some revenue matters:
New South Walks (8 months to 28th February).
Revenue (Rents
of Resumed
Area of Sydney Ordinary
not included). Expenditure. Deficit.
1901 £6,910,510 .. £7,127,725 .. £217,215
1902 6,909,335 .. 7,311,148 ... 401,813
Decrease £1175
Increase £183,423 .. £184,598
Or if the See-O’Sullivan Government is given the benefit
of its own indefensible proceedings, and the rents for the
unpaid-for properties in the resumed area of Sydney are
treated as legitimate revenue, which they certainly are
not, the figures result as follows :
Revenue
(Including Rents
of Resumed Ordinary
Area of Sydney). Expenditure. Deficit.
1901 £6,965,026' .'. £7,127,725 .. £162,699
1902 7,085,902 .. 7,311,148 .. 225,246
Increase £120,876 .. £183,423 .. £62,547
Either way the expenditure is increasing in an alarming
fashion, and the new outlays are not accounted for in any
way by the Old Age Pension expenditure. That is far
more than counterbalanced by the fact that the cost of
running the postal, telegraph, and military departments
is now defrayed by the Commonwealth. If things had
been conducted on the same basis as last financial year
the expenditure, despite Old Age Pensions, would have
been much less, for the See-O’Sullivan Ministry charges
its unproductive public works to loans in the mad old
way, and there are no new charges against revenue under
that head.
But it is in the matter of loan expenditure that
the See and O’Sullivan crowd shows at its maddest. This
is the record :
New South Wales Loan Expenditure.
8 months to 8 months to
28th Feb., 28th Feb.,
1901. 1902. Increase.
Ordinary loan ex-
penditure .. £1,765,472 .. £2,522,689 .. £757,217
On Sydney land re-
sumptions .. Nil. .. 590,431 .. 590,431
£1,765,472 £3,113,120 £1,347,648
Hitherto, the worst year’s record in the way of loan ex-
penditure which N.S.W. ever put up was that of Parkes
and M‘Millan, in 1891, when these two amazing financiers
got through £4,750,167, but, if the present Ministry keeps
up anything like the last two months’ rate of outgoings,
it will break even that record very badly. Out of the
£700,872 spent last month, about half may be called
reproductive. The tramway conversions can’t fairly
be called productive, for they are merely abolishing one
productive asset and putting another in its place. Then,
in addition to the above, £755,179 of new debt has been
created in the shape of Treasury bills, and applied to
extinguishing the Martin Place and Centennial Park
Gjydney) suspense accounts, and to making good a certain
bygone misappropriation of sinking-fund moneys made
by the late Ministry. The proceeds of the misappropria-
tion were used as revenue, and the misappropriation is
made good again out of loans. It was resolved by Parlia-
ment that an extra sum should be paid annually out of
revenue to reduce the great mass of Treasury bill debt,
but it has never been paid as yet, and doesn’t seem likely
to be.
New South Wales closed last year with an
admitted shortage of £117,725, and a real one of about
£251,000 inasmuch as the revenue was swelled by the
inclusion of £133,043 received by way of rent from the
unpaid-for properties in the resumed area of Sydney.
This year, with a greatly-increased tariff revenue, it seems
likely to close with an even larger deficit. And it is only
a year or two since the Sydney press was asking, in an
anxious tone, what on earth N.S.W. would do with its
great Federal tariff surplus. The comedy of it all!
* * * *
Queensland shows up a little better this month:
Queensland (8 months to 28th February).
Revenue. Expenditure. Deficit.
1901 .. .. £2,825,930 .. £2,845,617 .. £19,687
Surplus.
1902 .. .. 2,445,418 .. 2,406,838 .. 38,580
Decrease .. £380,512 .. £438,779
This means a general improvement of £58,267, and if it
can be maintained the deficit (as compared to last year’s
monumental shortage of £528,000 odd) should not be
more than £450,000. Supposing it pans out at that
Queensland will again, despite the improvement, have, in
proportion to population, the Biggest Deficiency on
Earth. Loan affairs in Queensland continue to grow
worse. During the past eight months the Philp Ministry
spent £770,004 of other people’s money, an increase of
£38,627 as compared with the corresponding period of
1901. This is at the rate of £1,155,000 per annum, which
would be much the same, proportionately to population,
as if Britain borrowed £100,000,000 abroad in a quiet,
peaceful, ordinary year, to keep things going.

on Ist January, has begun very badly. This is the
showing so far:

No information about ordinary or loan expenditure is to

* » * *
Victoria shows a material improvement so far,
the very scanty information available showing that,
despite the loss of border duties, the revenue for the
eight months to 28th February, 1902, is about £109,000
more than for the corresponding period of last year. As
usual, no expenditure facts are published.
* * » *
As for Westralia, its boom still continues. The
Federal tariff increased its revenue from customs on
foreign imports. It is permitted, unlike the other States,
to still collect its inter-State duties, and it collects them
vigorously ; it purchases enormously from abroad, which
makes its customs revenue large, and it gets the money
to pay for these purchases to a great extent by huge
foreign loans. So far, its record is :
Westralia (8 months to 28th February).
Revenue. Expenditure. Surplus.
1901 ... £2,007,349 £1,997,650 £9699
1902 ... 2,351,508 2,078,325 273,183
Increase £344,159 £80,675 £263,484
If Westralia would decide to build the rest of its Cool-
gurdie Water Supply system out of surplus revenue
instead of out of loans, the shock to the community when
the pipe bursts would be so much the less noticeable.
* * * *
Mr. W. H. Vivian, secretary Sydney Stock
Exchange, having voyaged to Europe, learned things
which he has eagerly communicated to the newspapers.
Here is one of them : “ The public and private in-
debtedness of Australia was so great that a tre-
mendous amount of money was going from here in
interest, and unfortunately was not coming back in
THE SHAPE OP PRIVATE LOANS, OR MONEY DEVOTED TO
private enterprise.” “ Unfortunately was not coming
back in the shape of private loans ! ” Evidently then Mr.
Vivian’s remedy for a loan evil which is draining
Australia of “ a tremendous amount of money ” is more
loans, and a bigger drain of interest. He holds that
“ unfortunately ” we are not getting further into debt
quite so fast as we might do. And yet men with ideas
like that fancy themselves quite competent to run public
affairs.

The reserves consist of reserve-fund, £875,000 ; con-
tingency-fund, £125,000 ; and undivided profit, £24,499.
According to the estimate made a few years ago, there is
still £125,000 to be written off before losses are fully pro-
vided for. At the present rate of progress, and assuming
that no further disasters strike this country, another two
or three years should see the old depreciation wiped off.
The Union Bank’s profits for the six months to
31st August, 1900, were at the rate of £7 11s. 8d. per cent,
per annum on the shareholders’ funds as at the com-
mencement of the half-year—a rate such as very few banks
earn nowadays. It holds £3,613,713 in cash and £1,518,728
in Government and municipal securities, so its position is
even more solid than usual. It has over £14,000,000 of
deposits. And in general it is a fine, solid British insti-
tution, which has extracted large dividends—ranging
from 6 to 36 per cent.—out of this country for many
years, and has been one of the most costly and most
highly respected absentees in Australia’s experience.

The Australian Widows’ Fund Life Assurance
Society show’s a reasonably good result for 1901, and the
figures compare with some previous years as follows :

of Assurance Fund written off to cover losses.
1897 .. 150,602 58,446 209,048 38,060 49,005

This is the largest addition made to the funds since 1894,
and it raises the assurance fund to £1,509,234. In addi-
tion to this there is a suspense account or depreciation
fund of £9324, consisting of sums recovered from the
great writing-down which was effected in 1896. The total
funds are therefore £1,618,558. Also, there is a very
remarkable improvement in the total income.
The expense rate, however—always the worst
feature of the Australian Widows’ Fund—has not im-
proved, but has grown even a trifle worse than before.
The average of late years has been :
Expenses as Compared with Total Income.
1890 .. £19 10 7 percent. 1896 .. £17 13 0 percent.
1891 .. 18 6 0 „ 1897 .. 18 4 0
1892 .. 18 4 0 „ 1898 .. 19 11 1 „
1893 .. 16 7 8 „ 1899 .. 22 0 1 „
1894 .. 17 3 0 „ 1900 .. 22 5 6 „
1895 .. 19 15 8 „ 1901 .. 22 17 6 „
This is not by any means the highest rate in Australasia,
but it is a very high rate, especially if compared with
such offices as the A. M.P. or the Maoriland Government
Life Insurance Department. It is probably inevitable in
a country which has too many life assurance offices in
proportion to population, but, inevitable or not, it is not
a good thing for policy-holders. The increase in business
since 1893 has been bought at great cost, and during the
last six years the increase in the expense rate has been
continuous. And while the expense rate goes up the
rate of interest earned diminishes, as is the experience
with all Australasian offices nowadays. The Australian
Widows’ Fund realised since 1897 as follows:—
Interest Realised on Average Funds.
1897 £4 15s. 7d. per cent. 1900 £4 8s. 8d. per cent.
1898 £412s. Od. „ 1901 £4 6s. 3d.
1899 £4 10s. 9d.
In 1897 the Australian Widows’ Fund had just raised its
average interest rate very heavily by writing down the
book value of its properties in a most liberal fashion, so
that year makes a convenient starting point. Since then
there has been no writing down of values, and the drop
has been enormous. The Society provides for the decline
by reducing its interest estimates from 4 to 3J per cent.
But even at 3£ per cent, there is hardly any larger a
margin than there was at 4 per cent, in 1897. The posi-
tion is safe enough, but already the necessity of reducing
the interest estimates to 3 per cent, looks as if it might
not be far distant. And a good many other offices are in
a similar position. This isn’t specially good for the
bonuses, but that is unavoidable.
The Australian Widows’ Fund, however, does
one thing which looks wholly foolish and disastrous. It
has, as already mentioned, a Suspense Account or
Depreciation Fund of £9324, made up of sums recovered
from the writing-off of 1896. This isn’t much of a pro-
vision for possible losses on about £1,450,000 worth of
investments—it would only cover a depreciation of about
13s. on every £100. Especially it isn’t much in a country
where values are so grossly inflated by the influx of
foreign loans. It would have been a fair thing to increase
this reserve to at least £30,000, and to have made pro-
vision for investing it separately so that it would accumu-
late at compound interest against the next emergency.
But instead this little reserve, as well as the other
profits, is applied to paying bonuses. Apparently
the management has learned absolutely nothing
from the disaster which struck it only five years ago!
There certainly is nothing visibly wrong with the
assets now— but they looked the same way in 1891, and
yet there was something very visibly wrong with them by
1896. And the general position looks distinctly worse
now than it looked in 1891—interest is lower, the margin
between the rate of interest assumed and that actually
earned is very much less, and expenses have increased
heavily. Under these circumstances, there is all the
more need for caution, and it certainly isn’t a time to
scatter the little reserve in bonuses.

The Australian Mutual Investment and Build-
ing Co., Limited (Sydney) made only a small loss in 1901,
for the sufficient reason that there was mighty little left
to lose, except bits of land which are practically urn
saleable and unloseable. The co. will probably make a still
smaller loss in 1902, for the equally unsatisfactory reason
that there is now still less left to lose. The accumulated
deficit has reached £212,050 and if another £20,000 goes
the whole company will be lost, capital, deposits and all.
The proceedings of the past few years have been as
under:
Loss on Loss on Accumulated
Business. Property. Deficit.
1894 £99,562
1895 £5948 £93,176 198,684

1897 1161 7,740 207,853

1899 467 92 211,629

The Australian Mutual Investment and Building Co. owes
£57,058 to shareholders, whose case, of course, is hopeless.
There is property to the nominal value of £19,776 with
which to satisfy nearly £175,000 of liabilities to depositors
and others. But the property, consisting of scraps and
remnants of land at ridiculous boom valuations, appears
to be not easily realisable, and isn’t likely to fetch half
the figure set against it. The depositors received 6d. in
the £ during 1897 ; Id. in the £ in 1898; 3d. in the £ in
1899; Id. in 1900, and Id. in 1901. It looks as if another
2d. or 3d. might be expected, making about 15d. in the
£ on deposits. Interest has wholly ceased for a number
of years. The Australian Mutual Investment and Build-
ing Co. was once a 25 per cent, dividend-paying affair, so
it has been celebrated in more ways than one. Nobody,
not even the new directorate, has ever been able to quite
discover how all the capital and practically all the de-
posits were fooled away. A snowball left in Cooktown
in 1893 could hardly be more completely gone than this
once great concern.
» * * •
William Jamieson, who, as chairman of the
Blythe River Iron Mines, is pushing the movement to set
up iron-making in N.S.W. (a site has been chosen on the
Parramatta River), was one of the Broken Hill “ early
birds,” and most of the time since then has been a director
of the Barrier’s biggest concerns. Jamieson was a sur-
veyor in N.S.W. Lands Department, and came near re-
signing in disgust when ordered out into the Western
wilderness ; but his chief comforted him with the sugges-
tion that it was better to have his exile in his young days
than in his gouty sixties. Jamieson, though then with-
out much knowledge of mining, became first manager of
the Broken Hill; and that opened up a way to his con-
nection with Mt. Lyell.
• * * *
The smart Howell Consolidated G. M. Co.
dropped £10,000 or so in its Junee Reefs (N.S.W.)
spec. The _ desultory discovery of small 3oz. to 4oz.
“ pockets ” in the Dusthole claim kept luring the co. on,
and these pinched out in the end. The Junee Reefs,
which are on the same gold-line as Eurangilly, Temora,
Wyalong, and Yalgogrin, have been worked over 30 years
without success. Traces of gold can be got in a dish of
stuff, taken from anywhere about the district, but only
traces. The same co. has a similar in-and-out mine at
Reno, near Gundagai.

his exes. ; and Syd. Smith, then Minister of Mines,
wouldnlt. . Others just said “Whasseuse?” Westralian
karri and jarrah men don’t drowse like this.

North Central B.H., which, after nine years of
it, was getting pretty tired of phasing “ encouraging
prospects,” even with lead at £17 a ton, broke up when
the metal slumped to £10, though it really didn’t matter
to it whether the price was £17 or 17d. Six months’
suspension has been obtained, presumably to give the
directors time to consider what they will do with the
£1300 they have in hand.
• * • *
Broken Hill, “ratty” on the subject of trading
coupons for some time, has now developed acute mania.
An ’umble person, wanting bread and cheese, started it
going with a few pink and blue vases and a pile of glass
dishes. Then, the disease growing, a co-operative co. was
started to kill the’umble person. Next, the co-op. co.,
showing good profits (something like 200 per cent., it
seems, from the last balance-sheet), a “mutual” co.
started. It doesn’t run a vase and glass dish shop; it
only has an office where you can redeem your coupons for
credit-slips negotiable with any trader on the co.’s list—
and the list takes in even wood-merchants and rent-
collectors. Of course the Co.-op. co. and the original
’umble person respond with bigger bids; and the fool-
public doesn’t yet see that when it demands “ coupons,
please ” with its tin of sardines, it helps to keep three lots
of people in the finest clover-patch they have ever got
into.

of Australia before the inevitable syndicate arises with its
equally inevitable aud smellful demand for a concession.
Also usual promises. It talks largely and, of course

If concessions are to be granted, why should not Sandford

See and his Ministry. As to Lyne’s arrangement—am not

return of that deposit does not spell ‘ repudiation,’ other-

the prophet hopelessly in the rear. Moreover, your
humble servant does not notice any deposit put up this

latter within the * inaccessible ’ distance of a few hundred

Govt. Geologist Brown, of S. Aus., reports on
far-north Tarcoola. The gold won has been worth just a
third of the amount spent on the mines ; and the Adelaide
papers think this a splendid result considering that
the mines are yet in their “ least remunerative stages ”
The paper or the community or the 'company which
expects its mines to be more remunerative the deeper
they get is, undoubtedly, a juggins. Possibly one mine in a
thousand does “improve with depth,” but not more
S. Australian mines certainly haven’t; most of them in-
cluding the Burra and three-fourths of the gold shows and
every single one of its silver shows, have “ improved ” so
much with depth that they have had to shut up Still
Mr. Brown thinks deep sinking at Tarcoola niay be
“ taken in hand with the greatest confidence ” • and the
writer hopes it may, too.

Be the lately-reported discovery of mineral
phosphates in South Australia : 103 tons of the rock have
been sold for reduction to manure. The average value
in phosphate was 61 per cent., with from 2 to 7 per cent
of alumina and iron. The commercial value is not dis-
closed.

Q. Mining Notes: Gympie paid one div. in
January. No. 2 S. Great Eastern seems to be the ray of
sunshine. Ravenswood very dull. Croydon moderately
ditto ; tin field there gasping for water. Croydon’s divs.
over calls for 1901 upwards of £42,000. Up to date this
field yielded £2,300,000. Opalton (Central Q.) in a very
bad way with drought, etc. Kangaroo Hills tin field,
in Ingham district, practically deserted; no water.
Oloncurry gold and copper show gone into liquidation.
That boomed Syndicate Railway hasn’t materialised, and
the milk and honey flow is not on tap. Ebagoolah, in
the far North, is considered a good thing by shrewd
miners; all they want is a railway, a coal seam and a
river. Two or three faint barks from various Mt. Morgan
puplets. Towers still “ bucking up,” and divs. and busi-
ness brisk.

The Phillips River, newest of Westralian fields,
got a battery talking early. The Floater and Maori Queen
have mills running ; and the Harbor View and the Red,
White and Blue have theirs ready. Besides, the Options
Co. has ordered a copper furnace, though it hasn’t too
much money to pay for it; and the Govt, has been asked
to put up a reverberatory. The lodes are a bit irregular,
but carry good stuff ; 280 tons sent East from the Harbor
View gave 23 per cent, copper, loz 15dwt. gold, 6oz.
silver.

Queen Margaret, the Bulong (Westralian) show
which the Broken Hill crowd favored with some of
the crumbs from its table, finishes its 11th half-year with
a credit balance of £5997, after paying £3564 in divs. ;
and as it brought forward £5885 six months ago, the net
result of the half-year’s work is a profit of £3676. When
chairman Harvey Patterson visited the show six months
ago it looked so good that another 10 heads were added to
the battery and other plant was ordered; but the
directors are sorry now, for (in the nice way directors
have of putting it) “ the faces have entered a compara-
tively poor zone.” The manager puts it a little more
plainly : “ The lode is of an extremely patchy character.”
This is between 400 and 600ft. ; but, of course, better
things are expected at 700ft., and if they don’t get them
there they will expect them at 800ft.

LEROY
LIQUEUR
BRANDY
The great mass of the Sydney public dreads
the plague, and yet it experiences a feeling that is
almost disappointment when it comes into town
two or three consecutive mornings and sees the
information on the newspaper bills—“No fresh
cases.” It is a feeling as if an excitement and a
gamble had gone out of life—the sort of feeling
that a member of the Suicide Club might experi-
ence when he found the club-door closed, and a
notice on it stating that the quarterly meeting
was postponed. When the plague lags very badly
a subject of interest has gone out of life, and a
valuable and creepy topic has vanished out of
conversation, and the man who was quite
sure he wasn’t frightened loses his prominence
and sinks to the level of the common herd.
For other people also cease to be frightened,
and the morbid person who found a dark and
brooding thrill in inspecting himself for plague
symptoms takes less interest in his inspection, and
the old muddy flatness settles down over the com-
munity. Any sport at which there is a chance of
somebody being killed is a big draw—of course, a
sport at which there was a certainty of everybody
being killed wouldn’t draw worth a cent. And
while the. plague is so feeble that it only amounts
to a chance of somebody being killed it is also a
big draw, and when at rare intervals the disease
makes a notable hit and sends the germ to the
boundary for four, there is a feeling that ap-
proaches to applause. Of course the feeling
vanishes if that sort of thing happens too often.
Suicide clubs have been found a great success—a
man might belong to one for many years without
drawing.the fatal lot. But a club which proposed
to. hang itself by the neck unanimously on a cer-
tain date would be a bad failure. i\
THE REHEARSAL.
“ Baby peers are to be invited to the King’s coronation,
and each will be placed in charge of a guardian. The
ceremony of the coronation is so intricate and full of
ritual that the Archbishop of Canterbury and the other
high ecclesiastics who are to take prominent parts in the
service have decided to hold private rehearsal services
within the Abbey.” — S.A. Advertiser.
The Haggis Conscience at Ballarat (Vic.)
has been outraged lately by the appearance of
that scandalous person, Prof. Rentoul, in the
pulpit of the leading Calvinistio church of the
Garden City. Many of the supporters of the
kurruk have written to the local Courier about
it, and keep on doing so in a state of loyal frenzy.
It seemed to be thought necessary to boil the
Prof. —always freezingly alluded to as “ this per-
son ” —to assert the loyalty of Ballarat Scotsmen,
and as a guarantee of the warmth of their devo-
tion to the throne and person, and their loathing
of William Wallace and Robert Bruce and other
disgraceful Scotch rebels who broke the first
British empire asunder. It is safer to deny the
Lord in Ballarat than to deny “Judas” Chamber-
lain. .
“South Africa Day ” in Sydney was a cold
an d frosty exhibition, which certainly did no
credit, as a spectacular effect, to its originators.
There were 28 contingent officers and 304 soldiers
of sorts, including 45 veterans. These marched
to the wharf to the braying of two brass bands,
and made for Clontarf. The main feature of the
occasion was the substantial supply of food for
the “distinguished” guests—from G. No-longer-
Republican Dibbs to John See and George
Dry-dog Reid. The soldiers were not ad-
mitted to the feeding part of the entertainment;
their commissioned “comrades” drew the line
there. The politicians and the officers dined
together and made glowing speeches to each other
largely about the valor of the privates, and the
privates, in a neglected state outside, bellowed for
their alleged arrears of pay and didn’t get them.
To all appearance S’Africa Day won’t be a perma-
nent institution. Those who have it in hand don’t
know how to popularise it. “ Official luncheons”
won t serve.
* * * *
“ R. A.” also describes the great function ;
South Africa Day’s procesh to the Quay the other Satur-
day did not strengthen the “ silken bond ” overmuch.
The officahs marched in front, while the degenerate
cohentingenters mouched along behind, like cheap fowls
following an expensive rooster across a stubble paddock.
A few old farm-burners paraded the footpaths, openly
scoffing, and one of them, very drunk, got mixed up with
the drum and fife band. An onlooker went off into a
fit, kicking vigorously, till four rough-handed Samaritans
took hold of him and applied remedies. The band passed,
playing patriotic airs, while the poor smitten devil tried
to bite for “first holt.” Two or three of the farm-
burners from the draggled rear stopped to have a look,
and curse sympathetically. Presently the little gather-
ing melted away, and the first South Africa Day was
perpetuated. There won’t be another—at least I think
not.
* » * *
Banquetting Gov. Havelock, in his recent
tour through Tasmania, one village committee
was horrified.to find at the last moment that not
a solitary waiter had put in an appearance. The
company were all seated before the disaster was
discovered, and the situation didn’t take long to
grow painful. The Governor pretended not to be
in the slightest hurry, and did his level best to
make it appear that he was quite used to making
a hasty meal off an empty plate. But the com-
mittee blushed to its boots, and the secretary
froze to his seat. Still the waiters came not.
Just before the whole congregation started to yell
to relieve its feelings, a couple of hairy inhabitants
rose from the table with a sigh, took off their
coats, and rushed the viands round with terrible
earnestness. Their example was followed by
others, and the situation was saved. A local big-
wig, whose politics do not coincide with those of
the village committee’s secretary, and who had
been left off the list of invitations, had previously
engaged all the available waiters. They do that
sort of thing in Tasmania to show their brother-
liness.

Those two little girls of Melbourne, aged
respectively 15 and 14, sent to the industrial school
for burglary, exhibit woman’s remarkable capacity
for developing along the line of greatest resistance.
The innocents cheerfully confessed to 14 burglaries.
The plan of Maria and Victoria was to climb the
fence behind some shop. Then Maria gave
Victoria a back, and Victoria stood on Maria and
broke her way in through a window with a rock,
and opened doors from the inside. They must
have been at the game some time, and lots of wit-
nesses, including parents on both sides, testified to
the modest demeanor ahd apparent ingenuousness
of Maria and Victoria. This kind of dear child,
and the other kind of dear child —the one that has
a quaint longing to hang a man—always does look
doe-like and innocent. In fact, the time will come
when a girl will be liable to arrest for looking
unreasonably innocent in a public place.
Irascible Dr. Bakewell, who proses in
the press on every conceivable topic under
the sun, recently launched a fiery diatribe
against Maori art at Auckland (M.L.) Institute.
He inveighed against the spending of £lOOO in
one year on Maori curios—“the most hideous
objects produced by human beings on this earth.”
There was no beauty in them, proceeded the
rancorous critic, not even a rude commencement
of a higher art; Maoris were a set of savages
who, in the 20th century, hadn’t even passed the
stone age, and were imperfect at that. Maori
canoes and things were simply barbarous —hideous
pieces of wood chopped about. Such monstrosi-
ties he would rather have buried. A morbid
taste was growing up in Auckland for accumulat-
ing ugly, and even obscene, objects, because they
belonged to the Maoris. He was sorry to hear
that the brown race was on the increase, because
it represented a very low type indeed. This is
the most bracing language that the Maori has
heard about himself for a long while.

Some men have hard
luck (this may start a
hard luck competition).
In a back-country town
dwelt a very pious man,
who taught in the Sun-
day-school and did all a
pious man should. But
one night the Devil
tempted him, and he fell,
and for that night only his
lonely home was cheered
with a woman’s presence,
and on that night, of all nights of the year, a great
flood arose, and finally he and The Other had to
take refuge on the roof. There the wretched pair
awaited the garish light of day, when a boat took
them off. When it reached dry land the whole
township, including the parson, the choir, and the
vestry, were there to welcome that saved brother,
but when they saw the unsaved sister !!! h

One Ronald Gray, an English artist in
black and white, who lately did a trot around
Australia, has been saying, in the Morning Post,
that “ Melbourne and Sydney have spent thou-
sands of pounds in collections which would not
fetch as many hundreds under the hammer. They
are apparently happy in the possession of some of
the worst pictures I have ever seen hung in an
important gallery.” The supercilious visitor
seems to have mistaken silence for happiness.
Herkomer’s ghastly “portrait” (it cost £800) of
Queen Victoria at Melb. Nat. Gallery makes no-
body happy, although nobody thus far has heaved
a brick at it.
WHAT MAY HAPPEN TO A TALL
GIRL IN AUSTRALIA.
HE: “ May I have the pleashah?”
A N.S.W. Judge lately mitigated a con-
victed man’s sentence “ because he was useful to
the detectives, to whom he occasionally gave
information.” He was a criminal of the meanest
order, who worked “on the double cross; also,
he was a savage ruffian, and his present offence
was to break off the neck of a bottle and dig the
jagged edges into a man’s face. Sydney D.T.
suggests to the Judge that it would be better to
pay such people in cash for their services to the
detectives, and hold them responsible in full for
their brutal crimes. Meanwhile, the offender
doubtless feels grateful to his ,’Onah for publicly
mentioning that he is a police spy.
EAST AND WEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
East of the Sun lies the world in a swoon,
Panting and parched ’neath the burning caress,
Faint with the passionate ardors of noon,
East of the Sun lies the world in distress.
West of the Moon sleeps the pale, weary world,
Heavy the eyes that the moon-god has kissed.
Dreamless as death, and with dewdrops impearled,
West of the Moon in the shadows and mist.
M.N.
Prayers for Rain.
The Ball no Question makes of Ayes and Noes,
But Right or Left, as strikes the Player, goes ;
And He that tossed Thee down into the Field,
He knows about it all—He knows—He knows !
The Moving Finger writes; and, having writ,
Moves on ; nor all thy Piety nor Wit
Shall lure It back to Cancel half a Line,
Nor all thy Tears wash out a Word of it.
And that inverted Bowl we call The Sky,
Whereunder, crawling coop’t, we live and die,
Lift not thy hands to It for help, for It
Rolls impotently on as Thou or I.
Omar Khayyam (illustration by Herbert Cole).
John Lane, Publisher.

MERRY HEARTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
All mirth is for the merry hearts ;
All Freedom for the free ;
Who loves Life’s melancholy parts
Shall melancholy be;
In being fearful, mean, or brave
No one is Circumstance's slave;
Thy spirit maketh thee.
There’s joy enough this world and after,
Whatever paths we run,
To fill our hearts with lazy laughter,
Turning our woe to fun.
Whatever you may think it worth
You never saw a greener earth
Nor knew a happier one.
Sadness is but a bended head
That looks with grief on pain;
For do but lift it up instead
And you see Heaven again !
The lamest laughter-loving soul,
Enfranchised, reaches to the goal:
Sorrow alone is vain.

The most curious feature about the Port
Adelaide fiighway robbery case was Flynn’s alibi.
After 17 witnesses had sworn that he was in a cer-
tain place, miles away from the Port, the Crown
produced a letter written by him to a friend,
reminding her that he was in another (and quite
different) place, and asking her to swear to that.
Which shows that a prisoner should never try to
arrange a defence independent of his lawyer. The
jury found both accused guilty.
About Lawson’s guilt, nobody seemed to enter-
tain a doubt, and, when the verdict was given, he
admitted it, but protested that the assembled police
(whom he cheerfully addressed as “ sons of cows ”)
were aware of Flynn’s innocence. The man was
evidently excited, and might have said something
worth hearing, but the Chief Justice promptly
adjourned. It was then 10.30 p.m. on Saturday,
and the jury had taken from 8.10 to consider.
Three days later, Lawson was allowed to make a
long statement, in which he claimed Sparks, the
long-lost Pentridge escapee, as his fellow-robber,
and asked for the longest possible sentence, for
having been such a cur as not to speak before. He
got 20 years. Flynn’s case was adjourned for con-
sideration. One thing certain is that Myles Flynn
was not the man the detectives wanted to convict
at the start ; but so many witnesses swore to him
as the second man in the butcher’s clothes that the
police dropped their own suspect and froze on to
Flynn, who, as a “ pal ” of Lawson’s, had been
arrested for safety’s sake.

Solemn letter to Sydney News :
Sir, —As it has now become abundantly manifest that
the recent prayers of the faithful have availed nothing in
securing the much-desired rain, would it not be well for
Premier See to carefully consider the advisability of
declaring another, and, if necessary, a third public
holiday, for the purposes of prayer, so'that there may be
no doubt that the supplications will reach the fountain
head ? If Mr. See will read 1 Kings, 19 chapter, verse 27,
he will appreciate the value of this suggestion. Elijah
said to the prophet of Baal: “ Cry aloud, for he is a god ;
either he is talking, or he is pursuing, or he is on a
journey, or, peradventure, he sleepeth, and must be
awaked.” Singularly appropriate lines at this juncture 1
—Yours, etc.,
Rain is bound to come in the long run if Sydney
takes enougli holidays. Also, it is bound to come
whether or no.

“ T.W.”: Various Ballarat policemen
were called on the other day to give details of the
punishment they had awarded to an ill-advised
person who resisted arrest, and called them
names. One constable admitted that the offender
had been given “a smack in the face, but the
smack wouldn’t hurt a mosquito.” The same in-
offensive “ trap ” went on to say that his vie
that is to say, prisoner—tried to bolt out of the
watch-house (after the attempt had been made to
brush off the mosquito, of course), and Constable
Hayes chased him, with the result that they both
fell heavily in the yard. Then the other genial
individual took a hand again, and “hit the
prisoner three times, but only with the open
hand.” Apropos whereof the writer has a memory
of a night in a Sydney lockup, when three
or four jaded and cadaverous eighteen-year-olds
of the drunk-on-the-stnell-of-a-cork class were
rounded up for damaging a policeman in Hyde
Park. Over a score of Roberts were at the lockup
for the reception, and when the shivering weeds
had been formally charged, they were hurled
through an iron door into the cells, and the whole
contingent of indignant police swarmed in after
them. In the midst of what ensued one battered
desperado fell up against the iron door and
partially opened it, but he was promptly dragged
back and knelt on by three heavy myrmidons,
who proceeded to brush a mosquito off him.
There is no possible doubt that they got the
mosquito, off. And when I read about that
Ballarat policeman who reckons that he didn’t
hit hard enough to damage the mosquito. I
recognised the fact that it is a degenerate cop who
pervades our streets in these latter days.

The inquiry into certain charges preferred
against Rev. Father Curran, in his capacity as an
instructor at Sydney Technical College, appears
not to have yet closed. The Public Service Board
met on the subject last week, and is understood
to have had a very interesting sitting.
An elderly Niobe, in a cream gown with
black lace embellishments and a drab toque, has
just left off adorning the Appeal Court in Mel-
bourne. She sought to uphold a maintenance order
against her present partner, Pehanegay (no less)
Don Hendrick. The lady’s marriage ventures had
been frequent and, she alleged, mostly unlucky.
She started with one Saunders, in England, when
she was 15. Saunders seems to have faded away
indefinitely, and died some 17 years ago. But
the lady consoled herself in the meantime. First
with Bryers, a gentleman who served her family
with meat in England. Bryers, she said, married
her for her money, and ran through £lOOO in less
than no time. He seems to have wandered away
after a strange goddess, and to have disappeared
for a great space. Anyhow, she last saw him in
Bourke-st. 30 years ago, with a lady whose looks
exceeded her virtue. Bryers appears to have been
a bad egg, for, at the recollection of his
villainy, his relict wept copiously and entertained
the court with a long dissertation on his sins.
Next she married Crossley in 1888. Crossley got
an extended engagement breaking stones for the
Government, at rations only, and that was the
last she had seen of him. Here the semi-relict
wanted to tell the Court all about Crossley, but
was providentially choked off. The last dip in
the lucky-bag brought up Pehanegay Don Hen-
drick. Niobe assured the Court that words
failed to picture “his ” badness. As the weepful
lady was dodging about between Bryers and
Saunders and Crossley and Hendrick nobody
seemed to know which “ him " it was, but mostly
it was Bryers. Bryers must certainly have been
an exceptionally bad egg. Then a witness came
along who said he had witnessed both marriages
with Bryers—the lady having married that repro-
bate twice over to make quite sure she had got
him—and also that the gentleman died but two
years ago. Just there a staggered Court said it
didn’t see where Pehanegay Don Hendrick came
in, if he came in at all, but it would go home and
put a wet towel round its head and consider the
matter.

The latest gory Yarra-bank happening has
points out of the common. One Michael Lynch
casually strolled into Carlton police-station in the
small hours and stated that he had cut a lady’s
throat down the road somewhere, and threw out
a general sort of invitation to the company to
follow him and see the exhibit. He then led the
way to the entrance of the Melbourne General
Cemetery, Carlton, where the woman was found
with her throat cut almost from ear to ear.
Further than that he had done it, the self-accused
could give no particulars. He stated that on
leaving home he had put his razor in his pocket,
with the intention of cutting his own throat. But
(after drinking a bottle of beer) he met “ his girl,”
and they went for a walk in the cemetery instead.
They were sitting on a seat on the main drive
when, as accused put it, “ the thing happened.”
The police believe that Lynch, wno had been
suffering from delirium tremens, “became strange
in his head,” and, having committed the butchery,
fell into a stupor for several hours and only knew
of his deed when he came to. But Michael can’t
help the theory at all. To him the dead body of
his best girl just “ happened.”

lish Act forbids

Various ingenious

There were some mysterious points about
the death of Jessie Shallcross, Reuben Newbigen
and a dog, whose bodies were found lying under a
hedge in a remote Melbourne suburb. All three
had succumbed to an overdose of strychnine. On
the man’s breast was a pencilled note saying he
had taken his own life, as he was better dead ; on
the girl’s, a similar epistle, stating she had gone
to the Hereafter “to avoid bringing shame on her
family and friends.” It looked like a case of
mutual suicide, until Dr. Neild deposed that the
girl was perfectly chaste, and there was no
“ shame ”of that sort in the case at all. Several
witnesses stated that, though the two had “kept
company ” for a while, the morbid Reuben was
distasteful to the gay, light-hearted Jessie, and
she had tried to get rid of him; yet she had gone
with him, on the Tuesday, from Carlton by rail to
Camberwell, thence three miles out into the
country, to the paddock where their bodies were
found. There were no signs of a struggle, and
apparently they had tried the poison on the dog
first. They had taken return railway tickets.
Death occurred on Tuesday night, but their bodies
weren’t found till Friday morning. Nobody
seems to have made any remark about the girl’s
disappearance; and nobody at the inquest could
say whose was the dog or where it came from ; in
fact, nobody seemed to know anything at all. It
is part of the Melbourne plot against N.S. Wales
that, in the southern city, people seem to die with
a lot more mystery than in Sydney.
Plague-smitten Sydney has the consolation
of knowing that Melb. undertakers are complain-
ing bitterly of the badness of business. “ Give us
this day our daily dead.”
* * * *
A venerable gentleman, who rides daily
into Sydney by tram, neither reads nor talks. He
spends his time mentally calculating how much he
would advance, were he still in business, on the
clothes, watches, chains, and other property of his
fellow passengers. His verdict is that Sydney has
fallen away sadly in its clothes during the last
dozen years.
In future, domestic garbage is to be col-
lected in Sydney by night, and Mayor Hughes,
to ensure a minimum of noise, is arranging to
haye the dirt-carts rubber-tyred. But as the loud
and incessant talk of the typical garbage-man is
as bad a trouble to the would-be sleeper as the
rumble of his cart, it is to be hoped Mayor Hughes
will also rubber-tyre the tongues of the Town Hall
pilgrims.

Melbourne Hospital committee Has duly
inquired into itself for sending a delirious patient
to the gaol hospital, and has promptly acquitted
itself without a stain on its character. The
patient, it transpired, was imprisoned on a charge
of “insulting behaviour.” Charging a delirious
fever-patient witli insulting behaviour is an idea
which in comic opera would be worth £52 10s. In
the management of a hospital it should be worth a
few indignant sackings,

THE NOTE RAT.— "ACTEN.
ME WITH THE CHEESE.
OLD GOT.
The editor-compositor-canvasser on a way-
back educational medium writes up the “grand
show” made by the new butcher’s shop down
street: —
The verandah was tastefully decorated with greens, in
the centre of which was placed a photo, of the late Queen,
calling up sweet reminiscences of days gone by. Amidst
the decorations was hooked on what we might honestly
term the golden beef and silver mutton, for such it looked
while glistening in its own fat, especially the lambs. In
the centre of this was pork of all sorts and sizes down to
lovely young suckers in splendid condition. Entwined
about these were sausages of every description, thus
making the show complete. . . . Over the whole scene
the Union Jack was waving.
And all this for a two-inch standing advt., cost to
be taken out in meat!
A RAISON D’ETRE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
God made the roses, white and red,
“ They surely are most sweet," she said.
God made the glory of the light—
Most beautiful it was, and bright.
He made the mountains and the trees,
Bright birds He set, like gems, in these 1
Unsatisfied He was, withal,
And made sweet sounds and musical.
And yet He longed, His soul to please,
A form transcending all of these.
And so made thee, a form divine,
God’s own ideal, O love of mine !
THE WANDERER.
MY CURSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Night-Winds shriek like consciences oppressed
And flashlights of the Past go flitting by,
Of all the throng that will not grant me rest,
One stays behind and haunts my sleepless eye.
“ A man—a woman—whispering fearful Love,
Dark bushes round; the moonbeams slanting
through
Like fingers that might clutch the two apart—
Her passion—glinting eyes like sunlit dew,
Cold selfishness concealed within his heart.”
And more ! Oh, Curse that binds my soul in bars!
That makes an aching Hell my nightly doom !
“ A woman sobbing—prone—’neath naked stars l
A lover, slinking, dingo-like, through gloom."
