All Drapers Everywhere^
That crown that we hear so much about is
throwing its glittering influences over most every-
thing m this Empiah. The most swagger color
for a winter gown is royal orimson, ticketed
coronation,’ whereat brunettes will rejoice, for
the dark beauties have had a streak of bad luck
lately with so much black and purple about. The
crown itself, in the official badges of the R.N is
a brand-new shape. The old crown, in Queen
Victoria s day, was a depressed little head-piece
with a dent in the top, like a bashed-in boxer hat,
but the new badge shows the ‘ Tudor ’ crown, an
inflated and haughty-looking object with much
red velvet visible, and pretending to emeralds and
rubies on the brim, c '
Talking of love letters written for publication :
From the mass of sickening stuff, local and
imported, there stands out of the ruck one book
(imported) smart and amusing, “When Charlie
was Away.” Charlie is the writer’s husband, and
her letters are addressed to her man cousin. Of a
play with which she is dissatisfied she writes : ‘ ‘ An
epigram ought to spring from the situation like a
hjy * rom th® bulb, but all the smart sayings in
this play are sewn on like sequins. They glitter,
but you can see the stitch.” And again: “We
are told that men are devils. They are not.
Resist the devil and he will flee from you. Don’t
resist a man. and he will flee quicker than any
d®vil>” _ Writing of her husband she says:
Charlie never made love to me. The third day
after we were engaged he read Macaulay to me.
I sat on one end of the sofa dying to be kissed, and
he read me about the Bloody Assizes. I wanted
to lay my head on his shoulder, and lie kept telling
me how Monmouth laid his head on the block.”
Burgeon Thomas Fitzgerald passed through
Sydney last week looking none the worse for his
labors at the front (and his subsequent pleasure
trip). He was a great favorite with the Brasseys,
and was always called in when Governor Brassey
fell off things. There was no one in Melbourne
could repair the part of him that Brassey fell on
to as Fitzgerald could. As he is very fond of dogs
he must be a good sort. Often of a Sunday when
he yearns for a morning in an arm-chair he goes
fora long walk just to please the dogs, who sit
round him asking him with paws and eyes to
come. His wife died of consumption many years
ago, and Rumor has grown tired of remarrying
him to smart widows and brainy passd spinsters.
Latest portraits of Amy Castles make her
rather.Dutoh-faced. Her hair-dressing is at fault,
and aids the Queen-of-Holland effect. Round-
faced Edna May wears her “glory” in the same
fashion, but her expression (expression has to be
reckoned with in ooiffing) of demure gravity is so
different from Amy’s jolly, good-natured aspect.
By the way, what can be the cause, and whence
the source, of the disparaging newspaper pars,
that are cropping; up in Australia since Amy
Castles’ Australasian tour was announced ? All
the metropolitan press of London, dailies and
weeklies, hailed her after her first concert with
scarce an “if ” or a “ but” as a risen star. The
Times spoke of her as one whose studies were to turn
her into one of the greatest singers of the world.
Her execution of florid passages ” (invocation
from “ Les Pecheurs de Perles ” under
criticism) “was accurate and brilliant, the high
notes up to D. in alt ringing out with splendid
power and sweetness.” Same paper, in its sum-
mary of the year, said, “To Miss Amy Castles,
an Australian, the highest honors are likely to fall,
since in addition to a lovely voice of Melba-like
purity the singer has modesty and a capacity
which are all too rare in debutantes.”
Most of the critics counselled further study.
None of them said the girl was a finished singer,
and she makes no pretence of coming out here as
such. But one would think from those paragraph-
pricks that some “ London correspondent ” was
an £fT that Amy should have the front to come
out here before she was fat and forty-five, know-
ing her art perfectly, but showing where the file
of time had rasped its music. An ex-Melbourne
woman writer now in London wanted to “run”
Amy in that city, and is now most inkily angry
that the damsel prefers another runner and the
new world—which to Amy is the old.
S.M. Herald’s “fashionable” marriages are
rather entertaining. It has taken to chronicling
the weddings of persons of whom we never heard
before under the title of “ fashionable” in type as
big as it will use when it announces the capture of
De Wet. And the people of whom we have heard
are very sore about it. If the marriage of Jane
Smith, tenth daughter of Mr. Smith, of Sydney,
to Mr. Brown, of London, be a fashionable mar-
riage, how shall a marriage of, say, a State
Governor’s daughter and (perhaps) the son of a
belting earl be announced ? (An earl was charged
at a London police court with belting his wife,
lately).
Charitable and philanthropic Mr. J. S. Harrison
dropped dead last week whilst working in his
flower-garden. He was well-known in financial
circles, and had been auditor to the Bank of
N.S.W., and was connected with the firm of
Harrison, Jones and Devlin. But he was chiefly
known to the public through his long connection
with all sorts of charitable institutions.
A friend sends me a London paper containing a
few pictorial examples of Mrs. George Fitz-
George’s mode of rejuvenating the middle-aged
and imparting the secret of eternal youth to the
young. Mrs. George Fitz George is a daughter-in-
law of the Duke of Cambridge, and she has opened
an Institute of Beauty, an offshoot of the parent
house in Paris, only she labels her treatment the
Georgian method. Her own portrait shows her
as a very pretty young woman. The lady patient
who is undergoing treatment for a double chin
looks as if ready for electrocution. If you wear
the india-rubber collar and mask for one hour
daily for eight days your double chin will
disappear, and you may have a neck like those
seen in our grandmothers’ books of beauty. The
bandage that cures wrinkled foreheads is a thing
with strapsand buckles,and when in position looks
exactly like an instrument the Inquisition used
to squeeze secrets out of its victims. And under
the Georgian method roundness is restored to.
hollow cheeks by the wearing of what looks like a
patty-pan over the time-excavations. And, as for
noses, if you don’t like your Napoleonic nose, or
your snub, you can have it altered to the “style
piquant.” But at this one’s faith falters; I
cannot imagine Private Secretary Wallington’s or
Dean O’Haran’s noses lending themselves to
“ piquant ” alteration, no matter how often those
gentlemen sat in the institute of beauty. As for
obesity, the Georgian method cures that in two
shakes of a Manx cat’s tail. There is room in
Sydney for a branch of this beneficent institution,
for in this city you see more fat and double-
chinned persons than in other cities of the
Commonwealth. The climate destroys the desire
for exercise, but does not lessen the appetite.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“One of the peculiarities of the Australian is
his desire to drag his Governor all over the
country at the slightest provocation. Lately
a narrow-gauge railway was opened from Colac to
Beech Forest, and accordingly new State-
Governor Clarke was dragged out of bed at 6 a.m.
and hurried over 140 miles by a special train.
Arriving at the terminus of the new line, the
party had to alight on the permanent way, wade
through two or three hundred yards of slush in
blinding rain, and sit down at luncheon under a
marquee that proved not to be water-tight. The
spectacle of a damp representative of Royalty
chasing a wing of turkey round a plate full of
rain, and trying at the same time to keep the
water from running down the back of his neck,
never struck anybody as strange, and nobody even
laughed when his varnished chair became firmly
attached to his damp trousers when he rose to
make a speech. After the luncheon, the local
residents hauled the unfortunate representative
of the Crown out into the rain to see a big tree,
felled, and when the mighty stick came down it
raised a shower of mud that overwhelmed the whole
official party and left Gov. Clarke’s vice-regal
countenance encased in a mask of mud. Then
H.Ex. told the Beech Foresters how glad he was
to have had an opportunity of seeing their beauti-
ful district, and went back to the train to spend
three hours in wet clothes on the return journey.
Let us not be too hard on the imported Governor.
He has to graft for his living sometimes.
“All the women enthusiasts at the opening of
the Polo Tournament on Monday (3rd) sported
red and green gamps. Rain-showers periodically
sent the timid in under tarpaulin cover, and
played a tarantelle on the ’brellas that braved the
downpour. It was a dull, dark-habited crowd
that damped itself in honor of the ‘ muddied oafs ’
at the goal. The green crowd waved its verdant
gamp for Geelong, and the blood-stained red
umbrellas waved for Melbourne. Lismore and
Colac had less-defined supporters, with umbrellas
of no color in particular. The surprise of
the day was reserved for afternoon tea, when
from a hansom an alien poloist appeared
with a golden-haired dazzler, who rustled with
such a wealth of black silk pettiooats and silk-
lined costume that the whole tent staggered and
trembled on its circumspect pegs.
“ Wednesday’s continuation of the Tourney was
not only dry but sunny. The Moonee Valley
course fairly bristled with activity. Toorak
thoroughbreds sent the dust of the long drive
whirling to the blue sky. Mrs. Norman Bayles
drove her flash dog-cart. The L. S. Maekinnons
were the only ones to tool a four-in-hand and raise
a bugle-blast. Lady Hutton, accompanied by
Captain Dangar, made a dignified entrance. The
golden-haired, square-backed dame achieved a
good effect in a biscuit voile with matronly re-
minders and sobering black embellishments. A
hat and gingham repeated the biscuit and black
tones. Captain Dangar is the fat lion of the hour.
“Mrs. Roy Greene, in a spotless white muslin,
many coral strings, and a hat of smart build, went
near to taking the ‘ bun ’—if that victual hadn’t
been washed away in the first day’s rain. Miss
Ethel Guthrie, who has been doing the cricket
season to the detriment of her complexion,
adorned the premises in white linen with
severe clerical stole-ends, or tabs, and with
a twist-loaf hair arrangement on the neck
Miss Anderson, the dimpled sister of Mrs. E.
Manifold, also wore her hair neck-ways. It’s not
a becoming style. Mrs. Pursey Chirnside was too
neat and tape-measured in her grey outfit and deli-
cate blue toque ; her style begins to border on
shop-girl neatness,
“ Mrs. Tom Fairbairn looked like a dishevelled
Christmas doll. But her troubles as long as things
are humming. Miss Hilda Cumming reflected on
Nature’s bounty in blue, and a back-built hat,
showing one large, solitary pink rose on the crown.
A pink, silk goffered petticoat seemed of more
importance than the blue linen skirt which was
hoisted at half-mast all day. Miss Armytage
wore her fair thatch in voluminous puffs, on the
topmost mast of which sat an erratic recalcitrant
hat. Although the teams fought for the Madden
Cup, none of the family watched the fight.
“ The ‘ World of Flutterers ’ went disconsolately
to the races on Tuesday, because an uncertain sky
of threatening greyness tabooed the brightest rai-
ment, and the autumn frock, which it impera-
tively called for, was not in readiness. The most
common compromise was a trellised muslin and a
three-quarter cloak. Miss Muriel Williams ap-
E eared on the terrace like a ship in full sail—all
er sheeny black and white bull’s-eye rigging
coruscating in the gradually-brightening noon.
A black hat of shady character (this literally) was
set veil-less above a brown rim of tresses. Another
showy figure was Mrs. Harry Levy, from Plague-
ville. The rather captivating and confident young
dame seemed to set a high value on the hang of
her black voile skirt with its velvet ribs, the swing
of her unlaced figure, and the poise of her pink-
millinered head.
“ The fair Palotta had an hour of shining con-
quests on this Tuesday at Flemington. Coming
in tow of the broad-shouldered Surgeon O’Hara
sand his pale background of a spouse, Grace, in her
Autumn rig-out of chocolate-cloth and saucer hat,
blue-rimmed, soon starred it on the rather unin-
teresting Terrace. An interesting quartette at
one time on the Flemington flags were Nellie
Stewart, Manager Musgrove, George Lauri, and
Carrie Moore. * Sweet Nell ’ struck a note of her
own in dove-grey, a dressing-jacket affair over a
trained skirt, and a grey hat with a corrugated
rim and a flight of sea-gulls.
“The most persistent admirers of Carrie Mnore
must admit that the little one is more fit for a
health-resort than for the stage. She wears her
own complexion by day, and that is sallow, or
rather of a pale coffee-color. It doesn’t boast a
streak of red, even in the lips. The eyes in day-
light lack lustre. The face is thin and old. Clever
Carrie is over-doing it. She hasn’t an inch of
flesh to spare above the covering of her bones, and
she really ought to take a rest. People had to
take a second look before they could believe that
the delicate wisp of a thing that went round with
smug-looking Lauri was the popular Carrie, of
Her Majesty’s. She was clothed tiredly, too, and a
strawberry-pink felt hat on her badly-dressed head
was the least-becoming shade she could have
chosen.
“The Gymkhana crowd at Moonee Valley, on
Friday, steamed in the face of a north wind, but
the sound of revelry ran high and frockers fought
strenuously for leadership. Lady Clarke came with-
out the spruce Guv’nor, and her pleasant smiles
were spilt over the whole gathering. A modest
black and white foulard with cheap lace trim-
mings, a net ruff, and a black feather hat scratched
the wife of the Guv’nor for the dress race. Miss
Sydenham Clarke had not a ghost of a chance
either in the contest. Her unpretentious green
Japanese silk frock was of the simplestdescription.
Hows of green sea-shells were her only ornamenta-
tion and a toque that has seen much active service
was in keeping with the somewhat infantile attire.
A much handsomer study in black and white was
Lady Hutton’s rich gown, touches of lettuce
green adding a cool effect. A wide-brimmed hat
was filled in tastefully with green, black and white.
Another noteworthy person—John Forrest’s wife
—furnished a contrast in the crude blending of
heliotrope and pink in her dress, and the glaring
lilac hat that called further attention to the color
disaster.
“ Their Superior Exes., the Hopetouns, returned
to the public walks of Melbourne’s life on
Monday (10th), when they put in the afternoon at
the Kooyong Polo Show, and the evening at the
Mayoral shivoo. Sassiety puts on an extra f. ill
or two, and crimps and manicures a point more
when the greater Exes are on the scene. Not
that all the crimps or curlings in the world would
count an iota in the regardless eyes of the re-
frigerating Countess.
“ You ought to know the latest style in boots.
Perhaps you are aware that the one-toed is dead,
and the specimens that adorn the boot-shop win-
dows now would comfortably allow at least three
toes inside their points. Even women are having
broader toe-caps, and, when they appear in san-
dals, pancakes aren’t a circumstance to their feet
for flatness. Sandals, however, are trippy, slippy
things ; and a still newer notion is on sale now,
a kind of shoe with criss-cross leather-straps for
uppers, called ‘chulpies.’
“ Mrs. George Chirnside, who, a few weeks ago,
was good enough to confer a daughter on this
sparsely-populated region, made a graceful reap-
pearance in a smart black skirt and short sac
jacket of tucked white glace silk—the newest of
its kind. A flat black-and-white hat was set
cunningly on large waves of fair hair. For once,
Miss Iluby Madden looked ‘off color,’ —the
summer has left her less plump, less rosy,
less dimpled. Sister Lesly, in blue, and
Mrs. Osborne, in green linen, formed the
Madden batch. Col. Campbell was in com-
mand. Conspicuous about his dress was a
Paddy-green tie. One of the daintiest of small
pocket-edition women was a young squatteress
m a cream skirt, heavily piped about the
hips, and a heliotrope muslin blouse innocently
buttoned up the back. O, these matrons that are
buttoned up the back! Mrs. Willie Manifold
was the bonniest of the Western district convoy,
in white muslin and a vivid green hat.
“Mr. George Lorimer was the hero of the
gymkhana day. He won three trophies—or
rather one, two going to his racy wife. The Lorimer
pair are of the horse, horsey. Once Mrs. Lorimer
was mannish, hard in the wrist and mouth, and
addicted to dickies, tailor-built suits and straw
deckers. Then, one day, the small still voice of
Wisdom spoke, and she became Womanish (this
is not a Pale Pill advt.), took a healthy interest in
frills and furbelows, and fell into line with the
rest of smart Womanhood. Mrs. Lorimer’s chief
prize was for manufacturing the funniest dummy
of the day. In the obstacle race of the day, Jim
Osborne, the well-worn trophy-taker, fell in the
ruck for once. He was burdened with ‘ Sweet
Nell, ’ and was so occupied in keeping her on the
neck of his mount, that he lost pace and lowered
his record. ‘ Sweet Nell’s’ white-stockinged legs
were a cruel libel on the queen of comedy.
“ A feature of the gymkhana show was the
number of girls who went about snap-shotting
the polo players and their dummies. These smart
girls have no humor. They went about “taking”
tb# perspiring poloists, while some of the best
‘ mixed pairs ’ going were left unfocussed in the
quiet rear of the tents.
“The last silks and roses of the declining
summer bloomed on the Flemington terrace last
Saturday. It was a balmy day, just sufficiently
breezy to blow out the chiffons and laces. The
meeting was Governor-less, but great in size for
the season of falling leaves. Mrs. Ernest Raleigh,
who has rung the most changes in frocks this car-
nival, was betimes with a brown and pompadour
mixture, the brown skirt trimmed over the hips
with diamond-shaped patches of velvet, the top
part made of strips of silk and treacle-colored lace
—something new and strange in autumn trim-
mings. A hop-green velvet hat struck a note of
contrast with this dress of the day. Mrs. Lawson
stepped out with Rupert, and showed one of the
smartest figures and frocks of the day—a black
voile, saved from melancholy by piping of blue.
More blue stood by the head-rigging.
“ The Madden girls were nowhere in the con-
test. Mrs. Harry Osborne put on 20 years to her
age with a large flowered middle-aged blue
foulard. It was a sacrilege of fashion. Mrs.
Frank Stephen, back from summering somewhere,
was up to the tick of the clock in a black and
white check, tabbed and stitched over the hip
line, finished with a ruff, and having for a top-
rung a knowing toque of blue with a large soft
pink bow astride the crown. Miss Hilda Cuin-
ming was a pattern of pleasant comeliness in pale-
green foulard, a feature of it being flapping shoul-
der revers of a rose-patterned chiffon. Grace
Palotta, in tow of matured Othello Kiddle (father
of the frocky Rita Kiddle) and the me lical
O’Haras, strutted her hour in a rich rig-out of
black silk veiled in chiffon, with many lace
dependencies thereof, and sundry pink applique
pancake decorations. Her big biack hat was veil-
less, and her hair, which looks chestnut than
of yore, became unruly, so that the tossed Jocks
spoilt the picture. ' '
“Of the other Circes of the limelight, Nellie
Stewart led by an easy length. She was less
erratic, more the dressy divinity of this 1902
world. A skirt revelled in a succession of real
lace flounces on a richly-scrolled net; and a blouse
of old gold and white effects had insertions and
frillings of real lace. The reddish-gold (something
like 100 carat) hair was twisted and plaited and
coiled in an indescribable way on the down hill
slope of a head, on which tottered an immense black-
feathered hat. The three other dazzlers of the
day were Florence Young, in bright, ink-red
foulard with lace interpolations, and a waist that
defied all the mandates of Frockland ; Blanche
Wallace, in billowy black, all frills and laces and
restrained sheen ; and Elsie Moore (another
“Runaway ” light), in the cornflower-blue dress of
the day, and a white felt hat built up with more
blue. Manager Musgrove never parted with his
gloves _ for a second. He wrote in them, shook
hands in them, lost and won in them, went home
in them—is probably still in them. >
“When this is printed the Hopetouns will be
back at Govt. House, after their holiday on a
station near Ararat, rented from a widow of the
Chirnside clan. The thin-blooded Gov.-General
doesn’t care for the breezy sea-side or bracing
mountain-top, preferring the dry, solid heat of
Western-District summers. His younger son was
born nine years ago at that same station and that’s
why Pa paid Ararat A.N.A. the compliment of
proffering them “ Charlie-boy,” as an honorary
member. Offer was accepted (I believe), there
being no age-limit in the case of honorary mem-
bers ; but is it a safe precedent ? If A.N.A.
parents follow suit and take to thrusting forward
infants as small-sized Nationalists, branches must
sooner or later cope with the creche question.
“ Mayor Gillott, ere this is printed, will have
entertained the Hopetouns at a big conversazione
in the Town Hall (March 10th) by way of welcom-
ing them back to town. They could do with a
little house-warming, too, I guess. The Big
House has looked very gloomy in their absence,
with most of its shutters up and sad memories of
young Savile-Gore’s last illness clinging round it.
He brought back typhoid when he returned from
Westralia with his Chief, but he always looked
rather fragile, with the sort of delicate, flushing
and paling complexion that goes with a weak
heart. Only 26 years old, the only son of his
military father and with property, at the time of
his early death, worth £26,000, he had more
reasons than some people for clinging to life.
Perhaps ‘ that’s the very reason he was took,’ to
quote a pessimistic old body, who earns her crust
by nursing (tenderly enough) the sick poor of this
neighborhood. With regard to her patients, she
has a dismal way of saying ‘ Oh, he won’t be took,
he’s too hard-up,’ or ‘ she won’t be took, her
’usband treats ’er too bad,’ or ‘ they'll never make
old bones with 11 kids dependin’ on ’em.’
“ The Hopetouns had arranged for their regret-
ted assistant private secretary to be buried in
Melbourne cemetery, but at the last minute word
came that the bereaved father wished otherwise.
So the funeral-service was held all the same, and
then the undertakers ‘ took charge of the casket
pending its departure for England ’!
“A packed, enthusiastic house made much of
Nellie Stewart last time I sampled ‘ Nell of Old
Drury.’ The Naicest are taking ‘ our
Nellie’ to their fickle bosom again. She wears
more clothes than in pantomime, of course, and
the fact that she now represents a character of
worse moral standing, so to speak, than poor
Aladdin is merely a matter of detail. Dowager
Clarke and her diamonds fronted the d.c. that
evening. Raleighs and Fiskens filled one box,
and nice young girls, platonically interested in
Conductor Bertie Zelman, were plentiful in the
front stalls. So were fair, fat, golden-haired
charmers, who gloated on the Gwynne gowns in
intervals of worshipping their own flowered-
brocades, set off by miles of needlework in the
shape of tucks, ruches, and skirt-panels of goffered
white chiffon.
Yours affectionately,
AEENEHI.

Related was it of Jay Gould (?) that hia
handwriting was so bad as to be illegible to the
old man himself. A letter of his replying to an
application for employment on one of his numerous
railways was on the strength of the signature
used by the recipient as a pass over the line for
many years. In later days the typewriter lias
done much to prevent fraud and mistakes
formerly traceable to bad writing. Had J.G.,
like The Bulletin, used a “New Century”
machine his reputation as a letter writer would be
better to day. New Century Typewriters, the
latest from Yankee-land, are sold only by the
United Typewriter and Supplies Co., of Sydney,
Melbourne and Perth.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
N.S.W. Minister Crick is back from
Maoriland, where he made a strong impression.
The people of those parts had never seen a
Minister like him before.
Think of barrister Lilley, son of the late
Democratic Chief Justice of Queensland, standing
for Maryborough as a Phiipite ! How the old
warhorse must turn in his grave !
About the most disquieting piece of news
with regard to the outbreak of plague in N.S.W.
is that Dr. Ashburton Thompson has returned to
resume control at the Board of Health offices.
Cardinal Moran and Rev. Dill Macky are
agreed upon one point, that mixed marriages are
an abomination. But they differ violently as to
which end of the mix the abomination starts at.
A relic of Victoria’s first elected Parlia-
ment passed over the other day. G. W. Johnston,
who died at Kyneton, aged 91, was elected mem-
ber for that town as far back as the remote and
inaccessible fastnesses of 1856.
Meteorologist Wragge is lucid on the
drought puzzle:
Physical factors, with cosnncal law, most distinctly
operate, and we look for the clue resolving the dry
weather problem primarily to solar physics, and
secondarily to ice distribution in the Antarctic.
As an example of the folly of throwing
chances away a recent suburban municipal bye-
election would be hard to beat. There were two
candidates each having two votes. One voted
for himself. The other didn’t, and was beaten by
one vote.
The recenc statement by one Bundaberg
editor that the other Bundaberg editor’s paper
“ reeks jwith the stink of the polecat and glories
in shame,” is a mere circumstance alongside the
statements made by the latest Sydney 3d. paper
about its detested enemy, the editor of the 2d.
paper. To say nothing of actions for criminal-
libel, surely some more pistol-practice looms out
of the Sewer Feud.
The Late JOHN LUCAS, M.L.C.—A familiar
Sydney street-sight of long ago.
Late John Lucas had been, giving him his
dues, a man of some weight in the community in
more ways than one. To old John, the State of
N.S W. partly owes the Chinese Exclusion Act
and the Jenolan Caves Reserve, whilst the city of
Sydney was indebted to him for the Belmore Park
Reserve. In fact, 25 years ago John Lucas was
a useful Radical: but he wasn’t able to keep up
with the times, and towards the end was as
Conservative as he was stout.

traffic in the city.

dry dog to swim in the gutter.

and speech, rarely, if ever, imbibes fire-water ;

will shout till—well, every man in the crowd is
insured in his office, or dead, or oblivious.

Mr. Kingston makes a powerful leader—good-humored,

A Lilydale vigneron, crawling
through a wire fence t’other day with the usual
loaded gun, emptied the charge into his right foot,
In the last few years the same man has, on different
occasions, broken three ribs, his left leg, both
arms, his nose, and his left thumb.
Bishop Parry, the death of whose widow
is reported from London, was born in Govern-
ment House, Sydney, in 1830. He was educated
in England, but had the offer on one occasion of
being Bishop of Sydney. He became instead
Suffragan Bishop of Dover, the only holder of
that office for 300 years.
Two sons of politicians and a M.P. re-
ceived commissions in the S.A. contingents for
S’Africa. One of the former has fewer qualifica-
tions for the position in the eyes of the troopers
than he apparently possesses to the thinking ot
the Federal authorities.
Miss Guthrie, an Australian lady, com-
mitted suicide in London last week whilst enjoy-
ing the hospitality of Rev. Thomas Spurgeon and
his wife. A spinster of the least suicidal ten-
dencies is likely to grow tired of life if she stays
long enough under a Baptist minister’s roof. And
this writer has heard, Thomas preach ! !
Premier Philp, prosecuting his first elec-
toral contest, rode his horse through the Barron
River with his clothes strapped on his head. The
river was in flood, and a naked Philp got across half
a mile of troubled water. Now an older and fully-
clothed Philp is trying to get across half-a-million
of troubled deficit with much less success.

The mild-mannered W. G. Spence spends
nearly all his time in the House of Reps, copying
out hundreds of pages of MSS. At rare intervals
sympathetic friends, like Deakin and Brown, are
privileged to read a chapter or two. ’Tis said
that Spence is writing a book, largely dealing with
Socialism and the abolition of competition in
industry.
Bishop Stuart, brother of late Premier
Stuart (N.S.W.), expects to take some missionaries
with him on his return to Persia. He was Bishop
of Waiapu (M.L.), about a dozen years ago, but
preferred the heathen of Persia to his local flock
—which, by the way, was at the time very slow
with its stipends. Dr. Stuart will spend some
months in Australia, having a 12 months’ leave,
and is enthusiastic over the prospects of Ispahan.
Sydney’s most distinguished and hos-
pitable gourmet is an eminent dentist, a superb
amateur chef, in whose library are
hundreds of cookery-books. He
once entrusted a servant with the
preparation of a huge English
nam, intended for the entertain-
ment of friends on a very special
occasion. She boiled it until the
bone came out. The gourmet actu-
ally wept —for the first time in 30
years.
If Jenkins, the American gentleman
who runs S.A., were to go to the Corona-
tion he would attend as an alien, for his
letters of naturalisation have no force out-
sido the Commonwealth. In the pre-
Federation days he ceased to be a British
subject directly he crossed the S.A.
border. Ex-Attorney-General Hotn-
burg, who has just resigned the leader-
ship of the S.A. Opposition, is in a
similar position.

The latest about Arthur Streeton is that
he has painted a portrait of Mrs. Maesmore
Morris, the Victorian beauty, for exhibition at
the Royal Academy. Some people never will lay
to their book. So long as the clever Streeton
addressed himself to pumpkin-patches on the
Hawkesbury of his own country he was on the
real road to fame.
“Rolf Boldrewood ” (T. A. Brown)claims
to have been the first man to sell fresh beef on
Ballarat (Vic.) in the digging days. He made
money rapidly for a little while, but he hated the
gory cow-steak, and disliked the “mire and
misery ”of the diggings, so he sold out. Boldre-
wood ever afterwards regretted that he had not
stuck to the business and made a pile.
The three Rutledges, sons of the Parra-
matta schoolmaster, take the world from different
points of view. The Rev. Woolls, who was
elected president, the other day, leads the
Methodist flock; the Rev. Doctor bosses an
Anglican church, and Attorney-General Arthur,
battling in Queensland politics, leads the Devil’s
Brigade in particular, and wobbly politicians in
general.
Died last week, aged 81, Oswald Curtis,
for nine years Superintendent of Nelson Province
(Maoriland). He sat in the House of Representa-
tives for eleven years, and was once a Minister —in
Stafford’s tempestuous Ministry of 1872, which
lasted one month and a day. Curtis was a cour-
teous old gentleman—so courteous that once, when
he had a big political meeting on, and R. S. Smythe
arrived suddenly with a conjuring show, he post-
poned his meeting to avoid damaging the show
business.
Editor Croft, of Gulgong (N.S.W.) Adver-
tiser, who was struck for libel damages in the
matter of one Hilton, has decided not to pay up,
and will take out a year’s confinement in Mudgee
gaol. He is writing a prison diary. A brother
editor from Mudgee is sending his meals in from
a hotel. The local priest and parson supply him

with books, and friends turn up every day to have
a chat through the bars. A philosophic spirit and
a careful selection of your gaol help a man through
many troubles.
Hock Shung, the Chinese who communi-
cated to Sydney Evening News the intelligence
that the local Joss had arranged for rain, and
would send it in a few hours, which prophecy was
duly fulfilled, is well-known on the cycling-track,
and is a sport with no end of an Australee
vocabulary. “ You see,” he said, “we made a
dead bird of it. We got two priests on the job,
separately, and, when they both got the same
answer, we knew it was good enough ; see ? ”
And Hock’s dead bird poured heavily next morn-
ing, as promised.
Departure of Cardinal Moran should lift a
load of anxiety off the shoulders of the two rival
N.S.W. R.C. organs. One or other of these was
always getting a pull on the other by suddenly
printing some remarks that the Cardinal was a
Chrysostom and a Demosthenes, and a Solon
rolled in one ; then the other had to feverishly
exploit its classical dictionary to balance up
next week with some more beautiful analogies.
The constant swinging of the censer must have
proved wearisome. And now there is Blessed
Rest.
A lately-deceased M.L. trainer desired to
embrace the R.C. faith before dying, but was
unconscious when the priest arrived, and remained
so till his.death. The priest consequently declared
his inability to conduct the burial service of the
R.C. church. Anglican parson said he was in a
similar position ; he couldn’t conduct the service
in the Catholic portion of the cemetery, but offered
to read it either in his church or at deceased’s late
residence. This appears to have been objected to,
and ultimately the Mayor of the town, himself a
racing man, read prayers at the grave-side. How
particular these sects are.
DON’T PRAY FOR RAIN—DAM IT.
The only lady who has ever been accused
(writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent)
of being engaged to Lord Ketchener has got
married to someone else not in the meat trade.
She is Lady Helen Stewart, daughter of the
Marquis of Londonderry; the “him” is Lord
Stavordale, heir of the Earl of Ilehester. Ketchener
was a good miss for Lady Helen—she is said to
have jilted him because of the universal asper-
sions cast on his war-doings. Though physically
a fine specimen of the ruthless fighting type,
Ketchener always suggests an essentially-useful
chucker-out.
Anthony Anderson, aged 63, sole survivor
of the aboriginals of Birchip (Victoria), was
solemnly presented with a brass chest-protector
the other day by the Birchip Shire Council. The
plate bears the quaint legend : “ King Anthony
Anderson, of Birchip, Morton Plains, Donald,
and the surrounding district.” King Anthony
Anderson ! King of Donald and the surrounding
district! And there were two shire councillors
and a long dog with short legs present at the
coronation! Premier Peacock was not in the
least distressed about not receiving a royal invita-
tion, and has made no disturbance whatever re
the “sovereign rights” of the States. A vast lot
the most fanatical Jingo cares for royalty when it
has nothing to give !
The name of Howard Spensley, once (for
five months in 1871) Solicitor-General of Victoria
under Duffy, was mentioned in London Ba ik-
ruptcy Court in connection with Whitaker-
Wright’s disclosures as to the squaring of the
financial press over certain company-promoting
transactions. Spensley married a rich Victorian
lady who owned Melbourne Royal Arcade, and a
romantic little adventure which attended a vi fit
of his to Australia some years ago is still fresh in
the memory of the Melbourne public. He has
lived in London for many years, and has, it
transpires, been a part owner (with one Brousson,
of Labouchere’s Truth), of a city print named the
Citizen. Brousson was the man subsidised by
Wright.
“Melb.” : Pastor Fitchett, of the butchery
books, attains the pinnacle of glory, his zenith,
apex, and apogee, by getting elected President of
the Methodist Conference. The things liiat
absorb the mind of an ordinary Methodist cl -rgy-
man are polished off by Fitchett with the mere
fag-end of his intellect. A very small carpet sack
contains all his needful supply of creed and sect,
while the magazines of his mind, the useful store-
houses, are occupied with the Review of Reviews,
and all the worldly concatenations. Other Metho-
dist parsons have to go on weary itinerations. He
can stop where the big money is made. There
would be a row if one of the small ecclesiastics
opened a hay-and-corn store. Fitchett could open
a public house with impunity.
One of the sights of the recent levee held
by Victorian Governor Clarke was Premier
Peacock in a Windsor uniform. He walked out
of the public buildings where-he has his office (and
evidently his wardrobe as well) one mass of gold-
lace trimmings. A stately sergeant of police strolled
along at his side, while Peacock himself held his
cocked hat in his hand and puffed volumes of
murky smoke from a well-blackened pipe. He
smoked all the way up to the reception rooms at
the Exhibition Building, handed his lighted pipe
over to his policeman, did the usual hen’s scratch
before His Ex., slid out and took up his pipe just
where he had left off. Peacock possibly thought
the,democratic pipe would atone for the ridiculous
and crawlsome Windsor toggery. His mouth
puffed Radicalism ; the rest of him was solid Tory.
Mr. E. G. Fitz Gibbon, of the Melbourne
Board of Smells, has issued an open letter
in exceedingly bad Irish Scotch to Mr.
David Syme, which makes entertaining reading.
E.G.F. accuses Syme of pursuing him with
relentless malice, and prints one or two stories :
When George Paton Smith was yer sub-editor, ye
devoted yer first leading article to me, an’ made me oot
to be a blate fule. That same day yer same sub-editor
cam to me in Collins-street, an’ said : “ You think X wrote
that article ? ” I said : “ I do.” He then said :“ I pledge
you my honor I did not write a word of it.” Anal
answered : “As you tell me so, I am bound to believe
you, but if you had not, I should have said you wrote
every word of it.” Some time after that I was in company
wi’, amang ithers, a mon wha haed been a neebor o’ his,
and news cam to us that he was dead, an’ the neebor
said: “ Ah, poor fellow ! He and I lived next door to
each other on the Cotham-road, and many a night he has
been in my house, and many a night I in his, when he
dictated and I wrote his leading articles, which enabled
him to say he had not written a word of them.” Dauvid,
that sub-editor was worthy o’ ye.
The Vic. Govt.’s selection of comparatively
young man Tate to guide the hand of the puerile
Gurr in the Education Department is bound to
provoke bitter resentment among the score or
more of antediluvian inspectors and red-tapeists
who were senior to him. A rumpus was raised
once before over Tate’s rapid advancement, and
he was temporarily banished to fill in time some-
where in the backblocks. But, even while super-
vising the frowsy mind of Mary Jane in the bush,
Tate stood out so conspicuously from the mediocre
crowd who held the snug metropolitan billets that
he was recalled by Education Minister Peacook,
and given control of the training college. That
aggravated the seniors badly, and now the threat
that a mere boy of 39 is going to root a few of
them out is dust and ashes and a Good Friday
diet of salt ling to them.
“ Elijah ” Dowie, the Man with the Purple
Wings, now states that he has been spiritually
assured of immortality. Many a creed hsa
split on that same foolish rock. There was
once a sect of Immortals in England which
hung out on a farm. The ladies of the
sect all wore short skirts, not because they
were ballet-dancers, for they weren’t, but
to keep their tails from getting muddy in
agricultural pursuits. One rich lady-
believer, who put all her money into
the sect and kept it going, did the
hardest and muddiest work to show
that she was not sinfully inflated by
her wealth. The funds ran out, how-
ever, and the mortgagee took the farm,
and the poor, short-skirted sisterhood went to
pieces.
A member of N.S.W. Supreme Court
Bench withstood, lately, a pressing invitation to
take office in a Masonic Lodge. He advanced
the proper theory that a Judge should not be
bound by a secret oath. It were well if Judges
generally patterned by this. Chief-Justice Griffith
is Grand Master of the Freemasons Order in
Queensland, and delights to have it known—quite
a sufficient reason why other Australian Judges
should stand out.

■ The bell-bird (crested orivica) was not to be confused
with the bell-bird of Gippsland, which is correctly termed
the bell-minah.” I use the term “ bell-minahon the

One Sydney parson in his prayer-for-rain
sermon struck a new line when he said :
A little reflection suffices to show that the droughts of
pre-historic times were sent by God for a definite pur-
Pose. It sometimes happened'that there was no food in
the blacks’ camps, and the gunyahs were the habitations
or starvation and bitterness, but God looked in mercy
upon the black man and sent a drought, and caused the
herbage to fail and the young gum-trees to wither.
Then it came to pass that the kangaroos became too thin
and weak to escape out of the range of the boomerang,
an 1 the rock wallabies lost their agility and fell a prey to
the nullah and the spear, and soon the camps were re-
dolent with stews, and thus the black man’s wants were
provided for. Who then shall say that the hand of God
is not discernible in a drought ?
J. he idea that God starved one set of his creatures
so that they would become so feeble in their
misery that another set of His creatures, also
miserable and starving, and consequently slow on
their feet, might totter successfully after them
and eat their fleshless bones, is ingenious, to say
the least. And a drought takes a good many
months, or a year or two, to reduce the kangaroo
to the state above described. So, by this theory
the Lord looked down on the hungry nigger and
miraculously sent him food in about eighteen
months. A Charity Organisation Society is faster
than that.

Dear Bulletin. —“ Why swaggies don’t
peddle?” (8., 3/8/’01.) Easily explained. The
police would say they stole the goods; the squatters
would call them loafers; the buyers would beat
down to minimum, because “You’ll only spend it
in drink ; the cookies would expect goods for
tucker and a doss, as well as a “little present for
Cissy ; and their (swaggies’) mates would say.
You re wuss ner a booted Syrian.” There! But
theres a dodge vacant: produce a rolled-gold,
double-enamelled Pretorian loot-trinket (cost in
morocco case, Is. 6d.), and it raffles for £1 to £5.
(Wrinkle patented by Scotty W.)
Queensland is attracting a host of settlers
from the Victorian mallee. Most of them have
sold everything, and begun the lurid journey to
the tropics in caravans. Their chief reasons for
this step is that land _ may be had reasonably in
Queensland and that it does rain at times It
never rains in the mallee now. Still the move
looks rash. Writer knew a selector south of the
mallee who sold out for close on £2OOO, bought
new horses, waggons, and other conveyances
then set out for Dubbo (N.S.W.), loaded up. He
got there a bit greyer than when he started
dropped the £2OOO, and finished up on a patch
of white sand and whip-stick west of Swan Hill
In his declining years he used to tender this
advice: “If you must sell the farm, spend the
boodle in a thnp to Limerick; hang Dubbo ! ”

“ Werrimee ” on the wild-dog question
* T O w e ! tr t> UC ii ion , di . n g° es - .A great mistake of most
iN.b.w. S.P. Boards is in refusing to accept any but the
yellow scalp and brush; even requiring that these shall
De irom the same animal and be connected by a strin ol
skin off back and neck. Wild dogs are now of all colors,
and in the North-West the black and black-and-tan are
the moot destructive to sheep. They are also more
, b . e ,?^ se boundary-riders and scalpers won’t
trouble to kill them when there is no reward in store.
What is wanted is that the dog-registration law shall be
moie rigorously enforced, particularly in the back
country ; and that every dog found wandering unaccom-
panied by a responsible person be considered a wild dog
the destruction of which shall carry the same reward as
m the case of the true dingo. The objection that in such
case unscrupulous persons would probably breed for
scalps could be met by a heavy dog-tax and a stricter
administration of the Act. Every scalp, not that of a
registered dog, should be paid for on the principle that
the nominally domesticated animal, when at large, is as
destructive as the dingo. In paying for the destruction
ot one dog because its color is yellow, and letting an
equally obnoxious animal go scot free because it is of
some other color, the Boards are simplv wasting public
money, and fostering a breed of mongrels that is a
menace to the pastoral industry.
* * • *
In a sanctuary near Auckland, a Maori
convert, called Hauraki Paul, was once
delivering a cadging missionary address.
Paul was a shining light of the crushed
and suffering order—the kind that has
gone through much tribulation for the
good cause. Commencing in a very
agitated manner he asked for the sym-
pathy and prayers of the congregation
on “behalf of my dear wife whom I
had to leave at a very critical time.
‘The parting was a (great trial to us
both.’ ‘ She-^-she’s—in fact, she’s”
(pause, and in an impressive whisper)
“ she's going to have a young ’un.” Paul
meant well, but his stock of polite
phrases had given out. Consternation
fell on the audience, and everyone
made heroic efforts to keep a straight
face ; but an irrepressible titter began
in the back benches, and gathering
volume, spread with a roar to the front
pews and overwhelmed the lady organist
as she faced the crowd. Her fearful,
stony, deaf-dumb-and-obliviousglare broke
up under the pressure, and hastily stuffing
her handkerchief in her mouth, slie turned
her ba,ek on the people and buried her face in
her hands with such a shriek of merriment that
even Paul began to think he had said something
extra. &
* * * *
“ E.S.S.” : Variety of color in the progeny
of brown rabbits (“ Wallah,” B. 24/8/’01) is no
indication of exceptional increase, nor is it in any
way special; but, of course, variations are more
observable amongst a multitude. With rabbits,
black, blue-black, tan, and sandy are the most
common divergences from the normal brown, but
brown-and-white and black-and-white are occa-
sionally seen, and are generally much bigger and
fatter, but less hardy, than the rest. The albino
rabbit snow-white, with pink or violet eyes—is
rarely met with amongst the wild stock.

“ Achtaen ” : “ Cobber ” (B. 24/8/’01) is
wrong about mutton-birds laying only one egg
Ihey always lay two-white, and about the size
and shape of large duck eggs. These are usually
laid about-24th and 25th Nov., and, if then col-
letted, are palatable, but are no good afterwards.
Many wagers are laid on these occasions as to a
man s egg-eating capacity. I once lost a bet that
another fellow could not absorb 12 dozen at a
sitting. He did it, having first fried them in
Vinegar, ihe oil from the young birds, as well as
being good for consumptives, is a good machinery
lubricant, and is also much used by tanners. The
risk from snakes, when collecting eggs or chicks
is mostly imaginary. I only know of one case of
snakebite in eight years. The old birds, though
very swift in flight, have such long narrow wings
that they cannot rise from the level, so that on
leaving the nest they have to run along the ground
until they can drop off a ledge, and thus get their
wings to work.

“ Pelican ” : On the Clarence (N.S. W )an
aboriginal never died—even if the tribe had ocular
evidence of, say, a tree falling on the unfortunate
except by the magic of an evil-doer in a neigh-
boring tribe. After the corpse was planted, a
relative or close friend would haunt the grave
about sundown and, sooner or later, would see a
churrigurri.” A churrigurri was not a common,
ordinary ghost {that gentleman was called “cooin-
booia ). It was the other self of the destroyer,
come to gloat over the grave of his victim. The
watcher never failed to identify the dernaterialised
one ; and straightway the tribe would sally forth
to demand retribution from the supposed offender’s
tribe. I his was never refused, and should the
owner of the churrigurri be absent, the first
straggling buck of his tribe would do equally well
asa victim to propitiate the departed.

“ F.L.G.” : Re “ H.S.’s ” remarks on the
dugong (8., l/6/’Ol). Possibly Cambridge Gulf has
a different species to that from Port Darwin and
Patterson, but those found here (P.D.) can hardly
be described as “ smooth-skinned,” the body being
covered with coarse bristles fin. long and scattered
from Jin. to lin. apart. There is no outward
trace of hind limbs. Rarely are more than two
found in company. The tusks are 4in. to 7in.
long, but seldom protrude more than jin. from
the gums. These may possibly, in some bygone
ago, have been formidable weapons. The local
nig. is not particular where he spoars the beast,
mil P rov ’ f } ec * *he gear holds, the result is the same.
The blacks probably learned the use of the line
from white men, as formerly they used only the
hand-spear. Also, here no lubra is allowed to
even see the body until cut up—much less eat
any of it. To do otherwise is supposed to injuri-
ously affect pregnant women. I didn’t know
the hides were of any value, and should be glad to
get address of buyers of “dugong products” if
prices quoted are correct.
• * • *
A'bdul Wade, the Afghan, has the
monopoly of the carrying at Mt. Garnet, and the
Chow monopolises it at Herberton, Coolgarra,
Irvinebank, etc. It is quite an every-day occur-
rence to meet 30 or 40 mules in charge of a Chow
along these roads. Billjim, the mighty teamster,
is practically extinct in these regions, and, instead
of his cheerful curse upon the highways, a thin
voice calls out in a strange lingo “ Whaffor?”
* * * *
“ J.S.” : Recently spent a few weeks in
sweet and fragrant Cairns (North Queensland).
The great feature of Cairns is China-
town, and the great attractions of
Chinatown are the Chinese lot-
teries and the Japanese ladies.
Both institutions are in full
swing and good working
order from early morning
till midnight. Little Miss
Japan (there’s about a
dozen of her), with Oriental
patience, sits all day in
the doorway of her dwell-
ing-place, reading strange
books backwards, knit-
ting or sewing, with a
bright smile and a pleas-
ant “Goo’ day; how are
you?” always ready for
the strange passer-by. If
one stops, this is fol-
lowed by a modestly-
whispered invitation to
“come insi’.” Chinatown
is conveniently situated
right in the centre of Cairns.
All Cairns gambles. Give a
child threepence or sixpence
to buy lollies with and it
will go and “mark a tic-
ket.” Also, the spare six-
pences of barmaids and
servant girls go in mark-
ing tickets. Pak-a-pu
is an ideal gambling
game, enticing, sim-
ple to play, and
seemingly easy to
win at. The odds,
in round numbers, are
about a billion to one
against getting a decent win.
When someone wins a few
pounds it is a nine days wonder,
and all Cairns discusses it, while
the “heathen Chinee” now and then des-
patches. a few boxes of sovereigns to China.
Yet this game is permitted where good, honest
old “Tatt.” is not even allowed to advertise. If
two, or three bushmen start a small game of “two-
up ” in the street, every policeman in the place
falls over his feet in his haste to disperse the
crowd. and stop the iniquitous performance.
There is truth in the legend that in Cairns they
lift their hats to a Chinaman ; and it is not to be
wondered at when the authorities, presumably
seeing in the Chow a superior being, grant him
privileges that are sternly denied to the inferior
white man.

“Riverina”: A lot has been said about
horse-butchering by policemen. A lot could
also be said about dog-butchering by the same
body. In our little Riverina township a trooper
essayed the assassination of an unfortunate
pup. He shot at the animal and succeeded in
knocking off an ear and otherwise mutilating it.
The pup escaped, but was brought back to face a
second shot. Again the charge only mutilated,
and the poor brute was eventually drowned to end
its sufferings. This policeman is a returned
cohentingenter. Now how—in the name of Seven
Devils—did a man who couldn’t shoot a tied-up
pup at short range in two tries, pass the shooting
tests for S’Africa ?

On the Esplanade, in Cairns (N.Q.), re-
cently, 24 Hindus were camped, with their mis-
cellaneous assortment of rags, billies, and flapjacks.
Women talked and joked with them. Suppose 24
white men camped there, how soon would they be
moved on? Truly, the ways of the Continuous
Dovt. are not understandable.

Thurla : Have seen a carrier when
loading whisky-cases, drop one end on to another
case, so as to badly jar the bottles. Usual result, a
broken bottle and a leakage through the packing,
the escaping liquor being caught in a billy. Of
course it would be a case of “ accident in transit.”

Governor Le Hunte tells how whooping-
cough, taken to Papua by
European children from
Cooktown, devastated native
villages. Le Hunte tells fur-
ther how native superstition
aggravated the evil, for—“As
each village is attacked,a sor-
cerer is consulted ; some un-
fortunate village of another
tribe is called the culprit, and
a midnight massacre of inno-
cent people is the result. ”

OUTSIDE O' GOYDER’S LINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A cloud of dust at noonday,
And dimly seen behind
A team of six gaunt horses
And a plough—the usual kind.
A man, unkempt and sunburnt,
With whiskers like a vine—
A God-forsaken “ cockie,”
Outside o’ Goyder’s line.
For years—in fact, so many,
That to count he’ll soon forget,
He’s been ploughing up the country,
And he’s got no crops as yet;
But still, like Vanderdecken
(Who for ever ploughs the brine),
He’ll keep on and on till Doomsday-
Outside o’ Goyder’s line.
S. A.
M. J. O’HAIR.

Re bush hermits’
lives (“Sox,” 8.,
8/2/’O2). I knew

larly in a strong

“ Max ” : Cannot let the ’roo-lapping
water controversy die out without having my
say. “H.J.Mc.” is a solid, deep-thinking prao
tical man; that is, he thinks the same as I
do. Near Torrens Creek, North Queensland, re-
cently, I found a perishing “old man” with his
legs caught between saplings laid over a shallow
well. Gave him a drink out of an old tin that
was lying there, and he drank the full of it twice
(about three quarts). Am prepared to swear on a
Bible—a book which I have seen for sale in a shop
up here— that he did not lap. Then lifted some
of the saplings, took the ’roo by the rudder and
dragged him out. When he found his sea legs he
showed his gratitude by trying to come up and
hug me. Didn’t want his too profuse expression
of thanks, so smote him on the brisket with 81bs.
of rock. At that he took the huff, and put up a
record for a quarter of a mile. If anyone disbe-
lieves this yarn I can show him the well; it is
there yet, and perhaps also the tin and piece of
rock.

“ W.T.G.” : lie carriers “ tapping ” casks
(B. 5/10/’01). Usual plan is to bore a hole in the
cask about lOin. from the bottom with a gimlet
and then drive a nail through the top, withdraw-
ing it to let the air in, when the liquor will flow
out below. A plug is afterwards driven into the
hole and cut off level. Then some axle-grease is
applied, so that it would take a sharp eye to detect
tlifi spot. Knew an old carrier who, on a trip
with beer for an out-back pub., “ tapped ” several
casks and varied the usual plan of treating all he
met by selling the beer at 6d. per large pannikin
full. Of course some of his customers “ blabbed,”
and he got into trouble.

“Jeff.”: Re strange night-experiences
(B. 24/8/’01). On the old South Coast Road, Tas.,
between Hythe and the next settlement the road
goes over a double-topped hill, and you cannot
cross the “saddle” at any time, day or night,
without experiencing a curious cold “creepy”
feeling. If you are riding, your horse will break into
a wild gallop. Local residents used, if travelling by
night, to take another track, and of late years the
road has been abandoned altogether. The only
supposedly collateral facts are that a pair of
leg-irons were found there and that 50 years ago
Hythe (then South Port) was a veritable convict’s
hell.
THE MOLONG (N.S.W.)
MARY LOUISA McNEVIN
(now Mrs. H. S. Carr, defendant).
BREACH-OF-PROMISE CASE.
RICHARD YOUNG COUSINS,
plaintiff, awarded £150 damages.
Concerning’ Mr. and Mrs. Carr and
Another.
The moral of the Cousins-McNevin-Carr romance,
as related to the public by a N.S.W. breach-of-
promise case, just concluded, is: When you win
the consent of your rustio heiress, marry her on
the spot, avoid the city and the miscellaneous
company of an hotel, and, above all, keep the
enterprising commercial traveller off the grass.
Otherwise lie will “ book the order,” sure.
Richard Young Cousins, of Heytesbury Park,
Molong—a member of one of the pioneer families
of N.S. Wales —was born to see to the ploughing
of modest acres, to ride and train a horse, and to
shine in the local quadrilles among the rural fair.
His state in life was modest, though not exactly
lowly. At the age of 27 he wooed and won the
“great lady” of the district, Mary Louisa
McNevin (variously stated in court as being aged
between 38 and 45). She was the only child of
John McNevin, of Cardington Hall, an unen-
cumbered holding of 20,000 or more chocolate acres
on the banks of the Bell River, valued at £50,000.
Tall, robust and generously proportioned, Miss
McNevin was a remarkable figure in Molong.
She could spring upon the tallest horse on the
station and handle a spirited team with the
crack whips of the country. And she would
ride or drive through the loneliest road .on
the blackest night untended and untroubled.
She managed _ the station and directed every
sale, purchase and arrangement, and under
her control Cardington Hall prospered splen-
didly. She had had no lack of suitors, and
had been accounted a spinster from choice. So
the announcement of her engagement to Mr.
Richard Cousins was a surprise to most. How-
ever, there was a farewell festive gathering at
Cardington Hall, and all the employees were
invited to drink the health of the proprietress,
and the Bridegroom Elect responded on her
behalf. The wedding-day was fixed for
June 6th at St. Patrick’s, Sydney. The
bridal party and the bridegroom’s party
journeyed to Sydney and put up at the same hotel.
Mr. Stockwell, the McNevin family solicitor, came
to town with legal documents for execution—a
marriage settlement, conferring one-fourth of the
flocks and herds and lands and tenements of
Cardington—a trifle valued at £14,500—0n the
prospective husband, and the last will and testa-
ment of the bride, devising nearly the whole of the
balance to him.
But there wasanother young man on the premises,
Henry Steadman Carr, a clean-shaved, fresh-faced,
dark-haired “commercial ” of 25, who travelled for
wool or something. Cupid inflamed him with
passion for Miss McNevin, and, masterful man,
he won her under the very nose of the young man
from the country. When the wedding took place
at St. Patrick’s, Mr. Carr was the bridegroom,
and he started on the honeymoon to England.
Mr. Cousins consulted his solicitor and issued a
writ claiming £5OOO. The case was heard before
Mr. Justice Owen and a jury of four. On Mr.
Cousins’ behalf it was pointed out that he had
been compelled to mortgage his reaper and dispose
of his horses, and exhaust his banking account, in
order to maintain the dignity of his position as
the affianced husband of the lady of Cardington
Hall. Also, he had to find the money to come to
Sydney to carry out his compact. Touching
details were given as to the lady’s interest in hirn
before Mr. Carr came on the scene. For in-
stance, once she sent a bottle of embrocation
and "trusted “ that it would be well rubbed
on Dick’s chest for his cold.” She also wrote:
“It had been the dream of her life to make
Dick happy.” The plaintiff related how he
had been more or less the object of the lady’s
affections since he was 12 years old, how she had
proposed to him, how he had cautiously, and with
an eye to a settlement and will in his favor, accep-
ted her suit, and how he spent much of his worldly
goods to keep up appearances for the sake of her
and Cardington Hall.
The jury awarded £l5O damages.
When Maoriland first went into the con-
tingent business the ranks contained a consider-
able proportion of “quality,” but the more recent
levies consist almost wholly of agricultural
laborers. The quality still volunteers for
service in the ranks, but there is a growing
impression that its father has arranged with the
doctor to have it rejected as medically unfit, so it
gets all the honor and the frill without any of the
shooting.
“ Invest iga-
trix” on opera-
tions, chloroform
and the like:
Re recent sugges-
tions that deaths
under chloroform
are sometimes due
to the use of masks.
The mask, to the
woman h o sp i t ai
patient, is, frankly
speaking, a stink-
ing abomination.
The heart-test is
frequently per-
formed in the ward;
then the woman-
patient, clad in
‘ operation clothes,’
and recumbent on
a species of dinner-
waggon, is trundled
into the ante-room
of the operating-
theatre, where a
resident doctor
claps on the mask.
To many persons
the odour of ether
(for the best reasons
preferred in this
hospital) is incom-
parably viler than
that of chloroform,
and shortly a sense
of suffocation and
nausea is added to
the intense, but
often well-conceal-
ed, terror of the
patient. The hap-
pily-ignorant
woman accepts
this sensation as
one of the inevit-
able details of the
whole affair ; the
patient who has
had a previous ex-
perience under the
private care of a
doctortakes mental
notes, but if she
be wise, says no-
thing till she is
well outside the
institution. She
can recall the pri-
vate doctor’s pleas-
ant chat, his
waving a hand
kerchief at a respectful distance, his gradual approach,
a creeping drowsiness —and no more. But, as to
the mask. In “operating' wards” it is frequently
desirable to get through eight or nine cases in the
short light of a winter’s afternoon. Also, a skilful
surgeon is often wanted in three or four places at once.
The use of the mask certainly minimises trouble for the
resident-doctor, economises time, and (presumably) econo-
mises the anaesthetic. Whether, for the sake of the
patient’s feelings merely, it is advisable to abolish it is
another question.

Bt E. J BRADY.
Tender Mercies!
A Sydney street. A horse slips and falls. The
driver of the van alights and thrashes the beast
by way of persuading him to rise. A few frantic
efforts, and then, with a heart-rending cry, the
horse stretches out, and it is seen that the leg-
bone is protruding from the flesh. A crowd
gathers, out of idle curiosity—nothing more. The
driver curses vividly, and bemoans his ill-luck-
no hysterical sympathy for the poor beast in its
agony. Someone goes for one of the men work-
ing on the new tram-line. He arrives with a huge
hammer. . The crowd now presses forward.
Things are getting interesting. The strong man
whirls the heavy hammer over his head, and . . .
thud ! The horse struggles pitifully, and almost
rises on the fractured limb. A couple of police-
men arrive, but they don’t seem to understand
that their revolvers would be comparative mercy.
Perhaps the regulations forbid. The great iron
death-dealer swings once more . . . then crash !
crash ! Splintered bones and protruding brains.
The crowd, as if satisfied, slowly disperses.
Again, a carriage collides with a cab- horse
and an Sin. strip of flesh is torn from the
horse’s leg and hangs nearly to the ground.
Cabby yells after the retreating carriage
and then for some minutes discusses with his
mates the identity of the occupants. Meanwhile
copious streams of blood run into the gutter and
form a little rivulet. The horse ceaselessly paws
with the injured leg, endeavoring to find relief,
and with each “paw” the gaping wound is torn
afresh and other blood-vessels spurt forth their
streams. A handkerchief would temporarily
stanch the flow and bind the flesh, but nobody
seems to think of it. Twenty minutes pass in
cursing the coachman—for causing the horse’s
agony ? No ; for rendering the animal unprofit-
able for a week or two. Two policemen stand
calmly by with notebooks until the horse is at last
led limping away, with the flesh still hanging from
the leg. Animai-Protection-Societies will never be
of much use in a community in which religion
teaches that man is a special creation. Not till it
is recognised that man and'the horse have common
ancestors—are, in fact, cousins many times re-
moved—will men have real feeling for the woes of
their equine slaves.
Charitable Aid.
A lady writes about the Charitable Aid systems
of New South Wales :—
N.S.W. has numerous charities, most of them misfits.
The other day a man was thoughtless enough to fall from
the cliffs at Manly, and applying at the district hospital
was told that there was no provision for persons who
broke their heads in this manner. He was informed, as
it were bv proxy (his head not being in a fit state to
master the intricacies of charitable aid at the moment)
that he should get his skull wrecked next time at some
place within reasonable distance of a casualty ward. We
are told that there are several thousand cases of tuber-
culosis in Sydney, but thirteen beds in a country hospital
(not enough to give more than halfan-hour’s bed time
par head) is all the provision made for those who intend
to succumb to the disease. Democracy is continually
prating of the Right to Live. I want to know where
the Right to Die comes in—the right to die without being
expected to get the business over in half-an-hour so as to
make room for someone else ?
Our charitable aid systems were evidently conceived in
a humorous spirit. If a society labels itself a Children’s
Protection Societv it usually looks after old women ; if it
professes to be a Women’s Protection Association it keeps
a parental eye on the fatherless. Thus a sick woman
appealed to be taken to a hospital, with the result that a
burly inspector came along to say that she must let her
boy be remo\ ed to a cottage home. The system has a
profound faith, not only in the philanthropy, but in the
abundant leisure, of everybody outside its own ranks, and
anyone who wants to give information in cases of sickness
or distress is chivvied about from one department to
another for a week or so. By that time the person has
died, owing, possibly, to a spirit of pure cussedness and a
desire to cause trouble all round, and to get some poor
official dismissed or censured. There is no General
Information Bureau, as in most other countries, and if
such a bureau were formed it would probably spend its
time rescuing last dogs, so, maybe, it’s just as well to be
without one.
In Germany, every city possesses such a bureau,
working like oiled machinery, no child can be fatherless
and friendless for more than half-an-hour, the State
parent at once assuming the responsibility.
The charity of a charitable aid official sometimes works
in a spasmodic manner. Each cultivates a special horror,
and coldly refuses to be annoyed by the horrors of any
rival official. When one of the band was shown a room
where a consumptive boy slept with some grown people,
he refused to lose his official calm till he noticed that the
boy also reposed in the society of a decayed pumpkin.
Diseased fruit was his particular horror, though he
declined to be horror-stricken at diseased boys. That was
the business of another society.
An official’s idea of extreme measures is to write to the
papers. “ Sool the press on to them ; they deserve
it,” was his advice. When you do “ sool ” a paper on to
them they get much excited for three days, during which
time the representatives of about ten societies, a
policeman or two, a sister Somebody-or-So, and the case’s
long-lost brother, all troop down to the case and dispute
the other’s right to him. In one instance they begged
that, as they could not personally supervise the invalid,
they might disinfect the premises, and for some days sul-
phur, chloride of lime and carbolic were distributed
through a set of office buildings till people coming in on
business made complaints that “ if must be a case of
plague and no policeman is stationed at the door.”
Our charitable aid system suffers from the public as
well as from officialdom. Whenever any Leading Citizen
notices that some institution or branch of one is not quite
O.K. financia’ly, instead of trying to put the small wrong
right by giving coin to set it on its legs again, he starts a
rival establishment which, after a while, also suffers from
financial depression. There are certain cases, however,
wherein the poorest mendicant is chased by doctors;
and every institution for the sick will give him room.
There is an aristocracy in diseases, and if a man has only
the sense to get some mysteriously intricate and unusual
complaint, he may pick and choose. Provided only his
vitals are so remarkable as to be worthy of preservation
in a bottle when he is done with them, and his case is one
to be discussed in the medical journals after death, then
he may be happy. But some mean people, after thus
revelling in luxury, recover and go away, leaving the
bottle which should have contained their vitals empty
and deserted. These things sour the charitable aid
system, and make if all the less responsive when next a
case of plain rheumatism comes along.
