POLITICAL
POINTS
If only G. H. Reid’s Dry Dog had a taste
for rats!
Writes a pressman employed in the gallery
of the House of Representatives:—
My tip for the High Commissionership is Edmund
Barton, with no one else in the running.
Tudor, M.H.R., contended warmly for
a heavy duty on tombstones, because, he said,
they are luxuries. Well, they’re not necessaries
of life, anyhow.
Pawnbrokers, tobacco dealers, billiard and
bagatelle proprietors, hotel keepers and people
of various other occupations are licensed
and pay annual fees. Why not compel finan-
cial agents and money-lenders to register and
pay annual fees also ? The profit might not
be much, but a very little money gladdens a
very large statesman these times.
Mauger asked M‘Millan in the House of
Representatives to explain why he declared
in politics that Protection always raised prices
enormously, and declared in his trade
circulars that under Protection his
firm was < selling below Free-
trade prices. M‘Millan’s
reply was mainly a sickly
protest against this mix-
ing up of politics and
business —in other
words, he protested
against being expected to
live up to his theories. All
the same M‘Millan’s firm
hasn’t managed to “ pass
the duties on to the con-
sumer.” The country
fets more revenue under
'rotection, more work,
more wages, and unless
M‘Millan’s firm is
Bhamefully inaccur-
ate, is getting its
softgoods at a
lowerprice. Pos-
sibly M'Millan’s
rich absentee
bosses are mak-
ing less profits
thougli, which
would explain
many things.
That verac-
ious politician,
Premier Philp, of
Queensland, went to
the poll promising,
if he came back
politically alive, to
prune the big salaries,
reduce the number of
members, and, in fact,
do just what he de-
clined to do last session,
when he fought tooth
and nail against the
reductions advocated
by the Labor party.
“ When the devil was
ill,” &c. Philp is a
humorless person.
Think of the man who increased the annual expen-
diture by £831,000 per annum in two years, calling
himself the leader of the Party of Economy !
Notice-of-motion in the Australian Senate
(and one day it will be so ordered): —
That, to provide a fund for the payment of old-age
pensions, and for other purposes, the manufacture and
sale of tobacco, cigars and cigarettes should become a
national monopoly within the Commonwealth.
A lieutenant with eight years experience
applied for a commission in M.L. Ninth Con-
tingent, and was quietly told to join as a private
trooper. A raw, inexperienced youth, son of a
wealthy Aucklander, was made a sergeant same
day, and got a commission within a week.
Apropos a recent cable. It is the doom of
Holland to be swallowed by the Germans, for
Holland stands between the open sea and
Germany, whose destiny and ambition it is to
become a great naval power. Holland was
German territory for centuries, and the Teuton is
coming back for his lost property.
Rev. Tremayne Dunstan, of Sydney, in a
recent sermon upon the war, said he saw
Right for ever on the scaffold,
Wrong for ever on the throne.
Clearly the allusion was to Commandant Scheepers
on his scaffold and Edward VII. on his throne;
but we didn’t expect that from Mr. T. Dunstan.
O‘Sullivan’s frenzied desire to give holidays
in honor of obscure Scotch Saints that went to
Ireland, and other small fry, will yet lead to
popular dislike. The civil servant, who is paid
for holidays, may not object, but the laborer who
loses a day’s wage every time O ‘Sullivan breaks
out already begins to say things.
An application is in for bringing under
Torrens Act in N.S.W. a part of 240 acres granted
to William Bligh. The legend goes that when
Governor King was retiring from office in 1806 he
made a grant to his successor, Bligh, and that when
Governor Bligh came into power he made a grant
to ex-Governor King. The kindly feeling which
these two hard-swearing old gentlemen had for
each other can’t be too highly praised.
On the late N.S. W. humiliation-and-prayer
day Government clerks and officers were paid as
usual, but the Government pick-and-shovel men
had the day deducted ! The navvies on the
duplication works, Katoomba way, who were thus
mulcted, sent in a remonstrance. Navvies, as a
class, are not much given to prayer, and they
don’t see why O’Sullivan should stop their pay
and pose as a benefactor of the workers at the
same time.
Pliilp’s demand that members of the Fede-
ral Parliament shall pay in ready money when they
travel on Queensland railways, instead of hand-
ing in an order on the Federal Treasurer, created
more indignation in the House of Reps, than the
most Tory proposal made in that Chamber.
Members called him “Quilp” amid scornful
cheers, and one Minister pointed out the scandalous
humiliation it would be if a member were accosted
on a railway platform and asked to pay cash.
Down with Philp I
HIS EXCELLENCY WILL WINTER IN
SYDNEY.
“A long letter has been received by Mr. See from the
Governor-General, Lord Hopetoun. His Excellency says
he hopes to be settled in Sydney at the time of the
arrival of the State Governor, Sir Harry Rawson. The
Governor-General adds that he is feeling a great deal
stronger than he has been for a long time, and hopes that
by spending the winter in Sydney his health will be fully
set up.”— AGE.
LORD HOPETOHN : “I go where duty calls, hut I
take precautions. Disinfection is the better
part of valor.”
William M‘Millan indignantly denounced
the proposed duty on boots and shoes as an
attempt to reserve the Australian market for
Australian manufacturers. In the eyes of the
unpatriotic and anti-Australian M’Millan the
Australian market should be reserved for the
German, French, American and English manu-
facturers. The imported importer - man, it
would seem, actually thought he was making a
devastating accusation when he charged the
Government with trying to let the Australian
have the use of his own market.
“ Melh.” : Judge Hamilton, in our county
court, had, t’other day, an opportunity for an
enunciation of the grand principles of the law,
which Judges of small courts magniloquently de-
light in. It was a twopenny half-penny case of
trespass, such a paltry affair that Hamilton only
gave a farthing damages. Yet it gave him a
chance to declaim on Sacred Proputty. He said
he had met with a legal dictum that even flying a
balloon two miles over a man’s land is trespass !
He was inclined to think that a landowner pos-
sessed the sky right up to heaven !
Colonial Sugar Co. in N.S.W. does nob
pay for cane at a fixed price, but according to the
“percentage of obtainable cane sugar,” on a
scale of the eo.’s own devising, and it will not
allow the results shown by this scale to be checked
by the actual sugar returns.. The growers regard
the co’s secret scale with distrust; and as the
rebate on Federal excise is 4s. per ton on cane
going 10 tons to one of sugar, with a proportionate
increase or decrease for cane above or below that
standard, the growers hope that the Federal
Government will ascertain the percentage of sugar
through its own officers.
Victoria’s Premier Pecksniff is stopping
the old age pensions of some men over the statu-
tory age on the ground “ that they look strong
enough to work.” One venerable, judged strong
enough to work and so deprived of his pension,
was aged 69 ! When Premier Pecksniff has
stopped the pensions of all the aged whose
characters are not good enough, those who look
strong enough to work, and those who are sus-
pected of being under age, of drinking, of having
affluent relatives or evil intentions, of being aliens,
and of not being the persons they claim to be, the
total Victorian pension outlay will be about £1
per annum.
The British Government has appointed a
committee to inquire into the increasing employ-
ment of Lascars m the British mercantile marine.
The Lascar is no great use for fighting purposes,
and if he, by his superior cheapness, edges the
Briton out of his own mercantile fleet, then that
fleet ceases to be a recruiting ground for the navy
in war time. Certainly, there is room for alarm.
Between 1891 and 1900, the Lascars employed on
British ships increased by 12,000, while the other
sailors decreased by 5000. And the other sailors
are becoming less and less British, and more and
more Italian, Greek, Scandinavian, Chinese, Negro
and miscellaneous.
Labor Herald (Adelaide) :
Not one of the lawyer’s craft has the slightest sympathy
with the aspirations of the masses to lead a nobler life
and escape from the eternal drudgery to which, mainly
owing to the lega 1 profession, they have hitherto been
subject.
Which is rough on Kingston, Turner, Deakin,
Barton, Leake, Glynn, and other present-day
politicians who profess Democratic sentiments,
and have all done something to justify their
claims. Also, it is rough on the memory of Higin-
botham. And, in addition, it is arrant nonsense.
There are many scoundrel lawyers, but half the
great patriots of history were of the legal craft.
Another letter from Wyalong (N.S.W.) :
Re meeting held at Wyalong (March 27), to condemn
Holman, M.L.A., for his anti-war sentiments : The Mayor
informed the audience that he would allow discussion on
the motion, and so induced the audience to keep quiet
while the Jingo speeches were being made. When one of
Holman’s supporters went on to the stage to speak on the
motion, the Mayor declined to hear him, and put the
motion, without reading it to the audience. The crowd
rushed the stage, and, but for the police, a disturbance
would have taken place. The Mayor declared the motion
carried, which it certainly wasn’t. This is a sample of
British fair-play in Wyalong. Holman’s opponents are
afraid to call a meeting in West Wyalong, as they know
the people here wouldn’t tolerate such unfair tactics.
Holman had a majority of 100 in West Wyalong last elec-
tion.
On the Day of Humiliation it was
announced that N.S.W. Premier See, in his
official capacity, would attend service at St.
Andrew’s Cathedral. Now why, seeing that
Australia has no State Church, and the Quakers
and the Mormons are just as official here as the
Anglicans, should Premier See officially attend
any Anglican tabernacle ? The Anglican is the
official church in England and the Presbyterian in
Scotland (Queen Victoria was an Anglican in one
country and a Presbyterian in the other); in
Ireland there is no official church whatever, and
every creed scratches for itself. This makes the
official business all the more absurd. There isn’t
even that dry and fleshless thing called precedent
behind it.
In palliation of the dreadful all-round
frost achieved by the first N.S.W. “ South Africa
Day” celebration, the promoters urge that this
time they were only getting their hand in;
future “S’ Africa Days,” they say, are to be
stupendous successes. No doubt the returned
heroes will think quite as much of them-
selves next year and the year after as they do
now ; in fact, they are likely to fancy themselves
more and more as time rolls on and their hair and
teeth drop out; but that the Australian public
will increasingly admire those citizens who set out
at five shillings a day to help in choking one of the
most gallant little nations in all history, at the
behest of a venal gang of Hebrew and other
conspirators, is open to doubt.
When the Federal Government proposed
to put a small duty on paper with a view to
building up, if possible, a local paper-making
industry, Freetrader Thomson rose excitedly to
make the usual brag about how paper mills were
flourishing in N.S.W. under Freetrade, which
demonstrated that sturdy industries grew up in
the bracing atmosphere of free competition, &c.,
&c. Thomson might have added that the paper
mills of N.S.W., which employed 101 hands as
late as 1897, had faded away so completely in the
“bracing atmosphere,” &c., that in 1900 they only
found work for 46 hands. Coghlan records this
sorrowful fact on page 648 of his Statistical
Register of N.S.W. for 1900, but the Freetrader
isn’t expected to know little things like that.

was about to furiously cut down the public ex-

The new family of “spit ” jokes will now
have an innings in Westralia, Parliament having
passed an anti-expectoration-on-pavements Health
Amending Act.
“Eller.”: It is about time the N.S.W.
Public Service Board was swept out of existence,
or else it should reduce its own salary considerably.
It has only half the Departments under its control
now. The customs, excise and G.P.O. have
passed to the Federal Government, and the rail-
ways and tramways and Harbor Trust are not
bossed by the Board. While on the point, what
dose theP.S.B. mean by the word “temporary”?
I have a friend 10 years in the service, yet he is
still a “ temporary ” clerk. After another 20'
years is there a fair chance that he may become
permanent, or partly permanent ?
The anti-Australian Freetrader, in his
anxiety to prove that everything manufactured
here must needs be poor and rotten, and that
unless we firmly reject the goods of our own
countrymen and buy only from “Brummagem ” or
Germany we are Lost, falls in at times. Paddy
Glynn, whom the Crow-eaters have been unwise
enough to put into the Federal Parliament, rose
the other day to urge members not to buy the
furniture made by his constituents because it was
no good, and called on them to discourage his
constituents’ furniture industry in every way,
and always to buy from the foreigner. S.A. has
been using well-deserved language to Glynn over
this matter. Why that superior gentleman con-
descends to represent people who are so inferior
that they can’t even make a decent chair is an
unsolved mystery.
The importers’ party—the Freetraders and
hired agents of foreign manufacturers—in the
House of Representatives became red-hot with
fury, over the proposed duty on imported boots,
and one of them declared, with a thick voice and
a bloodshot eye, that “with such unheard-of
duties no ordinary man will be able to buy boots
for his children.” The Freetrade man has an in-
sulting habit of starting with the assumption that
the Australian is a born idiot who can’t possibly
make his own boots, or his own anything ; conse-
quently, he must buy the imported article—the
one with the tax on it. Why Australia pays a
lot of these cheap and nasty traitors to run down
its products, and boost up those of the foreigner,
and hold its people up to scorn as a community
that is only fit to kill bullocks, or watch sheep
fattening in a paddock, will always ’■emain a
mystery.
A certain captain of the South African
constabulary, is stumping Victorian country dis-
tricts, gathering in recruits, who are led away
with promises of Boer farms and other Boer
property, if they will bind themselves to serve in a
certain “ defence force,” not particularly specified,
the members of which must “be at the disposal of
the authorities in time of need.” The captain is
very careful to assure everybody that he is not
recruiting, but his game is so like a press-gang con-
fidence man’s that the Federal Government, if it
has not lost its last little idea of its duty and
authority, will demand explanations, and mean-
while shut down on the Cap. suddenly and
with force. Perhaps even Edmund Barton
can see that it is not the clean vegetable to send
Australian contingents to filch farms in order that
the loot may be used to decoy other young
Australians away from this country to the land of
Cohen and Cohn.
“ Kiwi,” ou Maoriland Jingoism :
Re Bulletin article on the attempted revival of
Royalty. There are signs of it here, too. In bygone
years Premier Seddon was a stout advocate of an elective
Governor. Last session when several members ventured
to criticise the Governor—dared to state that he was not
above the law—Seddon denounced them as “opposed
to all lawful authority.” Speaking a little while
ago at a banquet to some returned cohentingenlers,
Seddon denounced the anti-Jingoes as “ traitors tn the
King.” How it resembles Salisbury’s drivel about “ the
King’s rights” in South Africa! Speaking at another
war function a few days later, Governor Ranfurly fol-
lowed in Seddon’s wake about those awful anti-Jingoes,
whose conduct, he said, should be punished by “the
strong arm of the law.” This disgraceful interference by
an imported Governor with the free rights of citizens
here was actually applauded by the foolish rabble.
Again, one Shaw, with a long tail of letters after his
name and the lenged, “ Six Years Examiner in Consti-
tutional History to the Melbourne University,” has con-
tributed an article to our local Times lamenting •“ the
inroads made by popular government on the King's pre-
rogative ” !

HYDATIDS CURED
BY
VITADATIO
AFTER EIGHT YEARS’ SUFFERING.
Symond’s Place, Adelaide,
November 7,1900.
Mr. S. A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.
Dear Sir,—About eight years ago I underwent an
operation for Hydatids. I understood at the time there
was a possibility of a return of the disease, but all went
very well until last Christmas, when I became ill, a lump
forming in my side, where I had been operated on, severe
pains ensuing, together with great weakness. I became
at last so bad that I felt like giving everything up and
dying, life was such a burden to me. I am a cab pro-
prietor, and managed to drive to your head office for
Adelaide, and there decided to take a course of
VITADATIO. I began to feel better after the fourth
bottle, and after taking nine bottles the lump has entirely
disappeared, together with all pains and weakness, and I
now feel in splendid health, and life is worth living again.
I think VITADATIO would be cheap at £1 a bottle when
it effects cures like mine.
I remain, yours truly,
J. MCNULTY.
Witness—Geo. R. Davie, Adelaide.
Mr. McNulty is a well-known cab proprietor, and may
be seen daily on the stand, King-William Street.
For further particulars ,
S. A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.
Correspondence Invited. Write for Testimonials
London Star replies to Kipling, and gives
that bounceable little bard a hot time: —
THE OAF’S ANSWER.
Fenced by our patient fathers, ringed by our peaceful
seas,
Long did we wake in quiet, and long lie down at ease,
Till you said of Strife : “ Where is it ? ” of the Sword :
“ Let it flash again ! ”
Till you made a god of wanton war and an idol of armfed
men.
We stopped our ears to the warning—we would neither
look nor heed—
We set our hate above our laws, and our lust above our
need.
Because of our witless rancor, and our pitiless pride of
race,
We grudged our brothers freedom and our friends a
resting-place.
You blustered and bragged and bellowed, and we paid
when you bade us “ pay,”
But where are the fifty thousand men that you twanged
to Table Bay ?
For soon were the judgments loosened, soon was our
shame revealed,
At the hands of a little people, few, but apt in the field.
Though now they are only a remnant (and Milner has
started his Star)
You ask for more of our millions and more of our flesh
for war.
Sons you would tear from their mothers, lads from their
lasses sweet,
And brush them under the wheels of war like the dust
and dirt of the street.
And what did you look they should compass ? Glory that
fades like a breath,
Glory to God in the highest at the price of a brother’s
death !
So ! And we asked : “Is it glory to hollow the veldt with
graves,
And to build a gilded empire on the backs of beaten
slaves ? ”
But you said:—“ The war is over ” ; but you said: —“The
end is come,”
And we swallowed the hocus of Hatfield, we swallowed
the lie of Brum.
Then we returned to our vomit, then we contented our
souls
With the kipling fools at the pothouse, and the rudyard
oafs at the polls.
Given to strong delusion, wholly believing a lie,
We saw that the war was not over, and we Let the months
go by,
Waiting some easier wonder ; hoping some saving signs,
Cheated —openly cheated—for the sake of the Sacred
Mines.
Cheated—and sick of your boasting, ah, what is your
boasting worth,
When the ink you fling is a blotch of blood on the rotting
Earth ?
Bard of the gutter Jingo, and Banjoist of the Beast,
Your vogue is the vogue of the vulture that follows the
tiger’s feast.
It was not made with the mountains ; it is not one with
the deep.
Fools and oafs devised it. Fools and oafs must keep.
Fools, not men, belaud you ; oafs, not men, adore ;
How should men applaud you, 0 Laureate of Gore f

After Bandmann had reached the third week of his
first engagement at Melb. Royal he complained that the
Arhus sent a common reporter to notice his performances
instead of a critic of established reputation, and threat-
ened to write to Edward Wilson, the chief proprietor of
the paper, then in London, and demolish the offending
parties. At his second visit to Melbourne, seven or eight
years later, he appeared in a small variety hall, and the
dog-reporter did him, and Bandmann was undisguisedly
glad to see the dog-reporter.
A bill in the possession of a Ballarat manager reads :
POPULAR CONCERT.
MISS NELLIE MITCHELL.
Admission One Shilling.
Melha’s prices have gone up since then.
Reginald Dartrey and Miss Bessie Thompson are return-
ing to Australia about the end of this year. People who
remember Dartrey as the battered old actor in “ Tre-
lawney of the Wells ’’—the one who protested against the
new order of dramatic art because it didn’t bellow as the
old order did—will be glad to see him again. And as a
coldly supercilious mother-in-law or a polished dame of
high degree who shuddered through an eye-glass at her
son’s intended lowly bride, and ordered her to be taken
away, Miss Thompson had few equals hereabouts.
When the one-time melodramatic Fechter first pro-
duced “ Monte Cristo ” in London it was past unidnight
before the curtain rose on the last act. Fechter, in the
name-part, was “ discovered ” in an attitude of contem-
plation. For some moments he made no movement, and
not a sound broke the silence, until a subdued, but
anxious, voice from the gallery observed: “I hope we
are not keeping you up, sir ? ”
Dion Boucicault, the original D. 8., was the first
manager to charge 10s. for reserved stalls in a London
theatre. That was when he produced his drama, “Led
Astray,” at the Gaiety', with two American actors in
leading parts. Previously, the price of a stall (except for
grand opera) had been three half-crowns.
Two pieces of quaint humor from London. Maxime
Elliott (Mrs. Nat. Goodwin) presided at the ladies’ dinner
of the O.P. Club, and made a short speech. Goodwin
should have spoken at greater length, but was called
away, and playwright Cecil Raleigh spoke for and as the
absent one, with sundry references to what “ ray- dear
wife ” had said. Simultaneously, at the Playgoers’ Club
(the two clubs were one until a split last year), one
Edwards was lecturing on “The Superiority of Shaw to
Shakespeare.” After which Bernard Shaw himself rose
“ to put in a word on behalf of my great rival.”
When Gerardy re-visits Australia with his wonderful
’cello he will probably make it a honeymoon tour. Many
girls in Melbourne and Sydney will be sorry 7 to hear that
Gerardy’s fate was fixed before they saw him. His
constancy to the far-away Phyllis (not of the musical pro-
fession) was something remarkable —for a ’cellist.
The dearth of good singers in- the Melb. churches is
remarked by musical visitors. Such small fees are given
that it is not worth a known singer’s while to take on a
choir. One very big R.O. church offered a visiting Sydney
tenor £l5 a year to sing twice every Sunday and attend
weekly rehearsals as well. When he scornfully refused,
a surprised and pained clean-shaven cleric explained that
he had a " first-rate man ” doing the job for five guineas
a year.
The story goes that when Melba was a young girl
living at Carlton (Melb.) her father, David Mitchell, did all
he knew to discourage wilful Nell’s ambition to sing in
public. Even then she was talking of her London debht,
which didn’t happen until she was about a dozen years
older. On one occasion she engaged a hall, and went
around the district selling tickets for a song recital on
behalf of some charity or other, but papa Mitchell also
went around desiring all kind friends to stay away from
his girl’s entertainment. He didn’t want her head turned
by this stage nonsense. As the result of the “ old man’s ”
parental diplomacy daughter Nellie was confronted by an
audience of three at her charity concert. So with an un-
turned head and a dauntless heart the young amateur
gave her trio of supporters the full programme, just as
carefully as Dean Swift executed his Divine Service duties
for the spiritual benefit of Roger, the clerk, when they
twain had the whole tabernacle to themselves.
Nobody ever saw Barry Sullivan with a stick or um-
brella. He said it made an actor lop-sided.
A London syndicate is working hard to. boom the new
Stroh violin, which has a corrugated aluminium disc with
trumpet attached, in place of the wooden box or “ belly ”
that acts as resonator in the old classic fiddle. The per-
former plays a Stroh exactly as he plays upon the strings
of an ordinary violin, but it is claimed that he gets a far
richer and stronger tone from the new instrument. Two
Strohs will serve the purpose of six fiddles pure and
simple, thereby throwing four men out of employment,
or increasing an ordinary orchestral effect threefold, just
according to circumstances. And as the Stroh disc and
trumpet can be applied to ’cello, harp and viola (it is
claimed) professional musicians are likely to lose more
than the sacred cause of music will gain by this labor-
saving invention. The enthusiasm of the Strohites is
doubtless, genuine enough. They have the testimony of
skilled performers that the new violin gives forth new
effects, but in the meantime there remains a healthy
doubt as to whether the old violin, with its more piercing
tone, is likely to be dropped. The Stroh is innocent of
“scrape.” The “scraping” of an amateur is vile. But
then the apotheosised “ scrape” is the most thrilling of all
musical charms.
“ Adelaide” : If it is easy to make fun of “On Active
Service ” (B. 15/2/’O2), what would your critic have said
of it as played here, in the height of the pro-Gore fever ?
The scene was violently changed from the Soudan to the
Transvaal, with intervals of oversight, whereby the vil-
lain threatened to throw the heroine to the “dusky
fanatics below.” Also, the comic man’s wife, who had
pursued him to the scene of war, remarked that she had
landed at Port Said. She must have walked about 4000
miles !
Verbatim copy of advertisement (in America) of a
phonographic impression of a lynching scene in a
Southern State : “ Hear the negro yell and plead for
water as the flames slowly consume him ! Get your ticket
at the counter and hear him ! This week only.” And
Britain, which professes to be shocked at the Boer’s
treatment of the black man, calls the U.S. its friend and
brother.
The theatrical libel action as we get it nowadays—Billie
Barlow v. The Bulletin, f’instance—was a thing unknown
n the ’7o’s or early ’Bo’s. Thirty years ago, Kate Santley,
ia plump, short-necked little woman, captured the Cock-
ney soul with her “ rorty ” mock-French style in opera-
bouffe. Kate amounted to an influential person in the
London mummer world. Even now she survives as sole
lessee of the Royalty Theatre, where, at latest dates, a
musical comedy, with Decima Moore and Phyllis Brough-
ton in the cast, was advertised for “two performances
daily.” And this is how the Baily’s Magazine critic wrote
of her performance of “ La Belle Helene,” at the Alham-
bra (Sept., 1873): “ We will not go so far as to say that
all the applause bestowed on Miss Santley was of a mer-
cenary character. We believe she has deservedly won the
sweet voices of the music-hall ‘ gent.,’and his applause
was genuine. On that undesirable specimen Miss Santley
lavishes her smiles ; for him she slaps her legs ; and for
him she dresses in defiance of every canon of taste, to say-
nothing of decency. And he well repaid her devotion on
the first night of ‘ La Belle Helene.’ Her most inartistic
entry was the signal for a roar ; at her first ‘ wriggle ’ to-
wards the footlights it deepened in volume, and at
‘ wriggle ’ No. 2 the house got frantic. . . . The Alhambra
proprietors, whoever they are, must be congratulated on
the possession of such a gem as our ‘ English Schneider.’
Poor Schneider ! We are no great admirers of the fair
original, but still we feel commiseration for her. At least,
she is clever, she is full of humor, she can act. We will
not pursue the contrast.” When you have carefully read
that uncompromising notice of a “ celebrity,” and con-
sider what would happen to any wealthy English-speaking
journal (outside of America) that dared to tell so much
truth nowadays, you have got an idea of how things have
changed during the last quarter-century. To say that a
modern stage-woman dresses in defiance of decency and
wriggles for the applause of a low-lived claque, would be
to rush into a law-court. But the aforesaid slating of
Kate Santley had no legal consequences. The truth was
mighty in 1873.
When Walter Montgomery and Jas. Anderson appeared
at rival theatres in Melb. in ’67, there was a fair stand-up
fight between the new or natural style of acting and the
robustious, melodramatic manner handed down by
Macready from the Kembles. Anderson, representing
the latter, went hopelessly to the wall. Yet there were
some fine points in him. He was a splendid Petruchio,
and in an heroic character like Ooriolanus, Montgomery
could not get within coo-ee of him. Ellen Tree (Mrs.
Ohas. Kean) used to say that Anderson was the best
stage-lover she had ever met. But he had outlived his
artistic time. Macready said that Fechter could not act
at all, and it is tolerably certain that the present genera-
tion of playgoers would not listen to Macready, except as
a temporary curiosity.
A very successful cinematograph show was given in a
Melbourne South suburban schoolroom one night, a week
or two back, the most popular exhibit being a realistic
fight between two liver-and-old-gold bull dogs. The dogs
rolled all over the curtain, and two experts behind the
rag lend a sound of versimilitude by growling and yapping
energetically. Suddenly, when the excitement was at its
height, a new combatant appeared on the field. With a
howl a large brown retriever sprang from under one of
the seats, climbed the platform, and went straight for the
picture. The new-comer grabbed a mouthful of the near
combatant and ate him right out of the scenery, and then
went for the fragments of the other amongst the ruins of
the screen, and worried the calico to rags before the film
was turned off. It transpired that two evil small boys,
who had seen the show before, had determined to try it
on the dog.
Violinist Bernard Walther played the “ Barnyard ”
composition at Wanganui (M.L.), and a local paper ex-
pressed regret that he should descend from the classic to
the vulgar, so to speak. Next night Walther’s co. gave a
silver-coin show, and after gazing sorrowfully at the array
of battered threepenny-bits, he said, “ Ze papier take
exception to me playing ze ‘ Barnyard ’ piece on Saturday.
Quite right. I should huff pahformed him to-night, und
I spek so : Ladies and shentlemens, I will now play you ze
threepenny piece to match so genairous contributions and
zen I make ze quadruped with ze four legs spek. Bah !
ze small silver coin ! ”
Colonel Mapleson, recently deceased in London, was
the last of the old impresarios—speculative, sanguine,
buoyant, and always on the eve of making a great coup to
retrieve himself. His line of business is now run by syn-
dicates.
Charles Arnold went over to Maoriland the other
Wednesday for the purpose of protecting his plays against
pirates in that country. Arnold’s copyright case in
Queensland was easily successful, but it cost the actor-
manager close on £5OO. Law may be the proper, re-
spectable and orthodox remedy, but still the fact remains
that £5OO is a lot of money, and Arnold could have hired
the push to sandbag the pirates for a tenner.
Kaiser Bill has perpetrated a play which, says The
Week End, is “ as bad as the best of machine dramatists
has turned out at his worst moment.” William is an
assiduous play-goer, and is given to summoning mummers
to the Imperial box between the acts in order that he may
tell them how to play their parts. Apropos: when the
first Napoleon once gave some advice to Cherubini, the
composer told him to confine his attention to mere war—
about which he did know something !
August Manns, the long-haired German who conducted
all the concerts and Handel Festivals at the Crystal
Palace (Eng.) for 45 years, has retired from his billet.
Musical people admired Manns for what he did with his
orchestras at the Cockney resort, whilst the multitude
loved him for the way in which he did it —from their
point of view. They saw his back hair waving furiously,
and were convinced that Manns was “ all right.” Manns
was a good conductor with long hair, but it is better to
be a bad conductor with long hair than a good one with
the hair short.
The Leisure Hour publishes a gorgeous account of the
annual Day of Sacrifice proceedings at the Moorish capi-
tal, where the Sultan, Abdul Aziz, is the first person to
kill a sheep and' consider himself cleansed of twelve-
months’ sin by the blood of the mutton. Morocco con-
tinues to present a fairy-tale aspect to the journalist and
comic opera writer, but The Bulletin notes, with a sigh,
the hard unpicturesque fact that 16 pairs of boots for
Sultan A. Aziz aforesaid have been made to order by one
Keily, of Melbourne, and are about to be shipped to the
brown potentate. The Sultan’s chamberlain, or some-
thing, is a son of the late George Verdon, and brother to
recently deceased Reggie Verdon, secretary of Melb.
Savage Glub. Verdon, in Africa, has seemingly been dis-
cussing the boots question with the white-robed sovereign
of the kaids and sheiks. Anyway, the Sultan desired his
Australian employee to order some royal lace-ups, elastic
sides, and bluchers from the same little factory where ho
got his own boots the last time he tripped to Melbourne,
the order came in a letter to poor Reggie Verdon who
didn’t live to see it executed, and now the Bourke-streefc
bootmaker prays invariably that Abdul Aziz is a good
mark as regards the bill. His Nibs has a No. 11 foot,
which runs into a lot of leather. “Morocco Bound ” was
revived at a London theatre the other week. The Agent-
General for Vic. ought to wait upon the manager and in-
cite him to introduce a topical song bearing on Melbourne
trade with the Imperial Court at Morocco. The Grand
Vizier might warble somewhat as follows :
Australia is advancing in the Commerce and the Arts ;
The Kangaroo wants catching—he’s a bounder when he
The Cornstalk storms the kopjes, and the Boer he slyly
shoots .
Whilst / stalk round Morocco—with my corns in Keuy s
boots.
(Now, be aisy, ye galoots !)
The countrymen of Keily
Pot the Boer, so slim and wily,
Whilst I’m conversing drily
In my styley Melbourne boots.”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Champion Stakes race was satisfactory-
only in the fact that the two highest “class” performers
ran away from their three mediocre opponents at the
finish. La Carabine’s victory, though absurdly incon-
sistent with recent “ form,” was in strict accordance with
the title and traditions of the classic 3-mile event. The
champion prize never falls to a common horse. Parthian,
in 1900, is about the least distinguished name on the roll
of winners for 20 years past, and Parthian, who had no
Older horses to beat, was the best 8-year-old of his year
after MerriWee had turned rogue. La Carabine was not
the first racehorse of quality to win the Champion after
having been ignominiously beaten on the previous
Saturday.
Nevertheless, Rupert Clarke’s great mare was
the first to accomplish a remarkable reversal of form
Without exciting critioal comment in the Melbourne
dailies. On the morning after the race one turned in vain
to the “ organs of public opinion” for some explanation
bf the difference between La Carabine’s running in the
Essendon Stakes (1) miles) and the Champion Stakes.
The difference was an awful caution to punters. On the
Saturday, it was not only Wakeful who got away from
La Carabine in a fast-run race. Everybody expected the
flying mare to beat the staying mare over 1 j miles, but,
leaving Wakeful out of the question, it seemed certain
that La Carabine, if fit to race at all, should have been
equal to beating Barbarossa for the second-place money
(6200). Therefore, when both Barbarossa and Limber
had passed La Carabine in the last two furlongs of the
Essendon Stakes, the mare’s chance of recovering her
“ form ” in a three-mile race five days later looked abso-
lutely hopeless. Melb. Age said of her Saturday’s failure i
“ La Carabine made her reappearance in this race, and
ran well for a mile, but could not pace it with the first
three at the finish,” The Argus casually referred to the
lnare’s “ leg” as a sufficient reason for her sick perform-
ance.
Note what the Akgus had to say about the
Champion race, where La Carabine passed Barbarossa at
the end of a mile, and subsequently “lost” him. “It
took 3min. Bsec. to run the first mile and a half. After
that the pace was a cracker, the concluding half of the
journey being run in 2min. 35fsee. Wakeful ran past
Haymaker at the home turn, but in the straight was cut
down by La Carabine, who won easily from Mr. Mac-
donald’s mare.” Nowhere any reference to the Essendon
Stakes, in which La Carabine “ran well for a mile, but
could not pace it with the first three at the finish.” The
betting showed that Rupert Clarke and trainer Scobie
didn’t anticipate the victory of La Carabine, but the fact
of there being no suspicious circumstances attaching to
the surprise didn’t make it any the less unsatisfactory
from the public’s point of view. The Ballarat mare is an
old favorite of the public ; they knew her to be a game,
honest racer, and a great stayer, but her performance in
the Essendon Stakes had misled them as to her condition.
Why this respectable newspaper silence re the misleading
“ form ”of La Carabine in the Essendon Stakes ? Had
she belonged to a bookmaker instead of a baronet we
would have heard some remarks on the matter.
Time (smin. 43|sec.) for the Champion was
slow—2osecs. slower than the record—but the pace even
in the first half of the journey was not so easy that
Wakeful could make light of the concluding 12 furlongs’
sprint. The distance found her out. The daughter of
Nightmare got a pain in the head seemingly when it was
all over, and she staggered for a moment as though one
of her legs had given way. This was the climax of the
stable’s bad luck. Aurous ran her last race in the New-
market, and because she was disabled Revenue went for
the Australian Cup, and because he has come to grief
Wakeful was started for the Champion, only to get
second money at the cost of an awful gruelling. Luck is
consistently fick e. The Bulletin of March Ist, said :
“The Y.R.O. autumn meeting is such a pronounced
picnic— 1 on paper ’—for Mr. Macdonald’s three brilliant
performers that it almost seems advisable to predict a
series of upsets for those good things. The unexpected
happens so often to the owner on whom the sun shone
hot and strong a week previously.”
* » * *
La Carabine’s two successive wins of Champion
Stakes equal the record accomplished by Zoe in 1860-1.
Mormon, Ladybird, Strop, First King, Commotion, and
Ca bine each got home twice, but not in successive years.
Since Strop’s score (’69) La Carabine is the first aged nag
to win. In *7l Romula won, but during the 31 subsequent
years Quiver, who dead-heated with Wallace, and La
Carabine are the only names of mares in the winning list.
La Carabine was foaled in ’94, and, beginning when 2yrs.
old, has been going strong ever since. Present owner
secured her for 1200 gs. two years ago, and won it all back
over and over again in stakes alone.
* * * *
Wakeful appropriated the All-aged Stakes on
Saturday, and, with 101 b. penalty up, ran third in Loch
Plate same afternoon. The All-aged feat was an eye-
opener. Besides absolutely romping away from a field of
good ones, Wakeful traversed the mile in 1.40), and with
something to spare, too. Australasian record—jointly
shared in by Wakeful and Djin Djin—is only |sec. better.
In Britain they have speedier figures. Caiman put up
1.33 1-5, and Sir Hercules 1.34 2-5. The ’Murkan fastest
is Salvator’s 1.35£.

Y.R.O. autumn gathering facts Wakeful,

V.R.C. dropped £3lOO over the 1901 meeting,
and ’tis anticipated that it will find a like shortage when
balancing up this time. Added money present year was
£450 in excess of that given last year, and though gate-
takings on latest occasion were larger they may only
make up the increased outlay.

Sydney Cup burdens are headed by La Cara-
bine s 9.13, which she richly merits and could, unques-
tionably on her best form, hump to the front at the right
end - Wakeful’s 9.7 won’t anchor her. Sequence is hardly
worth considering at the distance (2m.). Blue Metal’s
9.5 was well earned by his Victorian achievements. Sir
Foote and Aurous (9.0) are hardly liberally dealt with.

won tbe in love with his burden. Lucknow (8.5),

tous (7.12), Pelissier (7.10), Caravel (7.8), and Ace of
Diamonds (7.9) are not overtaxed—particularly the latter,

stable, has also been favored (7.4), and will cause Moses
generally many a qualm should the money come from the
right quarter and he lines up carrying Wootton’s confi-
dence. Of the lightweights, Lulworth, Tillimby, Lady
Darling and one or two others strike the mind as best,
but the race must be run to suit them.

The Newmarket Handicap “form” was con-
firmed by the victory of Andorra in the Bourke Handicap
three days later. Andorra had only 21bs. more than the
weight he carried into fifth place in the Newmarket, and
getting away with the lead as before the gelding won all
the way. Considering that he had none of the “ cracks ”
to compete against this time Andorra was a pretty good
thing on paper, but the “ books ” laid 10’s and 12’s to 1
against him.

Dear Bulletin.—Had a peculiar experience in
connection with the Newmarket just decided. The night
before the race I suffered a severe attack of nocturnal
horse, and dreamt that Bonnie Chiel won, with Aurous
second, and Sir Foote third; but on awakening found
myself head to foot in the bed, which, judging by the
result, was evidently the office to back mv dream re-
versed. But I didn’t; I backed Bonnie Chiel for a fair
stake —and lost.—R.D.
• * > * *
The moral of the Revenue case ought to be
obvious enough to horse-owners. Foul riding nowadays
is the rule rather than the exception, because the few
respectable jockeys are not sufficiently old and influential
to rank as monitors among* the low-class boA 7 s who go for
bumps and scrimmages whenever they see a chance of
interfering with a favorite. The V.R.C. stewards have
shown themselves a little less active and intelligent than
other stewards in trying to check these modern horrors
of , th ?,, 1 u rf ’ tbere fo r e it is “up against” the owners of
valuable horse-flesh to make the way of the transgressor
harder by reviving the old pace-maker system of racing,
which necessarily tends to spread the field and expose
the tactics of jockeys who are bent on mischief. Thirteen
horses started for the Australian Cup, and when
they had gone a mile of the journey the whole 13
jockeys were pulling at their horses’ heads. The slow
pace gave them all a chance to jostle and block one
another if they had been sailing under “ interference ”
orders. A “ bunched ” field racing around a circus course
means a considerable risk of unintentional bumping
and almost a certainty of trouble for the favorite if
trouble is down on the programme. Revenue was at the
mercy of circumstances when he came to grief in the
Australian Clip. To the present scribe it seemed a pr'e-
posterous thing that any horse should be backed at even
money to assert superior staying powers in a race where
there was no guarantee of a sound pace, and the character
of the jockeys was mostly worthless. Revenue got
bumped pretty badly. Then he got bumped very badly,
and seemingly this second “ mishap ” was so serious that
the favorite wrenched a pastern in the effort to avoid
falling. He came home-tenth—on three good legs and a
cronk one, whilst Blue Metal was beating Haymaker
ln Sisec., not the slowest time on record,
but lOsecs. slower than Dreamland’s race last year.
As the result of allowing a beautiful nag to run
entirely “on his own” in a field that was so long
bunched for want of pacemakers, the Macdonald stablS
may be said to have lost thousands of pounds in stakes
Revenue was reckoned good enough to win the Aus-
tralian oup, the Champion Stakes, and all the w.f.a.
races they might choose to send him for this season. And
now he is only a crippled gelding reduced, probably, to
the mere value of his hide. The odd thing was that they
didn t reckon him too good a thing to send on a long-
journey without taking some measures towards ensuring
a fair go for their money.
» • * *
Some people profess to know that the New-
market Handicap tragedy was not the result of an
accident.
* * * *
“ Tattersall ” seems to have designed a way to
drive a herd of racehorses through the provisions of the
Postal Act which aim at suppressing- his sweeps. He will
ask clients to send their sweep-money addressed to them-
selves, care of the secretary of Hobart Stock Exchange.
Thus, Josiah Tarnpley, of Richmond, Vic., would send £1
to Josiah Tamplev, care of the secretary aforesaid. In
due course, “ Tatt.” would get the letter and Tampley
the ticket. It seems as though this device might prove
temporarily successful, and there is already a heated
discussion as to whether the Federal Government should
not have another try at the work of suppression. Of
course it may yet declare “c/o the secretary, Hobart
Stock Exchange,” a prohibited address, and there will be
more trouble.
* * * •
The gifts of the Sydney Cricket Ground trustees
to the departing English cricketers were handsome
photographs of the ground during the fourth test-match.
But the gifts were only for the amateur members of the
team. Garnett and Robson, who did not take part in a
single test-match, were favored with a present apiece, while
Hayward, Braund, Lilley, and the other professionals, on
whom the burden chiefly fell, Were ignored. The N.S.W.
cricket authorities have an ignoble reputation for always
doing the wrong thing in the wrong way.
* ♦ * *
Figures for the five tests show the Englishmen
to have been beaten all along the line. Hill and Arm-
strong came out with a considerably better batting aver-
age than Maclaren. Duff was 10 runs per innings in front
of Braund, the second man on the visitors’ list, and 13
runs ahead of Hayward. Syd. Gregory beat Tyldesley’s
average, Darling was better than Lilley, and so on.
Howell and Kelly, the 11th and 12th of the Australians,
were more serviceable than any of the five (out of twelve)
men who formed the English batting “ tail.” Of the four
English bowlers—Barnes, Gunn, Blythe, and Braund—
the three latter are outclassed on their Australian figures
by Noble and Trumble, who, between them, took more
Wickets at considerably less cost. Barnes’s average of
17T5 runs for 19 wickets is a little in front of Noble’s, but
then Noble took 32 wickets in all. Braund bowled
just 10 more balls than Trumble throughout the
tests, but Trumble took seven more wickets, and the cost
of each wicket was 15 runs less than Braund’s price.
Nevertheless the bowling results are not favorable to
Australia’s chance of winning test-matches in England
this year. Their batting should save Olem. Hill and Go.
from defeat on a good wicket in a three-days’ game, but
their powers of attack have never been less promising l than'
now. Noble and Trumble loom painfully large in the
averages. They are the only two bowlers who went
to England with E. Jones, Howell, Laver and M'Leod in
’99. Jones and Howell are included with them in the
present team, and Saunders and Armstrong take the
places of Laver and M‘Leod. If the strongest English
teams don’t make big scores against the Australians this
scribe will be astonished. The batting failure of Maclaren’s
crowd was due to something besides Trumble and Noble.
• * * *
“ A. 8.” : That Barnes’s accident meant a serious
loss to Maclaren’s team is shown by the averages of Eng-
lish bowlers in the test-matches. Before his mishap
Barnes’s figures were 19 wickets for 323—an average of 17
—the best figures for the series. Gunn’s average was
about 25 ; Braund’s and Blythe’s about 30. Still the Aus-
tralian team never really collared the English bovvling.
The}' only scored 350 once, and had the visiting batting
strength come up to its paper r iputation, the t ;am would
have gone back—Barnes or no Barnes—with a far more
pleasant record. Yet Maclaren’s excuse will always be
that he was without his best bowler in three matches out
of the four he lost.
* • * *
“Adelaide”: Re the recent test-matches.
Seventeen men were tried in the five matches. Of these
M‘Leod, Laver, Eady, and Travers are dropped, and the
other 13 go to England, plus Garter. Seven Welshmen
were played, five Victorians, three from S.A., and two
from Tas., the proportion among those who go to England
reading eight, three, two, and one. The team for the
first match was made up entirely from the last Australian
XL, including all the six bowlers. The batting failing,
Duff and Armstrong replaced M'Leod and Laver, and
stayed in right through the season. At Adelaide, M'Leod
came in agai nin place of Jones. Fourth match M'Leod
and Jones both gave way to Saunders, and Hopkins
replaced the absent Darling. Both were successful. Last
match, Howell was at last dropped, and, Saunders being
hurt, Travers and Eady got in. Neither got much show,
Trumble and Noble naturally seizing the big chance
which rain gave the bowlers. Selectors’ choice was
always justified in the picking of new men, but not
in sticking to old ones. Public is thus justified in asking
whether Jones and Howell, quite useless here, will really
be useful in England?
• • * »
The test-matches in England are all to begin at
11.30 on the first day, and 11.0 on the others. Stumps
drawn at 6.30. This will give in the three days almost as
much playing time as four ordinary days here (12 to 6).
Hawke, Bainbridge, and M'Gregor select the teams, the
last-named replacing Grace. Yorkshire, Lancashire,
Lord’s, and the Oval get one match each, and the first of
all (at the end of May) is given to Birmingham, instead of
Nottingham.

Saturday’s round of Sydney first-grade cricket
was the penultimate one, and proved interesting, the
outcome being that, near though the end is, the
winning team takes some picking. Central Cumberland
accounted for Paddington, North Sydney settled Leich-
hardt-Balmain. Paddington, Leiohhart-Balmain, and
Sydney are on the top rung now—6 points each—with
only another round to see out, though everything points
to the probability of a decider. Burwood, against Waver-
ley, notched 361, but t’others came strong, accumulating
349 for 8 wickets before light failed and caused an excep-
tionally even draw, for Waverley still had 13 to get and
two wickets to fall. O. Gregory, for Waverley, compiled
162. Sydney had a soft snap in Redfern—94 runs to the
good on the first innings. Paddington, deprived of
Trumper, Noble, and Kelly, was in a parlous plight.
With Central Cumberland’s declared closed innings of
five for 329 to go against, Paddington only hit up 240, to
which E. C. Noble contributed 84. North Sydney’s win
from Leichhardt-Balmain was a gift—six for 389 to 216.
W. S. Duff (brother of the Australian Eleven man and con-
sidered by many judges equally good) scored 120.
G. R. Clarke got 86 (run out). In the Glebe- Sydney
O.G. Trustees’ team match, former gathered 265 for five
wickets to latter’s 204. Gallagher (Glebe) was not out
with 103 down to him.
* • * *
In the Paddington-Central Cumberland match,
Pye, so consistently ignored by the selectors for big
events, made 121 and took 7 wickets for 75 runs.
* * * *
Yankee bruiser Scanlon and Sydney heavy-
weight Tim Nolan fought at Sydney Gaiety A.C. Monday
nighc and supplied a depressing show. Scanlon, slow
and clumsy, jabbed and poked gentle lefts and jolted
rights, which would have been mere thistledown had Nolan
been in condition. But he was a mass of quivering tissue
that hung in lumps over hips and ribs, and he puffed pain-
fully -after every effort. His operations consisted of
swinging rights and lunging lefts that rarely reached
within feet of their mission. Scanlon stood off, plugging
straight, mostly on Nolan’s forearm and glove—which
were kept close across his face—but often on the mouth
eyes, and nose, but beyond grazing the skin slightly he left
no trace. Punches on the kidneys made Nolan wriggle
and look as if he’d like to jump on the puncher, while In
lumbered his apparent 14st. of bulk round the square
and crouched a pronounced hunchback. At the end of
the sixth round the “wipe’ was elevated in Nolan’s
corner and Scanlon scored his second successive win :
that against Starlight a few weeks ago being the other. ’
* * * *
Champion ’rastler R. J. Scott lately took on
two men (J. Claims and J. Trainor), both splendid sneci-
mens of physical development, at a Dunedin (M.L)
athletic tournament. Style, catch-as-catch-can, and the
pair were unable to floor Scott within the prescribed
seven minutes. Scott is wonderfully quick on his feet,
and uses his long arms effectively. He released himself
by a sudden wrench from the clutches of both his oppo-
nents and collared the gold medal.
* * * *
Victorians carried away nearly all the money
from the Adelaide Wheel Race meeting—only two races
going to S.A. men. It was a contest of Victorians on
S. A. ground. A batch of well set up and fast second-class
men from the Cabbage Garden caught the S.A. handi-
cappers napping. As a result Cameron, who soon must
be classed amongst the first-rankers, fled home with the
£lOO Wheel Race prize, whilst Rolfe snatched the £3O
second money easily enough from Champness, S.A.’s
champion 50-mile road-racer.
* * * *
Cabled that two-mile bike supremacy of Otago
(M.L.) was, on Saturday, won by Palmer with half-a-
wheel to the good from Connell in 4.52 2-5. At same
meet Plugger Bilmartin gathered in the mile honors by
only Sin. from Sutherland in 2.17, but latter turned the
tables in the three-mile, getting home half-a-wheel ahead
of Martin in 7.22 2-5. Wire says the race looked good for
Martin, but he dropped back three yards from the post.
This was a two-distance championship. Martin and
Sutherland whacked the money.
* • * *
Cyclist Sutherland, who races in M.L. until the
next Austral, was flattened out by a query from a Pig
Island interviewer who was anxious to know if racing
was “ straight” in Australia. Sutherland struggled with
the question a good deal. “The special conditions of
cycle racing,” he said, ‘‘ make it necessary for various
sections in a race to combine for mutual interest, and a
particular rider’s chance may be sacrificed accordingly.
The Australian papers greatly exaggerate this feature
and its tendency to produce cronk running. As a matter
of fact the result is entirely to prevent" loafing, and to
provide exceptionally fast racing.” Sutherland might
also have added that riders often voluntarily sacrifice
their chances when asked in a nice quiet boodle tone of
voice to fall behind in favor of a well-backed “ pea,” a
little act of graciousness which isn’t forgotten when their
day arrives for a flutter on their own.
* » * *
Sydney bowls pennant tourney is nearing the
end. Saturday witnessed Redfern and Ashfield drop
out, defeated by Petersham and City, as named. Latter
won easily by 46, but former just barely squeezed through,
with only two to the good. Three left in are City and
Petersham (undefeated), and Waverley, with one licking
against it. Judges opined that Redfern or Petersham
would win right out, and the result of their meeting
justified the doubt as to which was the better team. Had
Petersham got Saturday’s match with Waverley, that
would have ended the “B” tussle, but as Waverley
triumphed (with a lead in every link, arid 31 in the full
totals), there’ll be another fight ’tween the same sides
this Saturday.
* * * *
S.A. tennis supremacy contest revealed unsus-
pected talent, notwithstanding that Ballarat crack Heath
wrested the championship from Bowen, previous holder
for years. Record entries resulted. Hitherto unknown
men—Turner and Murray—ran Heath into five sets before
he won the singles premiership. This is the second time
other Staters have seized the S.A. blue riband. Kearney
did it two years ago. Heath was runner -up
last year when Bowen squelched him in three straight
sets. Heath’s success isn’t quite what it seems
owing to the fact that Bowen was decidedly ill and had
been medically attended on the eve of the challenge
round. Heath, in conjunction with J. R. Baker, also
secured top-rung position in the doubles championship.
Baker and Bowen, together, have carried this event six
times. Though Heath had previously shaped well in
inter-State tennis at Sydney and Melbourne, these are his
first achievements in the absolutely best class. Westralian
reps., Kelsey and Greayer, failed to rise above mediocrity
and were put out of the hunt during the initial batch of
heats. Home champion, Miss Payne, got through the
ladies’ events brilliantly, gathering three championships
through particularly good volleying. Victorian ladies
were badly beaten. Miss Payne also annexed the Ladies’
Single Handicap, thus repeating what she did in the Mel-
bourne inter-State tourney last November.
A Pathetic Parting.
A writer recently described the light-hearted
manner in which modern people say good-bye
at wharf or station. He tells how people set
out on a long pilgrimage with the prospect of
never seeing their friends again, and they say
farewell as if it were quite a jolly affair after
all. There are still partings at which anguish
keeps the tongue silent and the cheek wet.
But there are partings which should be merry,
partings which we should celebrate like a
wedding. It is a pleasure to chronicle such
partings, and consequently, we make public the
parting of Mrs. S. Aust, who resides in Hornsby-
street, Maldon, Vic., when she said a long good-bye
to headaches and nervousness. Her letter reads
as follows: —“I have great pleasure in testi-
fying to the good results I have obtained from
the use of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. For
some time I have suffered with severe pains in
the head, also nervousness, and after taking two
bottles of these pills, I had great relief, and I
can conscientiously give them a good word
wherever I go.” Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills are a perfect blood purifier, and a positive
cure for biliousness, indigestion, constipation,
headaches, sallow complexion, liver and kidney
troubles, piles, pimples, boils and blotches, and
for female ailments. Sold by chemists and
storekeepers. Is. l|d per bottle, or six bottles
6s. 6d. Sole proprietors, The W. H. Comstock
Co., Ltd. (Australasian Depot) 58 Pitt-street,
Sydney. Packed in amber bottles, and the full
name blown thereon.

South Australian 8-oar supremacy on Port
River, Saturday, brought out three crews and some sen-
sations. Mercantile and Adelaide fouled immediately
after “ go,” and a fresh start saw “ Mercs ” fill and swamp.
Port 8 just had time to run their shell ashore to save
sinking, and, after up-ending, went after Adelaide, whose
cox. did his best to run a ketch down. Meanwhile
“Ports” were coming up with every ounce on, but only
arrived in time to finish 1J lengths away.
» * * *
“Raupo” writes: N.S.W. swimmer R. Cavill,
before going to Wanganui, caused a pretty considerable
amount of wonder among swimming circles here (Wel-
lington, M.L.) by beating our crack, F. Roberts, with his
legs tied together at the ankles (Cavill’s legs—not
Roberts’s). Distance, 50 yards. No time was taken, as
no one realised soon enough that the thing was meant
seriously, but Roberts’ time for the distance is 29 to 30
seconds, and he was all out. Apparently Cavill can sprint
almost as well with his legs tied as loose.
* * » *
“ H C.C.” : In a Maoriland city the other day,
when some betting cases were brought on, the local!
Bench was packed with J’s.P., many of whom were-
habitual shirkers of duty. The cases were remanded to
appear before the magistrate, whereupon, on the
appointed day, two justices also appeared and claimed
their right to seats on the Bench. In the first case the
magistrate said that a majority of the Bench had decided
to dismiss the charge. He made no disguise of the fact
that he wasn’t the majority. As regards the second case,
the magistrate said that on his ovvn initiative he would
have committed for trial, but again a majority of the
Bench had decided to dismiss the charge. Another
betting-shop was raided. Every witness refused to say
whether he had or had not betted with accused, on the
plea of risk of self-incrimination. So the case went
slowly. At last a cabby stated, without objection, that
he had made no bets, and it was only after long delays
and many inquiries as to whether he was incriminating
himself dr not that he admitted he had just dropped in.
There is no other kind of case where it is so diffi-
cult to get a conviction, and there is no other kind
of case where the Bench and the witness-box smell
so highly of corruption, and the desire to pour Condy’s
fluid over the lot is so overwhelming. The tote has no
secret fund with which it can pollute the fount of justice
—and possibly the fount rather likes being polluted.
Clem. Hill completes his 25th year, March 18th.
Two of his brothers are playing in Adelaide club cricket,
and a third is in the Prince Alfred College first XI. whence
Clem, stepped straight into Inter-State cricket, and
thence, with but a short interval, into the Australian XI.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —I took a share in Tatt.’s sweep
some time ago. I fully expected to get, at least, £10,000
out of the affair; and had made up my mind as to what
I’d do with it all. The local hospital was to benefit by
£500—there was to be a new ward called the McGinty
Ward. Each of the seven churches would get £20 ; all
the youngsters were to go to college and be doctors—
girls and boys alike. Also, a house, that 1 had myself
designed half-a-dozen times, was to be built, at a cost of
£1500 ; and a considerable amount would go for new
false teeth and hair-dye. Then I was to marry the house-
keeper ; and look young, and be respectable for the term
of mv natural life. Further, in the event of not captur-
ing the ten thou., I had carefully considered what I’d do
if I got, say, the £100 cash prize. I even spent, in my
mind, the modest fiver, should that paltry amount happen
along. I didn’t win anything, but I had a glorious time
anticipating, and shall never be without a share again,
whilst I can possibly raise the needful. I never use
stimulants now—never require ’em—and have, thanks to
Tattersall, given up drink altogether. I reckon on
winning another £10,000 or so shortly ; but am going on
a new tack altogether this time. The programme includes
a trip right through America, taking Kamschatka on the
return journey. \Vhj r should such cheap fun be taken
from me?—Yours, etc., McGinty.

“ Scrap” : Didn’t like M’Growan-Tweedie fizzle
last week from the start. M‘Gowan rushed and hit too
recklessly considering he knew how clever Tweedie was.
Time after time Tweedie could have smashed any part of
the Melbourne mannikin’s mug, but didn’t—simply
jumped back and fended M'Gowan’s left and right swings,
which were hurled as if only a suspended ball had to be
hit at. Once Tweedie’s nose evidently itched severely,
and he stopped operations to rub it while t’other con-
siderately waited. The thing wore a most unwholesome
appearance. Further, Tweedie never trained fairly and
squarely an hour, and had his “ good time ” at night as if
the fight was months ahead ; the probable outcome seem-
ingly worried him little.
Apropos the row re the proclamation by
the O’Seeullivan Government of St. Patrick’s day
as a N.S.W. public holiday. When last year a
holiday was proclaimed on the 17th of Ireland the
cheerfullv-aceepted pretext was that it was
desired to honor the gallant Patlanders who were
so bravely fighting the battles of the Empire in
S’Africa . Well, what’s the matter with the
gallant Irishmen now ? Aren t they still bravely
fighting in S’Africa (and moonlighting in Tip-
perary)? Fact is, Australia was blood-drunk last
year and it isn’t quite so blood-drunk now.
Judge Heydon, of N.S.W., made a great
and angry row in court last Monday because he
found that in a case heard by him during the
previous week—a case where the jury had
apparently embittered him by failing to convict
all the accused—one juryman was a man who had
served 18 months in goal for a matter relating to
a cheque and who was still under surety to be of
good behaviour. Judges are much addicted to
strange breakings-out of this sort. In the case
in question the juryman was summoned to attend.
He would have been heavily fined by Judge
Hevdon if he hadn’t attended. But when he did
attend, and the jury (of which he was only a unit)
didn’t decide as Judge Heydon thought it should
have done, His ’Onah took the opportunity to
relate in open court the story of his past, and
complained of his being on the jury. It was no
fault of his that he was there, and His ’Onah
ought to have dropped (privately) on the people
whose fault it was. The course he took looked
like a sour, mean, revengeful, small-minded
business.

Quite a number of Australian non-Jingoes
will sympathise with General Methuen in his
latest misfortune. The cable states that he, with
a number of officers and men and four guns, has
been captured by De la Rey after a fight in
which 30 British were killed and wounded,
Methuen himself being wounded in the thigh,
Years ago “Paul” Methuen was a constant
visitor to the Savage Club, London, where
he foregathered with many Australians, all
of whom fell in love with his charming
manners and manly and generous nature. His
captor, De la Rey, is universally reputed a brave
and chivalrous soldier, and the sort of man from
whom a wounded foe might expect nothing but
kindness or- °
The “Spanish Prisoner” swindler, who
has been exploiting the fools of the world for years
past, has started a new game, showing much of the
wisdom of the serpent. He invites aid on shares
for the recovery of some £Bo,ooo —stolen and
planted, but at present ungetatable by the
advertiser. Fools who bite at this bait won’t dare
to squeal—the money being admittedly stolen.

Fremantle. It is believed that his mind was
deranged by trouble in connection with the Cool-
gardie water scheme.” No wonder, poor man !
The Victorian State Government is going
to sue Dr. Wollaston, the head of the Customs
Department, and Attorney-General Deakin for
income-tax, by way of deciding whether Federal
officials have any right to claim exemption. The
question is one of importance to all the States,
which will lose considerably if the Federal officials
succeed in their sordid attempt to avoid bearing
their share of the public burdens.

STAFFORD —SMITH.—On the 30th January, at 32
York-place, Parkville, by the father of the bride,
Edwin Stafford, of Meredith, to Caroline (Carrie),
eldest daughter of the Rev. R. J. Smith, 8.A., of
Wentworth, N.S.W., granddaughter of late Richard
Pennefather, Esq., Balinira, Co. Tip., great-grand-
daughter of late Mrs. T. H. Going, Myalive, Glebe,
Co. Tip., and great-great-granddaughter of late
Honble. Walter Shirley, brother of the late Lord
Ferrers, Leicestershire, Eng.

relations who came from ‘ ‘ Co. Tip.” I

The remedy—Morris’s Imperial Eye Ointment
—is acknowledged the only certain remedy; never

On Thursday night last as many of the
Sydney detectives as could ease off chasing the
nimble-footed burglar and the slippery dispenser
of drinks after hours, assembled at Sydney
Trocadero Cafe to eat a farewell dinner with Supt.
Camphin, who goes to Europe and America on
six months’ leave of absence before retiring on a
pension. Mr. Camphin was presented by the
headquarters staff with a pearl and ruby scarfpin
with an appropriate letter. The general staff of
society watchdogs presented him with a fin a
ivory-handled, silver-mounted malacca cane, with
his monogram engraved ; a silver cigar-case, which
Detective Roche filled before passing it over, and
a gold-mounted amber-mouthpiece in a silver
cover. After which the usual speeches were made,
and toasts given, and Supt. Camphin floated out
of active service.
“ Banjo” Paterson is a man who knows
much about horses, having worshipped them
from his youth upwards. Also, he was in General
French’s famous dash to Kimberley and the wild
race to head-off Cronje at Koodoosrand, in which
two operations French’s force rode 10,000 horses,
more or less, to death ; and therefore Paterson
ought to know something about what sort of horse
is wanted by the army. And Banjo says
straight that the kind of horse that is required,
the enduring, long-lasting weight-carrier, is very
scarce here, and the fact that the War Office buys
most of its horses elsewhere is really a proof that
the W. O. knows something about its business.
It is a straightforward, uncompromising statement
which is not calculated to cause the Australian
horse - breeders’ collective head to swell, and
coming from the man who created Pardon, the
Son of Reprieve, it is impossible to do otherwise
than give some credenee to it.

‘ 0., from Maori 1 and :—“Remarkable bow
%noraut some people are concerning matters of common
knowledge. A man remarked, t’other day, that he had
