* • * •
The Premier jock of Northern Vic. is a half-
caste Mongolian. He is also a top-notch trainer. At re-
cent Echuca races he was much in evidence as owner,
trainer and rider. At the same meeting the hurdle race
was won by another half-caste. The local Ohows were
present in force, and backed the mounts of their“ semi-
coinpatriots ” every time.
* * * *
An item of grim humor comes from Maoriland.
A well-bred galloping “ frost ” who never won a race, and
cost his syndicate a pretty heap of coin, at last found his
way to an undertaker’s hearse, and lately pulled one of
his former owners along to his last home.
* • • *
There is a little racecourse near Hobart which
has got the name of “ the regatta ground ” —because the
races there are mostly pulling ones.
* * * *
Two cocksure individuals of horsey aspect
turned up with a demure-looking prad for a trotting
razzle in a somnolent M.L. town, but preserved a golden
silence’ as to the clock-breaking capacity of this “ dead
bird.” Their advent had been looked for, however, for
same night the moke vanished most mysteriously, and on
race morn the sight of two cursing individuals poking
into stables and searching wayside paddocks was remera-
bersome. The deceptive-looking flier was scratched one
race at a time in case it should turn up during the day,
but it hasn’t been visible since.
* » * *
Legislation is once again urged regarding the
control of stallions in M.L. This time, the wail comes
from the Stock Department. Govt. vets, say it is scan-
dalous that so many unsound horses should be allowed to
perpetuate. The Department recommends that the
annual examination of all horses intended for service
should be made compulsory ; licenses be issued, and pro-
hibition applied to those unlicensed. Spider-legged, ill-
bred weeds are plentiful, particularly in the so-called
hack-racing class. They are trained before they have
time to develop, and, suffering from an inherited weak-
ness, soon crack-up. England was, at one time, the land
of equine weeds and wrecks, but a bygone king, of ill-
favored memory, appointed inspectors, with power to
slaughter stallions and brood mares which were below
standard. But for that drastic proceeding, the English
cavalry-man would have had to walk after his enemy in
many a glorious campaign.
• * * *
The names bestowed by one M.L. racing man
on his gee-gees have a political flavor —Female Franchise,
Social Pest, Land Tax, etc. They are mostly descended
from a mare named Policy.
* * * *
“J.A.0.”: Re Mr. D. O’Connor’s recent sug-
gestion, in a Sydney paper, that “ Tattersall ” Adams
should transfer his “ consultations ” to Noumea. The
French Government would probably not permit it. They
stopped the parimutuel, the original totalisator, which
used to do a roaring business, particularly in Paris.
“ Tatt.” might have a better show in Samoa, where the
local Chamber, as reported in a British paper, has granted
a gambling concession, on Monte Carlo lines, for 99 years.
But seeing that the Federal Government has stopped the
letters of a Hamburg lottery man it would probably stop
“ Tatt’s.” also, wherever he went. What’s the good of
“ Tatt.” going anywhere if he couldn’t take his business
with him ?
* * * *
“ 11. D.” makes a suggestion : “Could not letters
to ‘ Tattersall’s’ be addressed ‘The Treasurer, Tas-
manian Govt., Hobart.’ The State Treasurer is a
verv honest man and could be trusted with the money.
The Federal Post Office could not refuse delivery of letters
addressed to the Tasmanian Govt., and it is a simple
matter of arrangement between the high contracting
parties.”
* * * t
N.S.W. show-jumping nag, Archie, died at
Penrith recently. He was 16 years old, had spent nine
in business, and won over £6OO in prizes as a hunter and
heavyweight hack. Well-known ’cross countryman Roots
always rode Archie.
• *
At a free and festive country race meet re
cently an obfuscated jock, cordially slapped the president
on the back and asked him how his blanky brother was.
(The brother was in gaol.) The indignant official hastily
summoned the stewards, who decided to disqualify the
unregenerate one for the meeting on account of his con-
dition. A minute later it was discovered that the
bibulous mannikin, who rode best that way, had been
engaged to ride the president’s two horses that day.
Resolution was hastily cancelled and an official told off to
keep the brief and hazy lout away from the pewter pot
until' he got through his two races, both of which he
won.
* * * *
The familiar bald and grey-whiskered motion,
urgihg that the tinkling tote should be laid aside for fire-
wood, Was duly proposed as per custom and carried with
great unanimity by M.L. Council of Churches lately.
How delegates to these councils would fill in time if they
hadfl’t the-tote and the “ abawminable ” liquor tariff to
babble about writer doesn’t know.
• * • *
The Albert Cricket Ground, Sydney. “A
beautiful field for seeing is this, made on the old-fashioned
dripping-pan model. I do not know an important cricket
venue to compare with it structurally. Everyone of the
nearly 40,000 present would get a good view of the
play.”—London Referee.
* * • *
Hollowing on Duff’s recent Melbourne success,
London Referee told its readers this :—“R. Duff, who
made the highest score in each innings for the Australians,
is a gurrev man, having been born at Thornton Heath.
He learnt his cricket on the Whitgift Grammar School
Ground, Croydon, his mentor being Thompson, an old
Surrey professional. Duff emigrated some six years
since.” Which is not at all bad iri face of the fact that
Duff first saw light in Sydney, and hasn’t been outside
this continent.
♦ * * *
Armstrong, the new Victorian batsman, by the
aid of not-out innings, just beats the consistent Clem.
Hill for best average in the test-matches. His aggregate
is 159, and his average 53. Clem’s aggregate is 521, and
his average 52, Hill has been wonderfully consistent this
season, and only wants 38 in the return S. A.-Englishmen
match to complete his 1000 for first-class matches. As
there are only 10 fixtures this will mean 100 per match.
* * * ■ *
Novice bruising inflictions are being perpetrated
In Sydney again. Promoters see in them a means of
making a sure “ bit ” because they cost little and may be
wretched over several weeks. The pug game is, through
this, getting a big set-back —which means killing the
proverbial goose. Nothing is more degrading than these
cheap and nasty competitions. Competitors come from
the holes and alleys of the city, untrained, unwashed,
and un-anythinged, with as much idea of boxing as of
flying, and a majority are there only to get the “half-
caser ” beer-money offered to everyone toeing the mark.
Unkempt, pot-bellied, dirty-legged, sockless gutter-
bullies are the rule among the entrants, though here
and there one finds an exception, and marvels how he
got to that level. Novice tournaments in the past de-
veloped good men—Johns, Bob Turner and eo.-but
material bias degenerated since, and isn’t likely to im-
prove with years, judging from appearances.
* • * *
Peculiar that since Nov., 1900, W. Turnbull—
frequently dubbed premier amateur cyclist of N.S.W.-
hasn’t won a championship ; yet every test to guide in
the selection of a representative for other State meetings
has resulted in Turnbull being successful. Throughout
this season he has filled second place in the half, one,,
five and ten mile amateur supremacies of the continent,
and occupied the same position in the five-mile Common-
wealth Championship last January. What luck !
# m * *
Superstition that contestants in long distance
Pike struggles lose much tissue is knocked out .by results
of recent six days’ grind in New York. Details are in-
teresting : Winner M‘ achern scaled on finishing, 1581 b. ;
he slept 14 hours. Walthur lost 111 b., slept 12 hours, ate
three meals daily of chops, chicken, rice, oranges, apples,
and grapes, and drank milk and broth. Otto Maya
(1741 b.) gained 111 b. He had 36 hours’ sleep. Wilson
made 41b., slept 34 hours, ate chops, chicken, boiled and
fried eggs, and drank in one week 12 dozen bottles of
prepared milk and eight dozen bottles of Vichy water.
Munro never lost an ounce, and slept 6 hours, ate chicken
porridge, steaks, chops, eggs and celery. He drank 9
bottles of beef-tea and 36 bottles of Vichy water. Bab*
cock had only one sleep of half-an-hour, and gained 21b.
Turville gained 31b. He slept but 4 hours, ate oysters,
steaks, chops, ham, cheese ; and he drank four dozen
bottles of milk, 8 bottles of ginger ale, 6 bottles of sherry,
6 bottles of soup and 15 svphons of soda.
* * * *
Although M.L. League of Wheelmen doesn’t
officially recognise road races it takes more than a fatherly
interest in them and insists that snowy-white amateur
winners must accept trophies as prizes. So that when a
hamatoor gets home in front of a cash man he gratefully
carts away a tin mug, or something not near the value of
the advertised prize, while the runner-up pockets golden
sovereigns with the bald head of our beloved monarch
thereon. The Amateur Athletic Association now decrees,
on the other hand that an amateur competing with pro-
fessionals forfeits his status.
* * * *
A pair of pants was offered as a “trophy ” for an
amateur athletic event at a recent M.L. sports meet. More
serviceable than a tin mug, no doubt, but surely the con-
trolling association will not sanction such prizes in future.
Amateurs have enough prize shoddy piled on to them as
t is.
* * * *
Peerless ped. M‘Lachlin put up a hot 220yds.
run at Hawkes Bay (M.L.) Caledonian Gathering on Feb.
19th—21 2-ssec. from scratch, beating Harry Hutchens’
21 4-ssec., and running l-ssec. under Wefer’s amateur
record in U.S.A. In the 440yds., same day, M'Lachlin
ran home in the clinking time of 49sec. dead—fsfc.
behind Buttery’s world’s (pro.) record, and lsec. worse
than M. W. Long’s brilliant amateur run.
* * * *
“Via”: Re chess-playing cranks, the asylums
are full of them. At Goodna (Q.) lunatic asylum there
are a number of patients, troubled with a “ rush of rats ”
in other directions, who play the game for all it is worth.
Apropos years back, the writer lived in the same Brisbane
hash-house with Whistler, the British champion. When
he took an evening doze on the verandah his fingers
strayed over his lap all the time, and I could tell when he
shifted a knight in his dreams by the peculiar sidelong
shift. There’s the bicycle face and the tennis elbow ;
why not the chess.v brain ? j

The champion cool hand has been dis- '
covered at Willesden Green, London. A husband
suing for divorce from his wife put in the follow-
ing letter as evidence:
Dearest Arthur. —I have gone away with Gray. Forgive
me, and always think of your loving wife. Rose.
“Dearest Arthur” was “always to think” of his
“loving wife, Rose” —and Rose was then away
with the other man !
The Sydney reformed Council’s garbage-
destructor will have two useful adjuncts. The
first is the lethal chamber for diseased and useless
dogs, which are now either knocked on the head
by the police-barracks butcher, or “scragged ” at
the back of the Town Hall by the municipal dog-
Ketch. The dogs will in the future have eutha-
nasia, by chloroform spray and then carbonio
acid gas. The second adjunct of the garbage-
destructor is a disinfecting chamber for garments,
bedding, &c., in cases of contagious disease. The
stuff will be put through the superheated steam
process, emerging clean and fit for use again.

“L.C.”: In Brisbane, some years ago,
there was a great scandal about the alleged pre-
ference shown by a Judge towards his son, who
practised before him as a barrister. Referring to
the matter, I heard, at a dinner party, an old
lawyer relate the following concerning a similar
favoritism exhibited by Baron Maguire, once of
the Irish Bench, for his son. An application was
made for a new trial and refused, whereupon the
barrister who failed to obtain it returned the brief
with the recommendation that Mr. Maguire be
I retained. This was done, and on his making an
application a new trial was promptly granted.
The first barrister then pointed out in court that
a similar application by himself had been refused.
“Oh,” said the Baron, “Mr. Maguire has put
the matter in quite a different light.” “ Yes,”
was the scathing retort —“in the light of the
son ! ”

A woman’s view :
Why does the female perjurer go scatheless ? Not be-
cause her sex sympathises with the woman whose indecent
accusations may descend like a cloud upon husband,
father, or lover ; nor because the much-vaunted chivalry
of man or privilege of woman intervenes. These abstrac-
tions avail not the female who feels suddenly shabby, and
removes the 2s. lid. blouse from the draper’s shop-front,
or the woman who abducts a leg of mutton because the
children have nothing for their Sunday dinner. These
erring ones get two months, or thereabouts ; whilst Sap-
phira, of the loathly imagination, sails out of court scath-
less, sniffing up the savour of the desired odour of noto-
riety. If (though no other woman will countenance the
“ if ”) she is a victim to hysteria, why is she not detained
for medical treatment —good, strong, forcible, uncompro-
mising medical treatment ?
A noble story was related by a Sydney
domestic servant the other day. She was sitting
in her master’s house, all alone, at night, when it
was furiously attacked by a gang of burglars.
She didn’t say whether they had artillery, but a
terrible struggle followed, out of which the young
female person, who had defended herself despe-
rately at the point of the frying-pan or something,
emerged triumphant. The daily papers published
the narrative, and “the housemaid hero ” was a
passing sensation. None of these papers has
since even whispered the fact that inquiry proved
there were no burglars, no awful struggle, no
heroic defence. It was a case of imaginative
hysteria evolved while sitting by the fireplace.
Too wide publicity cannot be given to these
hysteria incidents until it is set down as an
absolute principle of jurisprudence that a woman’s
evidence on any exciting subject is to betaken
with extreme caution, if it is taken at all.

A romance of the racecourse. A whim-
sical individual, who struck a big div. at a M.L.
meet, heard a voice at his elbow as he was stow-
ing away his notes. Turning, he faced—his
divorced wife. Eyeing her for a minute, he said,
“ Well, you can share it, old girl.” And she did.
They have since re-married. ’Tis gold, after all,
that makes the world go round.
An aged wayback, to whom modern city
arrangements were a mystery, recently had an
awful experience in a Sydney hotel. When he
saw the string hanging down in the bathroom,
he recklessly pulled it with insufficient con-
sideration of the consequences. Instantly
there was a long, wailing splutter, and then a
violent howl like an escaping cataract, and a
torrent of water. Two housemaids saw a scared
and insufficiently-attired bush-gentleman rush-
ing towards them yelling “fire” and “mur-
der.” After making some incoherent remarks
about taking to the boats, he swooned. In
another Sydney hotel a country friend once
“pulled the string,” and was so certain that he
had wrecked the water-supply that he packed up
his bag and ran for his life lest he should be
ruined by the bill for damages. The shower-
bath should be furnished with a legend : “ Please
pull the string, and don’t be alarmed.” These
few simple words would save many a country
visitor an anxious time.
The boss Benevolent Society of an Aus-
tralian city is wrestling with a problem. On its
books were two families—one a widow with four
children, and the other a deserted wife with five.
Recently, the deserted wife reported that her hus-
band had returned. Investigation proved that he
had not only returned, but also that he was living
with the widow and four children. The society
promptly stopped further assistance to the latter.
The old man was so shocked at his wife for inform-
ing on him that he went to the old home, collected
his own five children, and took them also to live
with him and the widow. This relieved the
society of ten pensioners, and the society is
pleased on the whole. But the deserted wife is
clamoring at its door, demanding assistance to
prosecute her husband for desertion and to recover
her children ; and the society feels that it should
move in the matter on high moral grounds, yet, if
it does move, the man will probably be sent to
gaol, and it will have the widow and the nine
children on its hands again. So the institution
almost wishes it wasn’t so moral and could let the
thing slide.

AUDITORS’ CERTIFICATE AND REPORT.
In accordance with the provisions of “ The Companies Act 1900,” we certify that all our requirements as auditors
have been complied with.
We report that we have verified the cash, investments, bills of exchange, and other assets in London, and have
had access to the accounts sent to London from the various branches in the colonies, and having examined the same,
and the books and accounts of the London office, we are of opinion that the above balance-sheet is a full and fair
balance-sheet, properly drawn up so as to exhibit a true and correct view of the state of the company’s affairs as
shown by the books of the company.
WHINNEY, SMITH, and WHINNEY,
Chartered Accountants,

That rash young man, Kirton, M.L. A. for Bal'
larat (Vic.), got up the other day at the Methodist
Conference and recklessly struck its principal
religious stock-in-trade in the eye. The Confer-
ence had just been of opinion that the non-success
of the movement for restoring sectarian education
was greatly owing to the machinations of the
R.C.’s, and to the R.C. block vote. Kirton
thereupon remarked in a dhsual way that as the
Catholics numbered less than a quarter of the
Victorian population, it was absurd to talk as if
they ran the whole show ; that there was no R.C.
block vote except on rare occasions, the Holy
Romans being just as little unanimous in their
politics as other sects ; and that the habit among
incompetent Protestant parsons of deciding that all
their failures were due, in some hazy fashion, to
the R.C. Church made any sensible man
tired. Then Kirton made some disparaging
remarks about Orange societies, but the roar of
parsonical disapproval drowned his voice just
then, and the Methodist Conference raged like a
troubled sea, and cast up many dismasted
adjectives ..upon the shore. That Kirton spoke
the simple-truth is beyond all question. The
State where the largest proportion of the popula-
tion is of the R.C. persuasion is New South
Wales, and the R,C. vote in N.S.W. wasn’t
strong enough to get its Cardinal even tenth place
at the Federal Convention election. The Protes-
tant churches make very little progress with their
Bible-in-schools propaganda, but they would
probably make no more progress if there wasn’t a
single R.C. in Australia. The trouble is that they
are calling in“o the deaf ear of a flock which
doesn’t take its own church seriously and
which is rush in 'to back a horse. And the R.C.
church is bawling in the same deaf ear. The myth
of the Protestant parsons that they are a great
power which would prevail, but for the mighty
opposition of the shaven and celibate priesthood
of Rome, is comforting but thin. Both parties
are opposing one another certainly, and shoving
all they know, but it is very like a case of two
grasshoppers shoving against each other in a dark
corner while the bulk of the community doesn’t
know that they are there.

On Friday afternoon last Mr. Edmund
Resch, Consul for the Netherlands, held a recep-
tion at the Hotel Australia, and the following
gentlemen returned the official call made by
Mr. Resch some time previously— viz., Acting
Lieutenant-Governor, the State Premier (Mr.
See), the Mayor, Town Clerk, and the undermen-
tioned consular representatives : —M. Georges
Biard d’Aunet, Consul-General for France ; Herr
Paul von Bnri, Imperial German Consul-General;
Mr. Hisakichi Eitaki, acting Consul-General for
Japan ; Esper Nassour Bey, Consul for Turkey ;
Mr. Theodor A. Boesen, K.D., Consul-General for
Denmark ; M. Rene Vos, Vice-Consul for Bel-
gium ; Mr. E. M. Paul, Consul for Russia ; Mr.
Marc Rutty, Consul for Switzerland ; Mr. J.
Brown, Consul for Chili ; and Mr. Jas. T. Tillock,
Consul for Argentine Republic.
A SCIENTIFIC PICK-ME-UP.-No better
tonic and restorative for the victims of social fes-
tivities exists than a morning draught of Hunyadi
Janos. Sure, swift, searching, yet safe, and in
every respect salutary. The best and safest
natural aperient. *
Daring the recent Queensland electoral
campaign, a candidate stated that he was not
averse to companies building private railways,
but what he did not want was to have syndicates
building them. He probably thought the syndi-
cate was a kind of kangaroo.
Miner “Jack” Goggin, the well-known
Wyalong athlete, writes stating that the local
resolution condemning Holman, M.L. A., for
his attitude on the Boer war was rushed
through by the chairman, Mayor Neeld, who
prevented any discussion on the motion con-
demning Mr. Holman, after inviting discussion
on the previous patriotic motions, about which
all were agreed :
The motion was put while the audience was standing
up or rushing to the platform and demanding that a
division be taken, and if the motion had been put so that
it could be voted on by all who wanted to vote on it, or
if the division had been taken, it was evident to every-
body (including the chairman) that the motion would
have been badly beaten. The reports in the Sydney
papers have misrepresented the public of Wyalong
throughout Australia ; they have injured Mr. Holman by
holding him up to be a man condemned by his con-
stituents, when he is not so condemned. Any excuse is
good enough for attacking a Labor member, and every-
body knows that the so-called patriotic meeting was
arranged by the men who led the opposition to Mr.
Holman at the last general election, but this was not
published in the Sydney press, although it was called
attention to often enough at the meeting.

Herr Rassmussen’s Australian Career:
It is about Twenty Years since Herr
Rassmussen introduced his Herbal Remedies
to the Australian Public. At that time
he was only known to the public as a Botanist
pure and simple, having devoted almost his
whole life to the study of that most useful
and interesting braneh of science. It how-
ever, soon dawned on Herr Rassmussen that
immense virtues existed in many plants which
were entirely unknown to medical men, and
that it was his duty towards his fellow man
to bring these new discovered Herbal Remedies
within the means of suffering humanity.
Herr Rassmussen at once set to work,
and his entire energy was directed towards
extracting the medical properties from all
kinds of plants, and putting them up in the
most suitable and practical form, according
to the nature of the complaints which they
had been found a cure for.
Pills and Powders were found to be the
most practical form to administer these new
Herbal Remedies.
It is unnecessary to particularise here the
immense success Herb Rassmussen met with
in his venture twenty years ago ; suffice it to
say that in less than twelve months the
great and wonderful cures brought about by
his Alfaline Herbal Remedies were known
throughout Australia, New Zealand, and many
other parts of the world. Testimonials and
Honors arrived from all parts. Gold Medals
from Paris and other places were awarded, and
Herr Rassmussen’s name has been a house-
hold word ever since.— (Sydney Press.)

He was sick of life (in other words he was
very drunk) so he laid his head on the tram-rail
and waited for the vehicle to end his woes. Ry-
and-bye he got tired of lying there, and found the
tram-rail very cold on his ear; besides he had
become a little more sober. So he sat up and
looked at his watch. It was 2 a.m. and the next
tram wasn’t due for four hours.

“ Alba * on a legitimate grievance :
I was talking to an official in a Sydney post office
recently when he asked if I could give him any facts con-
cermng Perkin Warbeck, orZerah, King of the Ethiopians.
He explained bleakly that he was preparing for a Public
Service exam., and Perkin and Zerah seemed to be the
two stillest subjects. Just then a worried female rushed
to the counter and asked a question as to certain Govern-
ment Savings Bank forms. The official regarded her
blankly and said that he didn’t know. The woman almost
wept. Her brother was dying and wished to split up his
bank deposit among certain of his relatives, and didn’t
know how. Couldn’t the official find out for her ? She
remained at. the counter some time, pleading sorrowfully
for information, but in vain. But she has the consolation
perhaps, of knowing that there will be shortly complete
information available as to Perkin Warbeck, and Zerah
King of the Ethiopians, and, possibly, even some facts
about Hoshea, King of Israel.
A quaint story of a hasty marriage was
related recently in a Maoriland court. A red-
necked, commonplace individual confided to a
talkative waiter that he would like to get married,
and offered him £2 if he would procure a lady who
was languishing for a helpmate. The waiter
agreed, and later the applicant was introduced to a
joyous child of nature with hair the golden color
of a summer cornfield. After a two hours
acquaintance the lady consented to make life
endurable for him, and with a new-born joy in his
heart he set about making preparations. His ex-
perience of wedded life was not very exhilarating.
The very day of his marriage he refused to pay
his friend the waiter on the ground that the
wife was not up to standard. The waiter naturally
sued for his £2. Also within three weeks the
happy home was broken up and the lady sued for
maintenance. Husband’s complaint was that she
was always under the influence of gin, and smoked
a packet of bad cigarettes a day. Cruelty was
alleged as a set-off, and the beak made an order
for 10s. a week. After all, the ways of some men
are more inscrutable than those of women, and
these latter—especially the way a woman contrives
to button her dress up the back—no one can hope
to understand.
The luck of drunks and children : At
Rushworth (Vic.) the driver of the Elmore coach
left his empty vehicle in front of a shop for a few
minutes. Six children, aged from 9 years to 15
months, got in, intending to have a ride. The
horses bolted and smashed into a fence. Five
youngsters were thrown, along with the hood of
the trap, over the fence into a garden ; the sixth
was pitched on to the footpath. The coach was
broken to pieces, and yet these six children only
panned out for four scratches among them.
Mr. John de Pass, a fine, handsome, genial
Melbourne merchant of the 70’s (and before), wa»
a member of the Victorian Yeomanry corps (all
paid their own exes.). At a dinner given by the
corps, in responding to a toast, Mr. Lie Pass said
that .he believed he was the first Jewish sol-
dier since Josephus. Which recalls a fact. An
influential Hebrew organisation in London, 20
years ago, asked the Commander-in-Chief to per-
mit all Jewish soldiers in the British Army an
opportunity of observing the Passover and other
religious ceremonies. The “Chief” granted the
request, and notice was sent to the colonel of
every British regiment asking him to make a return
le Jewish soldiers under his command.
When the returns reached the War Office it was
found that in the entire British Army there was
just one Jew. All the same, De Pass was miles
out in his history. Jewry put a score of good
armies in the field after the days of Josephus,
and the noses blotted out the sun when they
battered the declining Roman empire at Tyre
and fought against Mahomet at Koraidba. And
beside when a member of the rich, ornamental
Yeomanry called himself a'soldier he was taking
himself too seriously.

CHE APPRECIATION of the AN by those
who have had an intimate association with it in
the home, centres attention on that which
constitutes the chief value of the AHQLJAN
—the inexhaustible source of pleasure it pro=
vides in the household .
It is a solo orchestra, always ready for any occasion, and the playing of it requires no
musical skill. Overtures, symphonies, and entire operas can be played by the brain-
tired business man, his wife or. his daughter. The experiences all the
pleasure of hand playing, because he controls the expression, which is the soul of
music. If you wish accompaniments for dancing or singing, or if you wish to hear the
latest popular air you need only turn to your .dEolian.
The majority of those wh@ hear the for the first time express
surprise, saying they have been unable to comprehend until then what
it was like. Many of our patrons, after using the .dEolian, for six
months, go ©ut of their way to tell us of the pleasure they have derived
from their purchases, evincing a degree of enthusiasm which leaves no
room for doubt as to their complete conversion.
This enthusiasm is the
rule, not the exception.
Why is it ?

gests that the iEolian
is worthy of your atten-
tion, and that every day
you delay you» investi-
gation ofthe instrument

self off from a source
of pleasure about which
you might become
equally enthusiastic.

Did She?
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The name of the place doesn’t matter. People
who’ve been there don’t want to go back ; people
who haven’t have lost nothing.
At all events, the black "fever was there
and the yellow flag—and as much excite-
ment as the latitude allowed ' of. And the
solitary Briton at the sole hotel had asked
himself, many times, why the several things !
he didn’t go away before it struck him
that he couldn't. Few ships called, and no ship
would take him. So, being a globe-dawdler—not
trotter—and fearing the fever little, Ormiston
sketched and scribbled, and grumbled, and
waited.
But, one evening, when the treacherous miasm
was sneaking out to work more mischief than the
bullying sun, there came to him a message from a
doctor whom he knew. Would he, as the only
other Englander in the place, come to the hos-
pital to see a countryman, left fever-stricken
by a Swedish ship, and—thought the doctor
moribund, or near it.
Ormiston —not an “Englander”—didnt like
being called one ; but he went.
• • * •
Entering the corridor, he met her —the woman
whose shudder-breeding disfigurement had set
him, like others, guessing at the story she would
never tell. For she spoke no more to any than
her nurse’s duty asked for—a woman with the key
of her mind turned on the inside —night-haired
and olive-skinned, and with features that would
have given her, as nature left them, large claims
to beauty. But, round each eye—in deep blue
pigment—ran a line that rudely traced the bony
margin of its orbit; on each side the face another
line was deftly made to indicate the outline of a
fleshless cheek; the living mouth’s significance
was lost in the boldly-marked presentment of two
lipless ranks of gumless teeth that completed the
suggestion—grotesquely ghastly—of a skull —the
Head of Death upon a breathing body !
She took him to the bed, and said, in English
fragmentary but distinct:
“He sleep! Please, sit! He better when he
wake. Yes —smoke ! ” —as the visitor inquiringly
held up his cigar-case. “ But, not give he ! He
ask, all time, tobac-co ! to-bac-co ! ”
So, in the shaded lamp-light, Ormiston waited,
smoking, and thinking more of this mysterious
woman than of the man he had come to see, till
from the bed a voice, with a cyclonic flavor even
in its weakness, said suddenly :
“ Stitch me in a hammock ! —but that is a good
one! ”
A bullet-head had turned upon the pillow—the
eyes of it fixed on the cigar; a blunt nose sniffed
the smoke. “Oh, Lord!” commented the sub-
jacent mouth, with such a fervor of appreciation
that the smoker hastened to say :
“I’d like to give you one, my poor chap !—but,
you know ”
“ Oh, yes !—the reg’lations ! Sink the jabbering
Dutch lubbers !—l’d get better sixteen-knots-an-
hour if I’d a pipe in my mouth half-a-dozen times
a day! ”
“Get better?” the other echoed, doubtfully;
“ I hope so ! But, you’re—you’re very bad, you
know ! ”
The sick man jerked up a mighty pair of
shoulders from the bed. “See that?” he asked,
defiantly, as he flopped helplessly down again
“ ’Taint the fust of this here rotten fever I’ve
seen, mister —by a long way ! —and I never saw a
man go dead that could get half-a-foot of day-
light under him like that. And, anyhow, you’re
a what’s-his-name’s sort of a comforter — you
are ! ”
“ All right, my lad; I’m glad you keep your
heart up. But, don’t try any more gymnastics,
just yet, or, I shall have to call the nurse.”
“ Her ! The skull-faced one, is it ? Oh,
blazes ! ” and the patient shook with a feeble
laughter which jarred so fantastically upon his
sorrowful surroundings that the visitor got hastily
up. But the other stretched a huge brown arm—
as hairy as an ape’s.
“ Sit down, 3ir !—sit down ! Not off-my-head,
I ain’t!—if that’s what you’re thinkin’! You’re
the Englishman they told me about, of course!
’Twas kind of you to come, too. ’Scuse me for
not sayin’ so before. But, you see—about this
here death’s-head Spaniard ! Didn’t know she
was a Span, did you ? ”
Curiosity and the visitor sat down together.
This man wasn’t wandering—any more than he
was going to die—if Ormiston was any judge.
“ No ! ” he said. “ Nobody here knows any-
thing about her.”
“There you’re out, mister! Vm here—worse
luck!—and I know all about her. And she’d
know me, too, fast enough—if it wasn’t for this
here ‘up-endage,’ as they calls it.” His rope-
seared fingers gripped his bush of grizzling beard.
Then he beckoned, and the other stooped his ear.
“ A’mighty’s sake, sir !—let me have a puff on the
quiet! No —by gum ! —here she comes —and the
sawbones along of her ! ”
The young Dutch doctor felt the pulse, and
spoke to Ormiston in French.
“He’ll do—this fellow—after all. According
to all precedent, he ought to have died, but, like
the rest of you Englanders, he’s an obstinate kind
of animal! ” He ran an appreciative professional
finger over the herculean chest and shoulders —
said a word or two to the nurse, and passed on.
The woman poured out medicine and would
have raised the storm-battered head, but the
sailor hove it up himself, and watched her keenly
while he drank. And the visitor, watching also,
saw no flicker of recognition in the hideously-
perverted face.
“However well you know her,” he said when
she had gone, “ she doesn’t know you ! I suppose
we may venture now ?’’—handing a cigar, and
offering his own to light it,
The brawny giant veiled himself with vapour
before he answered—
“ Dunno but it’s as well she don’t! S’pose
she’d hardly go for to stick a knife in my gizzard,
hereaway ; but, by gum ! she’d have a rare good
slant to pizen me ! Holes in me I’ve had a good
few, in my time, but I’ve never been pizened yet
—outside a Scotch ship’s rations ! ”
Ormiston looked at his watch. “ I’ll come again
to-morrow,” he said. “You’ll be stronger then,
and your yarn will keep.”
A graver mood came over the other like a fore-
falling shadow. “My yarn will keep,” he said,
slowly—“but—p’r’aps I won’t! And I’d take it
kind if you’d keep your anchor down for an hour
or so. It’s no use heavin’ good cuss-English at
these here yee-yawin’ Dutchies ! ”
Ormiston sat down again. “ That nurse speaks
English, in a way,” he said.
“Good reason why! Her husband was an
Englishman. That’s what my yarn’s a-hangin’
to ! Light up again yourself, and I’ll heave ahead
with it. Won’t take long ; it’s short, if it ain’t
sweet!
“’Twas over in Amboyna, about a dozen years
ago, that I dropped across a mate I , hadn’t
seen for another dozen before that. Bo’s’n of a
Blackwall liner, Billy Hawkins was, when I was a
quartermaster in her, and great chums we was, as
well as townies. Only—Billy havin’ a lot more
eddication than me—l knew he was bound to
stump the poop, sooner or later; so, when I falls
in with him again, it didn’t altogether lay me flat-
aback to find him ‘ Cap’en Hawkins, at your ser-
vice ! ’ Not much of a ship, she wasn’t —a 600-ton
‘country-wallah,’ as they calls ’em hereaway —
Moulmein-built, and manned with little yaller
rats of Java-men ; but, for all that, I tell you old
Billy thought big brass buttons of himself —and
wore ’em, too —and had took a wife on the
strength of ’em likewise. A Luzon-woman she
was—Philippines, you know —Spanish, with a
dash of native, and as handsome as—as a angel.
But the devil was a lot more in her line. And
this is her.”
“The nurse?"
“You bet! And—poor swivel-eyed old Billy !
with the end of his nose like a mandarin-orange
what in thunder she made fast to him for lays me
clean out —’less ’twas for the name of bein’ a
cap’n’s missis. But, by the time she got down to
Amboyna, she’d hove all that overboard. Billy
was like dirt under her feet; ’cause, you see, the
poor old swab was that gone on her that he
couldn’t nohow hoist in the notion that she hated
him already. But she did—and scorned him
worse than a buck-nigger, too —as I told him one
day when I’d gone aboard to see him and she’d
slung a glass o’ wine over him. ‘Take that
aboard, old ship,’ says I, ‘and she’ll spit in your
face, next tack 1 Forbearance with a woman’s all
shipshape enough,’ I says, ‘ up to a certing point.
Pass that —and they only despises of you !’ At
that he begins to growl, something like his old
self—way down in his stummick. ‘ And she
despises of me, you think ?’ he says. ‘ Dead sure,
Billy, she do ! And that’s total wreck ! A feller
has always some chance with a woman that only
hates him !’
“Well, he says never a word for five minutes —
haulin’ in the slack of it, like —and then it _ hits
him sudden, like a brick ! ‘You’re gospel-right,
chummy !’ says he— ‘ I may haul down that flag as
soon as I like ! What’ll Ido with her ? —cut her
adrift ?’ ‘ Please yourself, Billy,’ says I; * but I’d
take some of the blasted pride out of her, fust, if
I was you.’ And I tells him of an old Spanish
buccaneerin’ dodge with women that carried too
much weather-helm. ‘ You don’t want nothin’, I
says, ‘ but common tattooin’-gear, and a stiff dose
of laudanum to keep her quiet, and a sketch I’ll
tell you how to make.’ ”
The listener sat back, with widened eyes, and
pointed along the shadowy corridor. “Good
God !” he said. “A sketch of that ?”
The other sucked back life to his cigar-stump
before he answered:
“That?- On the woman’s face ? Yes! And a
fust-class job Billy made of it!— ‘ exact unto plans
and specifications,’ as the shipbuilders say in
their contracks.”
But the twinkle in the cheerful ruffian’s eye
found no response. “ Did he tell you ?■” Ormiston
asked, staring at the floor.
“ Tell me ? Noll never clapped eyes on him
after he sailed ; nor on her, neither—till now.”
Ormiston still kept his eyes away. Coherent as
all this sounded, he thought it might just pos-
sibly be raving—a web of weird imaginings,
woven round that horror-striking countenance.
“ Then you cannot know this thing was done ? ”
he said.
The man of original suggestion stared at him
contemptuously.
“ Know ?” he said. “ Can’t I see ? You don’t
suppose she did it herself, do you? You don’t
suppose—oh ! be-lay ! ”
Ormiston looked quickly up. Between them
stood the nurse !—from what shadowy corner
stolen out, he knew not.
“How much has she heard?—how much com-
prehended?” was the thought in both men’s
minds; but the woman gave no sign, and the
seaman’s coarse audacity recovered.
‘ ‘ Time to go, mister, I’m afraid ! Here’s my
beauty come to tell you so ! ”
“ Yes ; time go ! ” the nurse said in her strange,
staccato English. “ Time he sleep. Again—you
come ! ”
There was dull monotony in her voice, as she
pointed towards the bed, but into her apathetic
eye, as she faced the visitor, there sprang a gleam
so swift and evanescent that he was hardly sure it
had been there.
“Good-night! ”he said. “ I’ll come again to-
morrow.”
“ To-morrow —yes ! —to-morrow ! ” the woman
echoed, as she left him at the door. “To-
morrow ! ” she repeated, as she walked back along
the corridor—and then her fellow-nurses heard
what they had never heard before —the Death-
Woman laughing!
* * ♦ *
When Ormiston came back, next evening, the
sailor was dead ! — and buried !
“ A bit of a surprise to us ! ” the tow-headed
young doctor admitted. “The fellow wasn’t so
obstinate, after all! He consented, you see, to
die secundum ordinem ! ”
“Your joke does you little credit, sir ! What
was it—a relapse ? ”
“Well ’.—hum !—hardly that! I’m rather in-
clined to think it was heart-mischief ! ”
“Inclined to think, my good sir ! Don’t you
know ? Was there no autopsy ? ”
The Dutchman looked everywhere but at his
questioner. “No !” he said. “ We’re not over-par-
ticular here in that way at any time, and, just now,
we’ve less leisure than ever for formalities. And,
why these inquiries, mynheer? What is it that
you think ? ”
But Ormiston didn’t know exactly what to
think.
And he doesn’t know yet!
N.s.w.
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
The eternal marriage question has been
giving some trouble at Florida, Solomon Islands.
The wife-buying custom obtains in that obscure
locality—or rather, money presents are made to
the bride’s relations, which is the same thing.
Latterly, however, the price of eligible females
has gone up so high that a poor nigger can’t afford
the luxury. One native teacher sold his daughter
to another pious individual for 150 strings of shell
money and 1000 porpoise teeth. This came to the
ears of the Bishop of Melanesia, who sacked the
teacher for extortion and for unduly forcing up the
price of one of the necessaries of life—girl. It
appears that the price was twice that asked for a
chief’s plump and well-favored- daughter in the
old days. The whole matter was gone into while
the Bishop was there, and the native legislature
fixed on 30 strings as the minimum price for a girl
among common people and 50 strings in the case
of the elite.

The Three Little Children.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Just three little children, laughing away in the
sun ;
The oldest one of them toddling and hardly able
to run.
Chasing the flame-colored beetles, sighing to hold
the bright things
Drowsily whirling above them, glitter of gold on
their wings.
Till the ’possum leaped from the gum-tree all in the
midst of their play—
Fleeing from three little children that followed
and lost their way.
Away from the grass-sown paddocks—away from
the home in view;
Far into the grim bush shadows, and out of the
world they knew !
How late were the children playing ! “ Coo-ee
them in to their tea —
They must be there by the wattles, or aswing in
the grey gum tree.”
But they came not back for our calling, and slow
o’er the broken plain
The black witch, Night, came slouching, in a
cloak of drifting rain.
“Muster the hands of the stations, and out with
the rising sun,
For the babes of the free selector, lost on the
Skeleton run ! ”
But the rain had blurred the traces, for the Night
was leagued with hell,
And the Bush was safe and secret, and she kept
her counsel well.
God! Why did the rain fall faster? Why kept
not the wind at bay,
For the sake of three little children that had
wandered and lost their way ?
And why did the morning's sun beat like flame on
the steaming sands ?
To burn on the rain-wet faces, and the small, weak,
blistered hands ?
We watched as the fifth dawn lifted the lids of
her slow grey eyes,
And saw a vulture sailing far out o’er a stony
rise—
Just a black blot on the sky-line, a-wheel on a
circling track:
As we raised a shout to the children, the scream
of the hawk came back.
“ ’Tis but a death-bird gloating o’er a lamb lost
out on the plain ” ;
But rousing the sleeping trackers, we took up the
quest again.
We came on a white sun-bonnet caught fast on a
bramble edge
Where a vulture rose from the bushes, as we
climbed the stony ledge;
And, there —with their wee, cold faces pressed
close to the#and, they lay ;
But the souls of three little children had long ago
passed away.

LOLA.
Our New Selection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We were encountering reverses. No rain for
months; nothing but heat, heat, and scorching
gales. Then it rained when we least required it—
in the middle of the hay-making—and poured for
weeks; poured till the cut-off thirty acres of oats,
the only crop in the district, rotted and was
ruined.
Two successive seasons the wheat failed—once
when it had grown higher than the fence a late
frost blackened and withered it all up in one
night; and once it didn’t grow at all.
“Don’t know,” said Dad, gloomily—“don’t
know at all ! ” Then, after reflecting :
“ Jimmy Tyson hisself couldn’t stand much o’
these seasons. Most uncertent. A season or two
more, ’n a man might lose all lie’s ever earnt an’
not have a bloomin’ stick.”
Dad’s reflections only made him unhappy. He
wasn’t so brave as he used to be, and the loss of a
few pounds worried him and gave him nightmares.
Dad was fond of money, now. He thought more
of it than he did of Mother.
Dad complained of his prospects to old Martin
M'Evoy, of the Twelve Mile. Martin believed in
butter. He milked a few old cows and once or
twice a week trotted his cart to the railway
station, and the inhabitants of Saddletop would
stand at their doors and stare and grin at him
whenever he passed along the road. Martin and
his butter were a source of amusement to them.
They regarded it as undignified to fake round a
lot of old milkers and drag fluid out of them.
They were farmers.
“i car At see wot you've t’ growl about,” M'Evoy
said, casting an eye over our lucerne paddocks,
and at the cattle camped near the gate. “ If I hed
them paddocks and them cows I’d mek a thousan’
a year.”
Dad grunted his incredulity.
“ I wud—no damn mistake.” (A pause.) “ Out
o’ ten I mek four nuts a week now,” and Martin
looked defiant.
“ Out o’ butter ?” Dad stared.
“Yes, butter,” Martin shouted—“a damsite
better ’n wheat." Then he jumped from his cart
and dragged some papers from his greasy trousers-
Iiockot. He showed Dad an invoice and a cheque.
)a 1 didn’t take his eyes off the cheque till Mar-
tin returned it to his pocket with a triumphant
snort; then he looked at the lucerne-paddocks
and the cows, and thought. Martin drove on.
• * » •
For several days Dad was inactive. He spent
his time in a chair on the verandah. Dave and
Joe missed him in the paddock. They wished he
would stay on the verandah all his life. But Dad
was only working out a problem.
D id left the verandah suddenly one afternoon
and went among the cattle. A fine-looking lot
they were —sleek and fat; and beyond an odd one
killed for our own use, which mostly went bad in
the cask and was thrown out, they were only a
nuisance on the place and devoured more hay at
IJad s hands than they were worth. To Dad,
though, they were priceless. He was never done
admiring them. They were his pictures—his oil-
paintings—his art-gallery.
cows .... twenty-six pounds a
week, Dad muttered and returned to tne house,
and sat till tea. Dad was cheerful and questioned
Dave about the ploughing; then he broke new
ground and spoke enthusiastically of dairying.
He went into figures and said the cow-yard was
to be put in working order the next day. Dave
was silent. Sarah was inclined to debate the
matter, but Dad silenced her. “ I’ve thought it
all out,” he said, waving his hand, “and know
just what I’m about.”
* • * *
Breaking day. A hard, biting frost that
whitened everything and crunched and cracked
beneath you when you walked, was over the land.
OoJd ! Charity was nothing to it. The horses
stood shivering at the big gate, waiting for their
hay; cockatoos screamed in the trees up the
gullv ; Cook’s roosters crowed faintly in the dis-
tance—-ours lustily answered back horse-bells
timcled-tankled on the reserve; the smoke of a
camp-fire curled into the frosty air 5 forms of
horsemen moved quietly about, and a thousand
head of travelling cattle took shape.
a shout or two, and the cows
with Bill close on their heels rushed into the
yard.
Dave and Joe and Cranky Jack came out,
grumbled at the cold, stared at the travelling
cattle moving from the reserve, and started rnilk-
P'cy milked in silence and were nearly
finished when Dad’s angry voice was heard at the
barn. He wanted to know why the devil the
ITH GS WerS not fed ’ aUd shuffled about yelling for
barah brought a can of hot tea and some bread
and butter into the yard. Dad came along swing-
ing his arms.
“They don’t want thet here,” he snapped
take it away ! ” ’
Joe grabbed some of the provender and swilled
a cup of tea. Sarah grinned.
Dad scowled and measured the milk in the tins
with his eye, then entered the yard and inspected
the cows, and stared sternly at the milkmen. Dad
was a watchful overseer.
Dad turned and called boisterously to Sarah as
she returned to the house. Joe, in a cheerful
mood, pointed a cow’s teat over his shoulder and
directed a stream of milk at Dad. Dad danced
round and looked in the air and down at his feet
and wiped his neck with his hand. Then he
growled at a red cow that was facing him and
shaking her head and throwing froth from her
nose.
Joe entered into an argument, across the yard
with Dave, about a pony mare of Doolan’s.
“Get on with th’ milkin’,” Dad said, im-
patiently, “an’ let th’cows out—lots t’be’done
without yarnin,” and he hobbled round some
more, returning to the same spot again, and ex-
hibiting increased impatience.
“ She’s by Badger,” Joe went on.
“ Gerrout,” Dave said.
“Bet y’ quid,” and, as if to book the bet, Joe
stood up, placing his bucket on the ground.
“Dammit,” Dad hollered, “why ’n th’ devil
carn’t y’ get on ? Are y’ goin’t’ be .”
Joe looked at him. The red cow with the
frothy nose had approached Dad closely, and
seemed to mistake him for a heap of hay. She
wasn t a handsome beast anyway you viewed her,
nor a dangerous-looking one, either.”
Look out that red cow don’t charge,” Joe
said (which was the last thing in the world he
would expect the brute to do).
Dad glanced down quickly. He was taken by
surprise. Sudden consciousness of the cow’s close
proximity startled him. Like a rooster hit with
a brick when half-way up his top-note, he cut
short a yell intended for Joe, and lifting his big
right foot aimed a heavy kick at the cow’s head.
His boot, a hard, ill-shaped blucher, grazed her
forehead, and sliding under the animal’s hoop-
horns held fast. The cow swung round, Dad
dropped on his back, and clutched with both
hands at the ground, and waved his left leg
menacingly at the brute. Finding Dad a fixture
she became hysterical. She bellowed and ran back-
wards and put her tongue out in an ugly curl.
“My G-G-God! Help, help!” Dad shouted.
I he other cows rushed round the yard ; one shoved
the rails down, and they all passed out.
Dave and Joe sprang to Dad’s assistance.
JNeither, for the moment, could see a way to extri-
cate him. Cranky Jack jammed a finger into
each of his ears and laughed—and looked
humorous. Dave rushed to the cow’s horns; Joe
to her tail; then vice versa.
Round the yard the cow backed, bellowing more
and more. She trampled over Joe’s bucket and
spilt the milk, and made a track through it with
Joe at last seized Dad’s hands and pulled back
—pulled till he lost footing and fell down. Then
the cow backed right out of the yard, bumping
Dad heavily on the fallen rails. Dad cursed at
every bump. She proceeded backwards towards
the barn, fearah rushed on the scene, wailing’
“Oh, mv gracious!” and frightened the beast
more. Joe recovered himself, and seized Dad’s
hands again. Two small dogs, constant com-
panions of Dad’s, arrived. They barked and bit
the mr near the cow’s head—sometimes near
Dad s—and fell over one another in a struggle for
position, till at last Dave, whogot tired of shoutirg
to them, let go Dad’s leg, which he was struggli g
to release, and kicked one of them into the air.
Mother, in a state of wild alarm, appeared and
added to the uselessness of Dad’s rescuers.
Dad lost his hat; he was covered with dust and
his shirt came out and hung over his head like a
bag. He looked very undignified.
The cow went back against the dog-kennel. A
bulldog was tied there. He crouched down and
waited for her. “ Give us a pull ’er q—quick ”
Dave gasped, and Joe jumped to his side—“ Little
more—now sh—.” Just then the dog fastened his
teeth in the cow’s leg. She roared and plunged
forward and knocked Dave and Joe down and
trampled all over Dad. Then she dishonestly
raced out of the gate with only Dad’s boot under
her horn.
STEELE RUDD.
A Melbourne Chinese fourpenny restaur-
ant employs white girls, and has marble-topped
tables.
N.S.W. Govt.-yacht Victoria (which one
jealous Victorian paper alleges is verminous) must
be wondering in its haughty mind what sort of a
world it has come to. Originally the Victoria
belonged to a millionaire and kept most exclusive
company. Now it is used for the welcome of an
R.C. Bishop, fora Methodist picnic, fora Parlia-
mentary jaunt, for an Andy Kelly Labor League
festivity, in short, for any sort of a festivity the
promoters of which have a “pull.” So far, the
Government rat-catching staff hasn’t had its
annual shivoo on the Victoria, but that will come
in time.
